Foreword

Tamil Nadu Economic Survey was published for the first time in our state last year. In
continuation of that tradition, we have prepared the Survey for this year as well.
Tamil Nadu Economic Survey 2025-26 traces the trajectory of our State’s economy
during the past five years. It comprises of ten chapters covering important facets of
the economy and how they have performed over time. Tamil Nadu’s growth story, as
we all know, is unique. Its economic progress is driven by a century long effort to bring
in social justice. The state focussed and continues to focus on both growth and social
justice, resulting in a robust model for many other states to emulate. This unique model
has resulted in a balanced sectoral growth. In fact, our double digit growth recorded last
year was spearheaded by the secondary sector which our national economy and many
other states aim to achieve.
The Survey not only highlights the stellar performance of the State’s economy, it also
indicates areas of concern that needs to be addressed to reach the desired twin targets
of a trillion-dollar economy and a per capita income on par with developed countries.
Sustaining this scorching pace with social justice is a sure way of escaping the middle
income trap by the next decade.
I commend the contributors for the survey. I thank Madras Institute of Development
Studies (MIDS) and Finance Department, Government of Tamil Nadu for coordinating
this effort on behalf of the State Planning Commission.

I fervently hope that this

document will be an important aid for policy makers, academicians and the executive
in discussing the growth story in an informed way, and ensure the sustained journey
towards our shared dream of making the State of Tamil Nadu great and the people of
our State prosperous.

J.Jeyaranjan
Executive Vice Chairman,
State Planning Commission

EXECUTIVE SUMMARY
ECONOMIC SURVEY OF TAMIL NADU (2025-26)
1.

Post pandemic recovery coincided with major policy shifts reshaping global trade. Since
February 2025, multiple rounds of US tariffs and retaliatory measures heightened trade
uncertainty. The global economy grew at 3.3% in 2025. India’s economy recorded 6.49%
in 2024-25, but increased its growth performance to 7.4% in 2025-26. Despite global
headwinds, Tamil Nadu remained resilient.

2.

With a diversified economic base, robust infrastructure, and progressive policies, Tamil
Nadu has positioned itself as a leader in development and inclusive growth. The State’s
balanced growth model, which combines rapid industrialisation with strong social welfare
and sustainability initiatives, has made it a role model for other states.

3.

Tamil Nadu’s sustained high performance has significantly contributed to India’s overall
economic and developmental progress. Despite accounting for only 4 % of India’s land
area and 6% of its population, the State contributed 9.4% to national GDP in 2024–25. Its
Gross State Domestic Product at current prices reached Rs.31.19 lakh crore in 2024–25,
reflecting a nominal growth rate of 16% and a real growth rate of 11.2%, the highest
among major states.

4.

The State’s sustained economic growth is driven by both the expansion of aggregate
supply and the strengthening of aggregate demand. On the supply side, Tamil Nadu
benefits from significant productive capacity and efficiency advantages. It has developed
strong physical infrastructure, including reliable electricity, extensive road and port
networks, and well-planned industrial corridors, which together enhance industrial
competitiveness. High levels of human capital, reflected in literacy, technical education,
and skill development, ensure a productive workforce capable of supporting both
manufacturing and services.

5.

Tamil Nadu’s diversified industrial base, spanning both traditional and modern sectors,
along with a vibrant MSME ecosystem, has promoted broad-based production growth.
The manufacturing sector recorded a robust real growth rate of 14.74% in 2024–25, more
than three times the all-India average of 4.5 %. Over the four-year period from 2021–22
to 2024–25, Tamil Nadu led the nation with an average manufacturing growth rate of
9.38%. The adoption of advanced technologies, the presence of research institutions,
and the development of IT clusters in Chennai, Coimbatore, and Hosur have further

strengthened productivity and innovation. Combined with sound governance and an
enabling regulatory environment, these factors have enhanced supply responsiveness
and attracted sustained investment. The Economic Survey of India 2025–26 recognises
Tamil Nadu as the foremost contributor to India’s manufacturing employment, accounting
for 15% of the nation’s total factory workforce.
6.

In 2024–25, the primary sector contributed 13.4%.to the State’s Gross State Value Added,
the secondary sector 33.1% and the services sector contributed 53.6%. As observed in
developed economies, a further increase in the share of the secondary sector could
significantly improve employment prospects.

7.

The services sector also registered strong performance, achieving a double-digit real
growth rate of 11.3% in 2024–25. Within the sector, real estate, public administration,
transport, hotels and restaurants, and other miscellaneous services all recorded doubledigit growth, reflecting broad-based expansion.

8.

Tamil Nadu continues to rank among the top Indian states in attracting foreign direct
investment. While India’s overall FDI equity inflows declined from US$46.03 billion in 2022–
23 to US$44.42 billion in 2023–24 amid global uncertainty and tighter financial conditions,
Tamil Nadu defied the trend. The State’s FDI inflows increased steadily from US$2,169
million in 2022–23 to US$2,436 million in 2023–24, and further to US$3,681 million in
2024–25.

9.

On the demand side, the State benefits from a large and diversified consumer base,
rising per capita income, and rapid urbanisation, all of which fuel household consumption,
particularly in urban and semi-urban areas. Strong investment demand, driven by
private investment in manufacturing, automobiles, electronics, and renewable energy,
is supported by a conducive business environment and progressive industrial policies.
Government expenditure on infrastructure, education, health, and social welfare further
reinforces aggregate demand.

10.

Tamil Nadu’s export performance provides an additional stimulus to growth. The State
consistently ranks first in exports of electronics, textiles, and leather products, and
second in engineering exports. Recognised as the ‘Detroit of India’, Tamil Nadu is a major
hub for the automobile industry. Merchandise exports nearly doubled from US$26.15
billion in 2020–21 to US$52.07 billion in 2024–25, highlighting the State’s growing global
competitiveness and industrial diversification.

11.

While demand-side forces generate short-term growth impulses, Tamil Nadu’s long-term
growth potential is anchored in its supply-side strengths, particularly its human capital,
infrastructure, and institutional quality. This structural foundation has enabled the State
to maintain growth momentum even in periods of global uncertainty.

12.

Tamil Nadu’s per capita income growth has consistently outpaced the national average.
In 2024–25, per capita income reached Rs.3.62 lakh, which is 1.77 times higher than the
national average of Rs.2.05 lakh, making Tamil Nadu the third-largest state in terms of
per capita income.

13.

Unlike states where economic activity is concentrated in a single metropolitan hub, Tamil
Nadu’s development is more evenly distributed across multiple urban centres. Cities such
as Coimbatore, Madurai, Tiruppur, Tiruchirappalli, and Salem contribute significantly to
the State’s economy, helping bridge the urban–rural divide. In a notable achievement,
every district in Tamil Nadu is now covered under one or more industrial corridor projects,
making it the only state in India with such extensive industrial integration.

14.

District-wise per capita income data for 2023–24 reveal variations arising from differences
in urbanisation, industrial development, and geographical advantages. Nevertheless, the
per capita income of top eight performing districts exceed the per capita income levels
of top-performing states such as Telangana, Karnataka, and Haryana. Notably, in 32 out
of 38 districts, per capita income was higher than the all-India average.

15.

As a highly industrialised and urbanised economy, Tamil Nadu aims to achieve a onetrillion-dollar economy by 2030. As the State continues to move up the industrial and
technological value chain, it is well positioned to attain both its trillion-dollar economy
goal and developed income status well in advance.

16.

Global inflation moderated gradually to 6.6 % in 2023 and further to 5.8% in 2024. India’s
retail inflation also declined from 5.5 % in 2023–24 to 4.6 % in 2024–25 and further to 1.7
% in 2025–26 up to December 2025. Tamil Nadu similarly experienced a decline in retail
inflation from 5.4 % in 2023–24 to 4.6 % in 2024–25 and further to 2.3 % during the same
period. Over the longer period from 2020–21 to 2024–25, however, both Tamil Nadu and
India recorded an average inflation rate of 5.7 %. In 2024–25, Tamil Nadu ranked tenth
lowest in retail inflation among the 18 major Indian states.

17.

Urban inflation in Tamil Nadu declined from 5.2 % in 2023–24 to 2.5 % in 2025–26, while
rural inflation fell from 5.8 % to 2.1 %. Urban inflation remains the primary driver of
overall inflation in the State. Among major categories, food inflation declined sharply to
–0.003 % in 2025–26, while inflation in clothing and footwear moderated to 1.3 %.

18.

Although inflation is primarily a monetary phenomenon, its impact is felt directly by
households through erosion of purchasing power. The Tamil Nadu government has
worked to mitigate these effects through subsidised supplies of essential commodities
such as cereals, pulses, edible oil, and electricity, along with income-support programmes
including the Kalaignar Mahalir Urimai Thittam.

19.

Agriculture in Tamil Nadu remains heavily dependent on the monsoons and contributes
Rs.1.9 lakh crore, accounting for 6.6 % of the State’s GSVA, making it the fifth-largest
sector. The gross cropped area increased from 43.31 % of the State’s geographical area
in 2016–17 to 47.58 % in 2023–24. In 2024–25, the gross cropped area stood at 62.25 lakh
hectares, with a crop intensity of 1.29.

20.

Tamil Nadu achieved notable success in agricultural productivity during 2024–25, ranking
first in ragi and sugarcane yields, second in oilseeds, and third in groundnut yields. The
State’s success in banana cultivation has also been cited by the Economic Survey of India
as a region-specific horticultural achievement.

21.

Agricultural credit has played a vital role in supporting farmers in Tamil Nadu. Credit
disbursal more than doubled from Rs.2.23 lakh crore in 2019–20 to Rs.4.52 lakh crore in
2023–24, registering a compound annual growth rate of around 19 %. During 2023–24,
nearly 299 lakh farmer loan accounts were served, of which 78 % belonged to small and
marginal farmers.

22.

The Gross State Value Added from the livestock sector stood at Rs.1.47 lakh crore in 2024–
25, contributing 5.1 % to the State’s GSVA. Egg production increased significantly from
39,249 lakh in 2001–02 to 2.2 lakh million in 2023–24, growing at an annual compound rate
of 8.2 %. Fishing and aquaculture contributed 0.99 % to GSVA, with fish product exports
of 1.34 lakh tonnes generating Rs.6,854 crore in foreign exchange during 2023–24.

23.

Accelerated growth in agriculture and allied sectors is achievable if the State effectively
addresses challenges such as groundwater depletion and climate change. The adoption of
climate-smart practices, promotion of mechanisation, and greater focus on post-harvest
value addition can significantly enhance farm incomes.

24.

Tamil Nadu ranked second in the Export Preparedness Index and in the credit–deposit
ratio at 120.5 %, and third in total exports at US$52.07 billion in 2024–25.

25.

The State’s infrastructure policy reflects a shift from isolated asset creation towards
systems-based development. Rising capital expenditure has strengthened transport,
logistics, energy, water, and urban and rural services. Investments in public transport,
electric mobility, metro expansion, and digital integration demonstrate a people-centric
mobility vision, while ports, airports, and logistics parks reinforce Tamil Nadu’s role as a
national gateway for trade. At the same time, execution challenges related to coordination,
land acquisition, regulatory clearances, and capacity building remain binding constraints.

26.

Employment generation remains central to economic growth and social stability. Tamil
Nadu’s labour force participation rate increased from 71.5 % in 2021–22 to 72.1 % in 2023–
24, while India’s LFPR rose from 66.2 % to 71.3 % over the same period. The State’s
unemployment rate declined from 5.1 % to 3.5 %, compared to a decline from 4 % to 3.2
% at the national level. Higher enrolment in higher education and skill development, along
with a strong social security system, likely contributed to these outcomes.

27.

Labour market outcomes in Tamil Nadu reflect decades of investment in education,
welfare, and economic diversification. Post-pandemic indicators show rising labour force
participation, higher worker-population ratios, declining unemployment, and a notable
expansion of regular salaried employment, placing the State among national leaders in job
quality. The transition out of agriculture has been relatively orderly, with manufacturing
and services absorbing labour in both urban and rural areas. However, progress remains
uneven, with persistent gender gaps, particularly among youth. Addressing care burdens,
safety, mobility, and workplace retention will be critical in the next phase, along with
strengthening pathways from education to stable employment.

28.

A unifying theme across this survey is that Tamil Nadu’s growth has been driven not by a
retreat from social policy, but by its elevation to the status of economic infrastructure. By
treating education, health, food security, mobility, and dignity as enforceable social rights
rather than residual welfare, the State has transformed historically excluded groups
into active participants in modern growth. This has produced a distinctive development
model in which equity and efficiency reinforce each other.

29.

The Tamil Nadu government has consistently increased its expenditure on the social sector,
rising from Rs.1,13,268 crore in 2021–22 to Rs.1,57,864 crore in 2025–26 (RE). Over time,
the State has built one of India’s most extensive and innovative welfare architectures.
Programmes such as Naan Mudhalvan, Pudhumai Penn, Tamizh Pudhalvan, the Chief
Minister’s Breakfast Scheme, Kalaignar Magalir Urimai Thittam, and large-scale housing
and urban renewal initiatives expand capabilities, reduce household risk, and strengthen
aspirations. These interventions are not merely redistributive; they stabilise consumption,
sustain domestic demand, and enable workers to avoid distress-driven employment.

30.

Several of these models have set national benchmarks and are being replicated by other
states.
Women’s development in Tamil Nadu represents both a major achievement and a new
frontier. The State leads in education, health outcomes, fertility reduction, financial
inclusion, and political participation of women. However, high educational attainment
coexists with low labour force participation among young and educated women. Care
burdens, social norms, mobility constraints, and a shortage of high-quality jobs remain
the binding constraints. The next phase of development must move from access to
transformation by centring women’s employment in policy, expanding childcare, housing,
and transport, creating jobs in sunrise sectors, and reshaping norms to redistribute care
responsibilities.

31.

Tamil Nadu is among the most literate states in India and consistently excels in elementary,
secondary, and higher education enrolment. The State has the largest number of
technical institutions in the country, including 463 engineering and architecture colleges
and 492 polytechnic colleges in 2024–25. Seventeen institutions from Tamil Nadu feature
in the NIRF 2025 Top 100 overall rankings, the highest representation among all states.
The State leads the country in the Gross Enrolment Ratio in higher education at 47 %,
compared to the national average of 28.4 %, and ranks third in female GER at 47.3 %, well
above the national figure of 28.5 %. These outcomes demonstrate the effectiveness of
Tamil Nadu’s welfare and education model.

32.

The State also performs strongly across key health indicators. Tamil Nadu has achieved
a low infant mortality rate of 12 per 1,000 live births in 2023, a low birth rate of 12, one
of the lowest levels of multidimensional poverty at 1.43 % in 2022–23, and ranks third
nationally in life expectancy at 73.4 years.

33.

Environmental governance in Tamil Nadu is institutionally advanced but faces increasing
strain from industrialisation, urbanisation, and climate risks. Persistent challenges related
to water pollution, waste management, and air quality call for technological upgrading,
stricter enforcement, and greater community-based transparency. Institutional
fragmentation and data silos weaken policy coherence, indicating the need for deeper
coordination and integrated data systems.

34.

Fiscal policy has broadly supported Tamil Nadu’s growth trajectory. While the pandemic
necessitated expansionary measures that raised deficits and debt, subsequent years
have seen stabilisation alongside a strong push for capital formation. Total capital
expenditure between 2020–21 and 2025–26 amounts to Rs.2.54 lakh crore, reflecting a
decisive emphasis on asset creation. Although the debt-to-GSDP ratio remains below the
thresholds recommended by the Fifteenth Finance Commission, future pressures arising

from pay revisions, pensions, and GST rationalisation could strain fiscal space. Sustaining
growth will therefore require disciplined and productive use of resources.
35.

In conclusion, Tamil Nadu’s experience affirms a fundamental principle: social justice is not
a cost to development but its most powerful catalyst. By treating welfare as entitlement
and capability, the State has transformed exclusion into participation and deprivation
into productivity. The coming decade will test the adaptability of this model amid ageing,
urbanisation, climate stress, fiscal constraints, and technological change. The task ahead
is not to retreat from welfare, but to renew it by making programmes more outcomeoriented, resilient, and aligned with emerging realities. Sustaining high growth with
equity will depend on preserving fiscal space for social and capital investment, deepening
labour-market inclusion, reorienting industrial and services growth towards learning
and employment, completing the shift to climate-resilient infrastructure, integrating
environmental governance across sectors, and converting women’s social gains into
economic participation and leadership.

Chapter 1

State of the Economy
1.1 Introduction
1.1.1

Tamil Nadu has consistently emerged as one of India’s top-performing states across
economic, industrial, and social indicators. With a diversified economy, robust infrastructure,
and progressive policies, the state has positioned itself as a leader in development and
inclusive growth. Its balanced growth model, combining rapid industrialization, strong
social welfare and sustainability initiatives, has made Tamil Nadu a role model for other
states. The state’s continued top performance significantly contributes to India’s overall
economic and developmental progress. This chapter provides an overview of Tamil Nadu’s
economic landscape.

1.1.2

Tamil Nadu is India’s second largest economy and one of its fastest-growing states. As
per the provisional estimates for 2024-25, the state recorded a real Gross State Domestic
Product (GSDP) growth rate of 11.19%. This double-digit growth marks the state’s
strongest economic performance in 14 years, since it recorded a growth of 13.12% in
2010-11. Notably, this growth is far exceeding the national average growth rate of 6.5%.
At nominal prices, Tamil Nadu’s GSDP is estimated at Rs. 31.19 lakh crore (US $ 370 billion)
for 2024-25, reflecting a strong 15.98% annual growth.1 The budget document projects
Tamil Nadu’s nominal GSDP at Rs. 36.57 lakh crore in 2025-26 and Rs. 41.03 lakh crore in
2026-26.

1.1.3

This remarkable growth was driven primarily by the secondary sector, which registered
13.43% in 2024-25. Within the secondary sector, the construction sector grew at 11.56%,
while the manufacturing industry posted a robust real growth rate of 14.74% (compared
to all India average growth rate of just 4.5%). Thus, manufacturing remains the major
engine of Tamil Nadu’s growth momentum.

1.1.4

The state continues to reinforce its reputation as an industrial powerhouse, contributing
12.65% of India’s manufacturing GDP in 2024-25. The state has one of the most developed
and diverse industrial ecosystems in the country, with over 40,000 factories, the highest
among all states. It also ranks third nationwide in the MSME sector, with more than 39
lakh Udyam-registered Micro, Small and Medium Enterprises (MSMEs), highlighting its
strong entrepreneurial base. 2

1.1.5

Tamil Nadu leads the nation in the four-year average growth (2021-22 to 2024-25) rate
of the manufacturing sector at 9.38%, followed by Jharkhand (8.77%) and Karnataka
(8.73%). Tamil Nadu stands second in Export Preparedness Index, and credit-deposit ratio
(120.5%), third in total exports ($52,073.59 million), and third in per capita (NSDP) income
(Rs. 3.62 lakh) in 2024-25. 3

1In 2024-25, Tamil Nadu’s GSDP stood at US $ 368.28 billion, surpassing the Czech Republic’s GDP of US$ 344.9 billion and slightly trailing Pakistan’s GDP of US $ 373.08 billion.

2 Source: https://dashboard.msme.gov.in/Udyam_Statewise.aspx (as of 8th December 2025)
3 Sources: Export Preparedness Index 2022 (NITI Aayog); RBI website; https://niryat.gov.in and MoSPI website.
29

1.1.6

Tamil Nadu’s Gross State Value Added (GSVA) is driven predominantly by the tertiary
(services) sector, which accounts for 53.6%, followed by the secondary sector (33.1%) and
the primary sector (13.4%). The services sector also registered a double-digit real growth
rate of 11.3% in 2024-25. Within this sector, real estate (12.42%), public administration
(14.2%), transport, hotels and restaurants (11.29%) and other miscellaneous services
(12,49%) achieved double-digit growth rates in 2024-25, reflecting broad-based
expansion. 4

1.1.7

The state has the largest number of technical institutions in India, with 463 engineering
and architecture colleges and 492 polytechnic colleges (2024-25).5 Notably, 17 institutions
are featuring in the National Institutional Ranking framework (NIRF) 2025 Top 100 overall
rankings, the highest representation among all States in India. 6 These institutions
produce a highly skilled workforce that supports both the manufacturing and services.
The availability of a disciplined and technically competent labour force continues to
attract major multinational companies. Furthermore, the state is investing in research
parks, incubation centres, and innovation clusters to foster a knowledge-driven and
technology-oriented economy.

1.1.8

Tamil Nadu consistently ranks first in exports of electronic, textiles, and leather products,
and second in engineering exports. Tamil Nadu is recognized as the ‘Detroit of India’ - the
major hub for the automobile industry. It is also a pioneer in renewable energy, ensuring
reliable power for industries and has developed into a significant logistic hub. 7 Tamil
Nadu consistently remains among the top states in attracting Foreign Direct Investment
(FDI).

1.1.9

Tamil Nadu is the second most urbanized state (54.72% in 2025), after Kerala (80.08%).
It has achieved a low Infant Mortality Rate (12 per 1,000 live births in 2023), low birth
rate (12 in 2023), and one of the lowest rates of multidimensional poverty (1.43% in 202223).8 The state ranks third in life expectancy (73.4 years), and fourth in literacy (80.9%
in 2011). 9 Chennai, one of the world’s largest urban agglomerations, is known for orderly
urban management and disciplined civic conduct. The Chennai Metropolitan Area Parking
Policy 2025 is recognised as a national best practice in urban demand management.
Tamil Nadu ranks first in Civic Behaviour in the India Today Gross Domestic Behaviour
Survey. 10

1.1.10 Tamil Nadu leads the country in the Gross Enrolment Ratio (GER) in higher education at
47%, compared to the national average of 28.4%. It also ranks third in the female GER
in higher education (47.3%), well above the national figure (28.5%).11 Thus, Tamil Nadu’s
welfare model is successful in bringing fruitful results.
4 Sources: MoSPI website.
5 Sources: Policy Note (2025-26), Higher Education Department, GoTN.
6 Sources: Economic Survey (2025-26), Government of India
7 Under the National Green Hydrogen Mission, V.O.Chidambaranar Port Authority is identified as one of the three Green Hydrogen hubs in the

country, alongside Gujarat and Odisha to produce 5 million metric tonnes of green hydrogen annually by 2030.
8 Sources: Report of the Technical Group on Population Projection, July 2020; SRS Bulletin, September 2025; and Multidimensional Poverty in

1.1.11

The Economic Survey 2025-26 provides a detailed analysis of Tamil Nadu’s economy,
offering an assessment of key sectors including agriculture, industry, services, and
infrastructure. The survey examines trends in inflation, employment, and select social
sector indicators along with medium-term growth outlook.

1.2 Global Economy
1.2.1

Economic growth is vital as it lays the foundation for development and long-term
prosperity. According to the World Economic Outlook (WEO), the global economy, which
had contracted by -2.67% in 2020 due to COVID-19 pandemic, entered a phase of recovery
beginning in 2023, registering a growth rate of 3.49% (Figure 1.1).12 The April, July, and
October 2025 and Jan 2026 WEO updates noted that ‘signs of stabilization were emerging
through much of 2024, following a prolonged and challenging period of unprecedented
shocks.’ The combined effects of the pandemic, ongoing geopolitical tensions, and
extreme weather events had disrupted global supply chains and triggered crises in both
energy and food sectors.

1.2.2

Global inflation, which had reached multi-decade highs of 8.6% in 2022, gradually
moderated-albeit unevenly to 6.63% in 2023 and 5.8% in 2024, moving closer to central bank
targets. Despite persistent uncertainties, cyclical imbalances began to ease during 2024,
leading to a more sustainable alignment between global economic activity and potential
output. Consequently, the world economy recorded a modest growth rate of 3.29% in
2024, reflecting cautious but steady progress toward post-pandemic normalization.

1.2.3

In the aftermath of the pandemic recovery, major policy shifts have reshaped the global
trade landscape, introducing new layers of uncertainty. Since February 2025, the United
States has imposed multiple rounds of tariffs on several trading partners, prompting
retaliatory measures and heightening trade tensions. 13 Despite these headwinds, the
global economy grew at 3.3% in 2025. It is also projected to grow by 3.3% in 2026 and

12 2023 means 2023-24.
13 However, the trade agreements between the USA and certain trading partners have lowered the USA’s effective tariff rate. As a result,
global economic activity has remained relatively stable in the short run.

31

3.2% in 2027 (Figure 1.1). Global inflation is expected to ease further, declining from 5.8%
in 2024 to 4.2% in 2025 and 3.7% in 2026, reflecting tighter monetary policies and easing
commodity prices. Average crude oil prices have fallen steadily from US$96.36 per barrel
in 2022 to US$ 80.60 in 2023, and US$ 79.17 in 2024. They are projected to decline further
to US$ 68.92 in 2025 and US$ 65.84 in 2026, offering some relief to net oil-importing
economies. 14
1.2.4

The growth in export volumes of goods and services accelerated to 3.75% in 2024, up
from 0.95% in 2023, supported by moderating inflation, lower interest rates, favourable
trade dynamics and easing geopolitical tensions. However, renewed tariff wars and
policy-induced uncertainties are expected to weigh on trade performance. According
to the WEO, global export growth is projected to slow sharply to 1.66% in 2025, before
recovering modestly to 2.45% in 2026.

1.3 Indian Economy
1.3.1

India is the world’s fastest growing major economy with its growth fuelled by its strong
macroeconomic fundamentals, robust domestic demand, rapid digital transformation,
and government-led reforms. It is currently the world’s fourth largest economy (after
USA, China and Germany), with a nominal GDP of Rs. 330.68 trillion (US $ 3.909 trillion) for
2024-25. The IMF forecasts India will overtake Germany by 2028 to become the world’s
third largest economy.

1.3.2

India’s GDP growth in 2011-12 prices was -5.78% in 2020-21 (the COVID-19 year) and showed
significant volatility post-COVID, dropping from a recovery high of 9.69% in 2021-22 to
7.61% in 2022-23. Although it rebounded to 9.19% in 2023-24, the growth for 2024-25
was moderated to 6.49%, primarily due to weak investment and slower manufacturing
industry growth (Figure 1.2).

1.3.3

On the demand side, while the Private Final Consumption Expenditure (PFCE) accelerated
to 7.2% growth in 2024-25 (up from 5.56% in 2023-24), the momentum in investment,
measured by Gross Fixed Capital Formation (GFCF) slowing down, and it decreased from
8.78% in 2023-24 to 7.06% in 2024-25. Regarding external demand, real growth in exports
of goods and services was accelerated to 6.34% in 2024-25, compared to 2.2% in 2023-24.
Crucially, imports growth declined significantly to -3.67% in 2024-25 from 13.75% in 202324. As a result, the drag of net exports on real GDP has lessened, improving from -3.2%
in 2023-24 to -0.86% in 2024-25.

1.3.4

Analysis of sectoral growth trends indicate that the tertiary (services) sector bore the
brunt of the 2020-21 slowdown with -8.35% growth. It subsequently posted a strong
recovery of 10.33% in 2022-23 (Figure 1.3), though its growth has since moderated to 7.19%
in 2024-25. In contrast, the primary and secondary sectors managed positive growth of
2.33% and 0.24% respectively in 2020-21, but their subsequent growth path has been
volatile, alternating between increases and declines.

1.3.5

During the four-year period from 2021-22 to 2024-25, the average growth rates for the
primary, secondary and tertiary sectors stood at 4.46%, 8.17% and 8.92% respectively,
yielding an overall average GDP growth of 8.25%. This robust performance highlights the
strong pent-up demand post-pandemic, primarily driven by the secondary and tertiary
sectors. However, it is important to note that the average growths for five-year period
(including 2020-21 Contraction), are notably lower: 4.03% (Primary), 6.58% (Secondary)
and 5.46% (Tertiary), resulting in an average growth of 5.44%.15

33

1.3.6

Between 2020-21 and 2024-25, India’s Consumer Price Index (CPI) inflation averaged at
5.68%, exceeding the 4% target (with a tolerance band of ± 2 percentage points) set
under the Monetary Policy Framework. However, the inflation trend moderated from
5.4% in 2023-24 to 4.6% in 2024-25, reflecting easing supply side pressures. In its recent
meeting of the Monetary Policy Committee (MPC) held between 4-6 February, 2026, the
Reserve Bank of India (RBI) opted to keep the repo rate unchanged at 5.25% (the rate at
which commercial banks borrow from the RBI), as the central bank has already cut the
repo rate by 1.25% in response to falling inflationary pressures.

1.3.7

According to the Department of Promotion of Industry Policy and International Trade
Promotion, India’s FDI equity inflows stood at US$ 59.64 billion in 2020-21, but declined
slightly to US$ 58.77 billion in 2021-22 due to the pandemic-induced slowdown. The inflows
further fell to US$ 46.03 billion in 2022-23 and US$ 44.42.08 billion in 2023-24, reflecting
global investment uncertainty and tighter financial conditions. However, the FDI inflows
rebounded to US$50.05 billion in 2024-25, signalling a recovery in investor confidence.

1.3.8

In the first quarter of 2025-26, the GFCF grew by 7.8% exceeding the average growth
of the previous four quarters (at 7.2%) by 0.6 percentage points. The Government Final
Consumption Expenditure (GFCE) growth was 7.5%, marginally higher by 0.3 percentage
point compared to its recent average. Exports grew by 6.3%, trailing behind import
growth of 10.9%.

1.3.9

A major policy reform during this period was the rollout of GST 2.0 on September 22,
2025, which simplified the tax structure by reducing the four core rate slabs (5%, 12%,
18%, and 28%) to two primary rates (5% and 18%) and introducing a special rate of 40%.
The reform is expected to reduce post-tax prices and stimulate consumer demand.

1.3.10 The RBI projects the Indian economy to grow by 7.3% in 2025-26.16 However, the recent
advance estimates indicate 7.4% growth (Figure 1.2). Indian rupee value has continued to
depreciate against the US dollar, falling from Rs. 64.45 in 2017-18 to Rs.91 - 92 in recent
months, primarily due to global trade imbalances, capital outflows and geopolitical
tensions.

1.4 Tamil Nadu Economy
1.4.1

Tamil Nadu, India’s second largest economy, has emerged as the fastest-growing state
in 2024-25, recording an impressive double-digit growth rate of 11.19% (at 2011-12 prices),
compared to the national average of 6.5% (Figure 1.4). This exceptional performance
reflects the state’s visionary policy framework, resilient industrial base, and commitment
to inclusive and sustainable growth. As Tamil Nadu continues to advance up in the
industrial and technological value chain, it is well-positioned not only to achieve its
trillion-dollar goal by 2030, but also to play a pivotal role in India’s transformation into a
developed economy status by 2047.

16 However, the WEO (Jan 2026) projects that Indian economy will grow at 7.3% in 2025-26 and 6.4% in 2026-27. The RBI also projected earlier
at 6.8%.

Figure 1.4: Growth of Tamil Nadu Economy

1.4.2

During the pandemic year 2020-21, when most state economies contracted, Tamil Nadu
recorded a modest yet positive real growth rate of 0.07%, primarily driven by the
primary sector, which expanded by 3.37%. This resilience highlights Tamil Nadu’s ability to
withstand global economic shocks better than the national economy. The state’s economy
rebounded strongly in the following years, growing by 7.89% in 2021-22, 6.17% in 2022-23
(mainly because the secondary sector grew only at 1.89%), 9.26% in 2023-24 and 11.19%
in 2024-25 (Figure 1.4). Between 2020-21 and 2024-25, Tamil Nadu recorded an average
annual growth rate of 6.92%, nearly 1.5 percentage points higher than the national
average. In the more recent four-year period, the state’s average growth accelerated
further to 8.63%.

1.4.3

A simple linear trend projection, based on these latest four-year growth rates, indicates
that Tamil Nadu economy could grow by 11.88% in 2025-26, reinforcing its trajectory as
one of India’s most dynamic and rapidly expanding state economies. 17 However, based
on the latest ten-year growth rates, the linear projection indicates a moderate growth
of 8%. Averaging these two estimates indicate approximately 10% growth of Tamil Nadu
economy in 2025-26.

1.4.4

Tamil Nadu also experienced a downward trend, with retail (CPI) inflation declining
from 5.9% in 2022-23 to 5.4% in 2023-24 and further to 4.6% in 2024-25. In 2025-26 (as
of December 2025), it declined to 2.3%. The WPI inflation continuously declined from
8.6% in 2022-23 to 3.5% in 2024-25. This sustained moderation in inflation reflects the
effectiveness of the State’s supply chain management, market intervention mechanisms,
and overall economic governance. The state’s performance in containing inflationary
pressures has contributed to protecting the purchasing power of its citizens, particularly
those in the lower income segments.

17 The MoSPI’s advance estimates indicate a 7.4% growth rate for the national economy in 2025-26, an increase of 0.91percentage points over
2024-25. Tamil Nadu is expected to align with this upward national trajectory. An alternate projection approach assumes that all ten non-agricultural sub-sectors will grow by 0.5 percentage points more than their 2024-25 rates, while the agriculture, forestry and fishing sector will
expand by 1.5 percentage points above its 2024-25 performance. Under these assumptions, Tamil Nadu’s real GSDP growth for 2025-26 is estimated at 11.89%. This estimate closely aligns with the simple liner trend presented in Figure 1.4.

35

1.5 GSDP Trend in Tamil Nadu
1.5.1

The growth trajectory of Tamil Nadu economy shown in Figure 1.5 reveals a robust
expansion at an average annual rate of 7.18% during the pre-pandemic period (2014-15
to 2018-19). However, the onset of the pandemic disrupted this momentum, with growth
slowing to 3.35% in 2019-20 and 0.07% in 2020-21, resulting in an average growth rate
of 1.66% during the COVID years. The post-COVID period marked a decisive turnaround,
as Tamil Nadu rebuilt its economic foundations and emerged as a national leader in
manufacturing-led and technology-driven growth. From 2021-22 to 2024-25, the state
achieved an average growth rate of 8.63%, which is 1.45 percentage points higher than
the pre-pandemic average, signalling a strong and sustained recovery in its economic
fundamentals.

1.5.2

During the pre-pandemic period (2014-15 to 2018-19), Tamil Nadu ranked tenth among

and sixth ranks respectively, Tamil Nadu advanced in the ranking, highlighting its strong
post-pandemic recovery and growth momentum.

(i) Sectoral Growth

1.6.1

Tamil Nadu’s economy mirrors the growth pattern of other large, industrialised states
and has consistently demonstrated strong performance. In 2024-25, it recorded a
real economic growth of 11.19% for 2024-25, the highest among all Indian states. This
Major
States
exceptional performance was primarily
driven
by the secondary sector which comprises
manufacturing, construction and utilities (electricity, gas, and water supply). The
secondary sector recorded a robust growth rate of 13.43% in 2024-25, following 13.7% in
2023-24 (Figure 1.7).

1.6.2

Within the secondary sector, manufacturing emerged as a key engine. The sector posted
a robust real growth rate of 14.74% in 2024-25, which is more than three-times the allIndia average of 4.5%. It also recorded a strong 12.59% in 2023-24. Tamil Nadu’s sustained
industrial leadership is rooted in early industrialization, sectoral diversification, strong
infrastructure, a skilled workforce, and stable governance. Its success is further bolstered
by a sharp focus on export competitiveness and forward-looking industrial policies.

1.6.3

Economic Survey of India (2025-26) has recognized Tamil Nadu as the foremost
contributor to Indian’s manufacturing employment, accounting for 15% of the nation’s
total factory workforce. The Survey cites the automobile and auto-components cluster
and the electronics manufacturing ecosystem at Sriperumbudur as exemplary cases of
successful industrial policy and ecosystem-led development.

1.6.4

Building upon its historical strengths in textiles and leather, Tamil Nadu has successfully
cultivated a diverse industrial ecosystem. Today, the state hosts thriving clusters including
Automobiles & auto components (Chennai, the renowned “Detroit of India”), Textiles &
garments (Tiruppur, Coimbatore), Leather (Vellore, Ambur), Electronics & IT (Chennai,
Hosur, Sriperumbudur), Renewable energy (Wind power corridor from Coimbatore to
Tirunelveli) and Heavy Engineering, Cement, Fertilisers and Chemicals. The state has also
emerged as a significant hub for India’s defence and aerospace industries. Tamil Nadu,
one among the three states, has received final approval for financial assistance of Rs 100
crore for establishing a Medical Device Park under the scheme for Promotion of Medical
Devices Parks.18

1.6.5

In a remarkable display of integrated development, every district in Tamil Nadu is now
covered under one or more industrial corridor projects, making it the only state in India
with such extensive industrial integration. Finally, the construction sector also played a
pivotal role, growing at 15.93% in 2023-24 and 11.56% in 2024-25 while the utilities sector
grew at 13.34% and 5.28% during the same periods.

1.6.6

The tertiary sector (services) sustained strong growth momentum, recording a doubledigit growth rate of 11.3% in 2024-25, up from 7.47% growth in 2023-24. Within this sector,
real estate witnessed a sharp rise, increasing from 7.33% in 2023-24 to 12.42% in 202425. Other service sub-sectors, including public administration, transport, hotels and
restaurants and miscellaneous services, also reported double-digit growth in 2024-25,
contributing to a broad-based expansion in this sector.

1.6.7

Tamil Nadu’s primary sector, comprising agriculture, livestock, and forestry and logging
and fishing and aquaculture and mining and quarrying, was the only sector to register

18 Source: Economic Survey (2025-26), Govt of India.
38

positive growth (3.37%) during the pandemic year (2020-21). Its growth increased to 6.82%
in 2021-22, but declined to 1.21% in 2023-24, largely due to a contraction in agriculture
(-1.96%). In 2024-25, the primary sector grew to 3.18%, supported by strong performance
in forestry (8.77%) and fishing (9.89%), though agriculture again recorded negative growth
(-1.83%).
1.6.8

An analysis of temporal trends indicates that the average real growth rates of agriculture
and livestock during the pandemic period (2019-20 and 2020-21) were 6.81% and 5.52%
respectively (Table 1.1). However, from 2021-22 to 2024-25, the respective average growth
rates of these two subsectors declined to 3.03% and 3.89%. A crucial factor influencing
agricultural performance was the impact of weather conditions. The climate variability
posed significant challenges. However, farmers with diverse income streams were better
positioned to navigate these uncertainties. Government supports also helped them.
Interestingly, Tamil Nadu ranked first in ragi yield (3,758 kg/ha.), and sugarcane yield
(1,01,435 kg/ha). It ranked second in oil seeds yield (1,976 kg/ha.), third in groundnut yield
(2,390 kg/ha.) in 2024-25. Its success in banana cultivation is also cited by the Economic
Survey of India as a region-specific horticultural achievement.

1.6.9

The average real growth of the secondary sector was 8.18% from 2014-15 to 2018-19
(Table 1.1). While it declined to -0.04% from 2019-20 to 2020-21, this sector showed a
strong revival with the average of 9.64% from 2021-22 to 2024-25. The tertiary sector
also showed a strong revival with the average growth rate of 8.7% from 2021-22 to 202425, while its average growth was 6.27% from 2014-15 to 2018-19. However, the primary
sector experienced a slowdown, averaging 3.85% from 2021-22 to 2024-25, down from
5.65% during 2014-15 to 2018-19. Notably, it recorded 5.02% during 2019-20 to 2020-21.

1.6.10 Table 1.1 shows that average growth rates of construction, utilities, trade, transport,
real estate, public administration, and other services from 2021-22 to 2024-25 improved
significantly compared to their average growths from 2014-15 to 2018-19, reflecting a
board-based revival across both secondary and tertiary sectors. Agriculture, mining and
quarrying also improved their performances.

1.7 Per Capita Income
1.7.1

In per capita income (NSDP) at current prices, Tamil Nadu ranked third among major Indian
states in 2024-25 (Figure 1.8), with a per capita income of Rs. 3.62 lakh, compared to the
all-India average of Rs.2.05 lakh (i.e., about 1.76 times the national figure). 19 Telangana
ranked first with Rs. 3.87 lakh, followed by Karnataka (Rs.3.8 lakh).

1.7.2

In real terms, Tamil Nadu ranked second among major Indian states in 2024-25, with
a per capita income of Rs. 1.98 lakh, only behind Karnataka (Rs.2.05 lakh). This shift in
ranking reflects the impact of differing price levels and purchasing power, which slightly
increases the real value of income in Tamil Nadu relative to other states. While excessive
inflation can be detrimental, moderate levels of inflation, as observed in Tamil Nadu, is a
natural outcome of a growing economy.

1.8 Tamil Nadu’s Economic Growth through Supply and
Demand Factors
1.8.1

Economic growth reflects an increase in GSDP (state output) and state income. Tamil
Nadu’s sustained economic growth is driven by two main factors: expansion in aggregate
supply (productive capacity) and growth in aggregate demand. From the supply-side
perspective, often summarized as ‘supply creates its own demand’, policies such as
deregulation (ease of doing), investment incentives(such as loans), innovation support,
and low inflation play a crucial role. These measures encourage capital formation,
investment, job creation, productivity growth, long term-economic growth, and a trickledown effect, where business advantages generate broader societal benefits including
higher wages and employment. On the other hand, increase spending by consumers,
businesses and government stimulate overall economic activity. Through the multiplier
effect, higher demand amplifies production and growth across sectors.

1.8.2

On the supply side, Tamil Nadu possesses significant productive capacity and efficiency
advantages. The state has developed robust physical infrastructure, including reliable
electricity, extensive road and port networks, and industrial corridors, enhancing
industrial competitiveness. High levels of human capital, reflected in literacy, technical
education and skill development, ensure a productive workforce capable of supporting
both manufacturing and services.

1.8.3

Its diversified industrial base, spanning traditional and modern sectors, and a vibrant
MSME ecosystem promote broad-based production growth. Adoption of technology,
research institutions, and IT clusters in Chennai, Coimbatore, and Hosur further strengthen
productivity and innovation. Combined with sound governance and an enabling regulatory
environment, these factors enhance supply responsiveness and attract sustained
investment.

42

1.8.4

Tamil Nadu continues to rank among the top Indian states in attracting Foreign Direct
Investment (FDI). Even as India’s overall FDI equity inflows declined from US$46.03 billion
in 2022-23 to US$44.42 billion in 2023-24, amid global investment uncertainty and tighter
financial conditions, Tamil Nadu defied the trend. The state’s FDI inflows rose steadily
from US$2,169 million in 2022–23 to US$2,436 million in 2023–24, and further to US$3,681
million in 2024-25. 20

1.8.5

This performance reflects the state government’s proactive investment promotion
strategy. At the Global Investors Meet (GIM) 2024, Tamil Nadu signed 631 MoUs worth
Rs.6.64 trillion, signalling strong investor confidence.21 In addition, the Chief Minister’s
investment promotion visits to the United States, United Kingdom, and Germany during
2024–25 secured new industrial commitments amounting to Rs.23,000 crore.

1.8.6

On the demand side, the state benefits from a large, diversified consumer base, high per
capita income, and rapid urbanization which fuel household consumption, particularly in
urban and semi-urban areas. Strong investment demand, driven by private investment
in manufacturing, automobiles, electronics, and renewable energy, is supported by
a conducive business climate and industrial policies. Government expenditure on
infrastructure, education, health, and social welfare further reinforces aggregate demand.

1.8.7

Tamil Nadu’s strong export performance provides additional stimulus. The state
consistently ranks among India’s top exporters, particularly in textiles, leather, automobiles,
and machinery. Its vibrant MSME ecosystem, comprising over 39 lakh enterprises, plays
a pivotal role in driving both exports and employment. In recent years, the state has
excelled in high-technology exports, overtaking Karnataka and Uttar Pradesh to become
India’s leading exporter of electronic goods, with export volumes rising sharply to US$
14.65 billion in 2024-25. Overall, Tamil Nadu’s merchandise exports nearly doubled from
US$26.15 billion in 2020-21 to US$ 52.07 billion in 2024-25, highlighting Tamil Nadu’s
growing global competitiveness and industrial diversification. 22

1.8.8

The interaction of strong domestic and external demand with robust productive capacity
explains Tamil Nadu’s steady and resilient growth trajectory. Rising demand has been
effectively met by strong supply-side strengths, preventing inflationary pressures while
sustaining output expansion. The retail inflation in October 2025 dropped significantly
to 1.29% and food inflation to 0.39%. While demand-side forces drive short-term growth
impulses, the state’s supply-side advantages such as human capital, infrastructure, and
institutional quality—anchor its long-term growth potential. Tamil Nadu’s experience
demonstrates how balanced demand and supply dynamics can produce an economy that
is both fast-growing and structurally robust.

20 Source: FDI Factsheet June 2025.XLSX by Department for Promotion of Industry and International Trade.
21 Source: Press Release No. 056 dated 8.1.2024 by GoTN.
22 Source: NIRYAT.
43

1.9 Towards One Trillion Dollar Economy and High-Income
Status
1.9.1

Tamil Nadu aspires to become a US $ 1 Trillion economy by 2030, a goal that carries
transformative potential for its 7.7 crore citizens, particularly the youth seeking quality
job and higher incomes. The prospect of achieving the target has significantly improved
due to the recent surge in the economic growth rate. If Tamil Nadu sustains its 202425 nominal growth rate of 16% and with an assumption of 2% (medium-term rate) rise
in the value of dollar against rupee per annum in the medium term, it can achieve the
trillion-dollar milestone by 2031. With 3.5% (short term rate of rise of dollar value), it may
be delayed for a year.

1.9.2

The World Bank’s per capita income threshold for high income countries-currently set
at US $ 14,006 for 2024-25 is widely used to define a developed country. Since this
threshold has risen from US$9,646 in 1997-98, marking an annual increase of US$176.33,
extrapolating this trend will provide the target income level of around US$18,414 for
2047-48 when India aspires to become a developed nation. However, this threshold will
be US$ 16,122 for 2034-35, and US$ 16,298 for 2035-36. In 2024-25, Tamil Nadu’s per capita
GSDP was US$4,763, which fall into upper-middle income category ranged US$ 4,516
-14,500.

1.9.3

At its present pace of 15.98% nominal growth, Tamil Nadu is on track to cross the highincome country threshold of US$16,122 by 2034-35. With stronger investments, export
diversification and expansion, and revival of the primary sector, particularly the crop
sector, the state is expected to grow at a higher rate in 2025-26 than in 2024-25. It is
noted that one of the core strategies outlined by the government to achieve the one
trillion-dollar target is to increase exports to $ 100 billion by 2030. Given the state’s
strong export momentum, Tamil Nadu appears well on track to meet this goal, despite
temporary challenges such as the recent tariff hike imposed by the United States.

1.9.4

The state’s textile exports, which account for a major share of Tamil Nadu’s export, are
facing headwinds due to the recent 50% hike in U.S. tariffs. Nearly 30% of the state’s
textile exports are directed to the U.S. market. While this poses a temporary setback, the
Union Government’s suspension of the 11% customs duty on cotton until December 2025
offers partial relief to the sector. At the same time, Tamil Nadu is actively diversifying
its export markets to reduce dependence on traditional destinations and align with the
shifting contours of global trade. 23 The recent India-EU free trade agreement and IndiaUS trade agreement by which US tariffs on Indian goods will reduce from 50% to 18%
soon may help in this regard.

23 As per the quick estimates released by Ministry of Commerce and Industry, India’s exports recorded a subdued growth of 0.63% during
April-October FY26, lower than 3.33% during the corresponding period of FY25.

44

1.10 Conclusions
1.10.1 Tamil Nadu’s forward-looking industrial policies continue to attract significant domestic
and global investments. The state’s Industrial, Electronics, and Electric Vehicle Policies
offer a robust incentive framework for investors, reinforcing its position as a preferred
manufacturing destination. Tamil Nadu’s strong reform orientation, manufacturing depth,
faster project approvals, and future-ready industrial ecosystem have been highlighted
in the Economic Survey of India (2025-26). The development of sector-specific industrial
parks, particularly SIPCOT estates with plug-and-play infrastructure, has further
enhanced the state’s industrial competitiveness. Tamil Nadu has also emerged as India’s
leading hub for defence and aerospace manufacturing and has received approval of Rs.
100 crore in central assistance for the establishment of a Medical Devise Park.
1.10.2 The state’s strong international connectivity through Chennai, Coimbatore, Madurai, and
Tiruchirappalli airports facilitates seamless trade and investment flows. In addition, the
proposed Aerospace and Defence Industrial Corridor connecting Chennai, Hosur, Salem,
Coimbatore, and Tiruchirappalli positions Tamil Nadu as a strategic node within India’s
manufacturing ecosystem, closely aligned with national industrial objectives. Tamil
Nadu’s industrial transition is further reinforced by future-facing initiatives, including
the designation of V.O. Chidambaranar Port as one of India’s three Green Hydrogen hubs.
1.10.3 A standout national recognition is Tamil Nadu’s first-place in manufacturing employment,
accounting for 15% of India’s total, the highest among all states. The Economic Survey of
India also cites Tamil Nadu as a model “entrepreneurial state” that enables cluster-led
industrialization, citing the state’s auto component ecosystem and electronic clusters.
These recognitions validate Tamil Nadu’s development trajectory, characterised by
reform-driven facilitation, ecosystem-building, and the creation of next-generation
industrial capacity, positioning it as one of India’s most credible long-term destinations.

1.10.4 As Tamil Nadu continues to move up the industrial and technological value chain, it is well
positioned not only to achieve its trillion-dollar economy goal by 2030, but also to play a
pivotal role in India’s journey towards becoming a developed economy by 2047.

45

Chapter 2

Prices and Food Management
2.1 Introduction
2.1.1

Prices and inflation are key indicators of an economy’s health. While the price index
measures the overall level of prices of goods and services, inflation refers to a sustained
increase in the general price level over time, which erodes the purchasing power of
money. Inflation in India is influenced by several structural and cyclical factors, including
supply-side constraints in agriculture, fiscal deficits, global crude oil prices, exchange rate
fluctuations, shifting consumer demand patterns, and geopolitical developments.

2.1.2

Rising prices or inflation can reduce purchasing power of households, as their income can
buy fewer goods and services. When the prices of essential commodities such as food,
fuel and housing rise, the overall cost of living increases, disproportionally affecting lowand middle-income groups. If wages fail to keep pace with inflation, real incomes decline,
reducing living standards. Businesses too, may face higher input costs, which can lead to
either reduced profit margins or further price increases.

2.1.3

While moderate inflation reflects healthy demand and economic growth, persistently high
inflation undermines savings, discourages investment, and hurts the poor most. Ensuring
price stability is therefore a central goal of India’s monetary policy. Since 2016, India has
adopted a Flexible Inflation Targeting framework, setting a medium-term inflation target
of 4% with a tolerance band of ±2%, under the joint responsibility of the Reserve Bank
of India (RBI) and the Government of India.

2.1.4

Although the Union government plays a major role in controlling inflation, State
governments also contribute through mechanisms such as the Public Distribution
System (PDS), enhancing the disposable income with people through direct cash transfer
schemes, social security schemes and other poverty alleviating measures. Effective
management of the food economy helps stabilizes prices, ensure food security, support
rural livelihoods, and promote sustainable growth. Tamil Nadu, for instance, provides crop
insurance, and input subsidies for irrigation, fertilizers and other agricultural inputs to
mitigate supply side pressures.

2.1.5

Tamil Nadu’s PDS is among the most efficient in India, ensuring the equitable distribution
of essential commodities such as rice, sugar, and kerosene. Complimenting this, the
Nutritious Meal Programme and the Chief Minister’s Breakfast Scheme enhance nutritional
access and food security for school children, reflecting the state’s strong commitment to
welfare and human development.
46

2.1.6

In the medium to long term, States can curb inflationary pressures by strengthening
supply chains, improving infrastructure and logistics, and enhancing market efficiency.
However, some inflationary factors stem from global developments-such as the COVID-19
pandemic, oil shock or the Russian-Ukraine war, that lie beyond the control of individual
governments. In such circumstances, proactive state-level measures become critical for
maintaining price stability and managing food economy effectively. This chapter, reviews
the inflationary trends and food management strategies in Tamil Nadu, highlighting the
State’s approach to ensuring both price stability and food security.

2.2 CPI (Retail) Inflation in Tamil Nadu
2.2.1

The consumer price index (CPI), which serves as the benchmark for measuring retail
inflation, captures the price movements of a comprehensive basket of goods and services
commonly consumed by households in both rural and urban areas. Figure 2.1 presents
the annual CPI inflation trends for Tamil Nadu and India from 2020-21 to 2024-25. During
this period, Tamil Nadu’s average CPI inflation stood at 5.68%, marginally higher than
the national average of 5.66%. In 2020-21, the year of the COVID-19 pandemic, inflation
exceeded the upper tolerance limit of 6% (the target rate of 4% ± 2%) in both Tamil Nadu
and India, reflecting pandemic-induced supply disruptions and demand fluctuations.

(till December), the inflationary pressures have eased considerably, with Tamil Nadu’s
inflation moderating to 2.3% (2.67% in December, 2025), compared to all-India average of
1.7% (1.33% in December 2025). This downward trend indicates improved supply conditions,
better food price management, stabilization of key commodity prices, impact of GST
rationalization from September 2025 and market intervention mechanism.

West Bengal (4.04%) reported the lowest inflation rates, all below the Reserve Bank of
India’s targeted inflation rate of 4%.

2.2.9 In this context, policy measures implemented by the Tamil Nadu government such as
subsidized electricity and transportation, serve attention, as they help reduce input
costs, and potentially moderate inflationary pressures. A key component of the state’s
inflation management strategy is its unique Universal Public Distribution System (PDS),
which provides essential commodities to 2.25 crore. This includes free rice in addition to
the National Food Security Act (NFSA) entitlement, and dal and oil at subsidized rates.
The state also provides 100 units of free electricity to domestic households and Rs. 1,000
per month to the female head of 1.3 crore eligible families under the Kalaignar Mahalir
Urimai Thittam. While some of these measures control inflation by directly influencing
prices, others support households by enhancing purchasing power.

2.3 WPI and GSDP Deflator Based Inflation
2.3.1

Wholesale Price Index (WPI) inflation measures the average change in prices of goods at
the wholesale or production stage, before they reach consumers. It mainly covers goods
(manufactured products, fuel, power, and primary articles like food grains) and excludes
services. Although WPI was extensively used in the past for assessing inflation and guiding
monetary and fiscal policy, the CPI inflation has gained prominence in recent years due to
WPI’s limited coverage and its non-representative consumer basket.

2.3.2

GSDP deflator-based inflation, on the other hand, measures the changes in the overall price
level of all goods and services produced domestically. It captures price changes across the
entire economy, including consumption, investment, government expenditure, and net exports.
Unlike CPI or WPI, it accounts for price movements across the full production spectrum, not
just consumer or wholesale goods.

2.3.3 As shown in Table 2.1, during 2020-21 to 2024-25, Tamil Nadu’s average WPI inflation
and GSDP deflator-based inflation were slightly higher than the corresponding all-India

2.4 Group-wise Inflation
2.4.1

As shown in Figure 2.5, the CPI is broadly divided into six baskets or commodity groups,
namely food and beverages, pan, tobacco etc., clothing and footwear, housing, fuel and
light and miscellaneous items. After the pandemic, fuel and light inflation in Tamil Nadu
surged to 19.3% in 2021-22 and 16.4% in 2022-23, compared to 11.3% and 10.4% at the
national level (Table 2.2). However, with the fall in global energy prices in 2023-24, and
retail price cuts announced by the Union government (Rs.200 reduction per domestic
LPG cylinder in September, 2023 and Rs. 100 reductions for non-subsidized cylinders in
March, 2024), the retails fuel and light inflation in Tamil Nadu declined to 4%, while the
corresponding all India figure declined to 1.2%.

2.4.2 In 2024-25, fuel inflation turned negative for both Tamil Nadu (-5%) and India (-2.5%),
reflecting a combination of falling global energy prices, increased output from non-OPEC
countries, and weak global demand. Prices of mineral oils, crude petroleum and natural
gas declined in both Tamil Nadu and India, as reflected by deflation in the WPI.
2.4.3 After the pandemic, the clothing and footwear inflation in Tamil Nadu increased to 9.6%
in 2021-22 and 10% in 2022-23, while the corresponding all-India figures were 7.2% and
10% respectively. Inflationary pressures in both Tamil Nadu and India eased in 2024-25,
mainly due to reductions in clothing and footwear, housing and fuel inflations.
2.4.4 Looking at the average inflation by commodity groups during 2020-21 to 2024-25, the
fuel and light category recorded the highest average inflation in Tamil Nadu at 8.4%,
followed by food and beverages (6.5%), miscellaneous items (6.1%). For India, the food
and beverages had the highest average inflation at 6.3%, followed by miscellaneous items
(5.6%) and clothing and footwear (5.6%). It is observed that the inflation rates in 2025-26
(till December 2025) for all groups in both Tamil Nadu and India, except fuel and light and
miscellaneous items have come down significantly. Specifically, in December, 2025, the
food inflation is 0.19% in Tamil Nadu and -1.85% in India.

2.5 Core Inflation
2.5.1

Core inflation refers to the long-term trend in price level after excluding temporary or
volatile price movements. It is calculated by removing the prices of food and fuel/energy,
which often fluctuate due to seasonal or global factors such as weather shocks or oil
price volatility. By filtering out these short-term supply shocks, core inflation provides a
more stable measure of underlying inflation, helping policymakers, including the Reserve
Bank of India, make informed monetary policy decisions.

2.5.2 After the pandemic, the core inflation in Tamil Nadu declined to 4% in 2023-24, but
slightly increased to 4.1% in 2024-25 (Figure 2.8). At the same time, India’s core inflation
declined from 4.3% in 2023-24 to 3.5% in 2024-25. During 2020-21 to 2024-25, the average
core inflation was 5.2% for Tamil Nadu and 5.1% for India.

2.6 Food and Miscellaneous Inflation in Tamil Nadu
2.6.1

This section focuses on inflationary trends in two major components that together account
for nearly 75% of Tamil Nadu’s CPI basket: food and beverages, and miscellaneous items.
Food prices are particularly sensitive to climate-related factors, including heatwaves and
unpredictable rainfall patters. In Tamil Nadu, the food inflation surged to 7.4% in 2023-24
and moderated slightly to 6.6% in 2024-25, remaining higher than inflation in all other
categories. This increase was largely driven by a decline in agricultural production due to
adverse climatic conditions.

2.6.3 Inflation in the miscellaneous category declined from 6% in 2022-23 to 4.6% in 2023-24,
before rising slightly to 5% in 2024-25. Apart from food, it remained higher than inflation
in any other category. This miscellaneous group includes durable goods and services
such as education, health, transport and communication, recreation and amusement,
personal care, and household items. Inflation for household goods and services, health,
56

and education declined steadily from 2022-23 (Figure 2.9). In contrast, the personal care
inflation steadily increased from 2022-23. It reached a double digit (11.4%) level in 202425. Inflation for recreation and amusement was 6.3% in 2022-23 but declined to 3.6% in
2023-24. However, it increased to 4.6% in 2024-25.

2.7 Inflation Outlook
2.7.1

Like the rest of the country, inflation poses both economic and social challenges for the
people of Tamil Nadu. In 2024-25, the state’s rural inflation matched the national average.
In 2023-24, the state’s urban inflation was aligned with the national figure but slightly
higher than all India figure by 0.3%. However, urban inflation remained lower than rural
inflation. The ease of international oil prices helped moderate inflation in 2024-25 but still
remained above the targeted rate of 4%.

2.7.2

As observed from Figures 2.1 to 2.4, by 2025-26 (till Dec), inflation in Tamil Nadu had
moderated significantly to 2.4%, with national inflation also declining to 1.7%. Rural
inflation in the state declined to 2.7%, while urban inflation declined to 2.1%. The easing
of inflationary pressure was largely driven by sharp reductions in food and beverage, and
clothing and footwear inflation, which came down to -0.003% and 1.3% in Tamil Nadu, and
-0.8% and 2.2% nationally.

2.7.3

In response to easing inflation, the Monetary Policy Committee (MPC) cut the repo rate
three times in 2025: by 25 basis points in February 2025, 25 basis points in April 2025, and
50 basis points in June 2025. However, in October 2025, the MPC maintained the repo rate
at 5.5%, ending a series of rate cuts. While in December 2025, it was cut further by 25
points at 5.25%, in February 2026, it was kept remaining at 5.25%.

2.7.4

Overall, the analysis indicates that certain inflationary pressures, such as those driven
by weather disruptions and international commodity prices, are cyclical and/or seasonal.
At the same time, Tamil Nadu faces some structural challenges due to its relatively high
socio-economic development. For instance, relative to other states, Tamil Nadu’s overall
consumption basket has a higher share of services, which are more sensitive to input
costs such as wages. In fact, this is true even for the state’s rural consumption basket.

2.7.5

While Tamil Nadu faces certain peculiar challenges related to inflation management, it
has been largely successful in managing food management, as discussed in the following
section.

57

2.8 Management of Food Economy in Tamil Nadu
2.8.1

Food management encompasses the planning, production, distribution, and consumption
of food to ensure food security, minimizes wastage, and promote nutrition. In Tamil
Nadu, a state with a strong agricultural base and a growing urban population, effective
management of the food economy is essential not only to ensure food security but also
for stabilizing prices, supporting rural livelihoods, ensuring affordability and promoting
sustainable development. As shown in Table 2.2, food inflation in Tamil Nadu stood at
2.6% in 2021-22 and increased to 4.8% in 2022-23. It subsequently surged to 7.4% in
2023-24 before moderating slightly to 6.6% in 2024-25. These inflation trends exhibit an
inverse relationship with agriculture growth rates during the same period, which were
9.46%, 6.46%, -1.96% and -1.83% respectively. To maintain stability and resilience in the
food sector, the state has developed a multi-layered food management system that
integrates agricultural production, public stocks, and welfare programmes.

2.9 Agricultural Production and Food Supply
2.9.1

Agriculture forms the backbone of Tamil Nadu’s food economy. Assured remunerative
prices, improved access to institutional credit, crop diversification, free power for
irrigation, support for sustainable practices, and enhancement of productivity have been
crucial for the agriculture sector’s growth. The Union government sets the Minimum
support Price (MSP) for essential crops such as wheat, rice, pulses, oilseeds, and nutricereals, providing a safety net for farmers. The state government’s paddy procurement
incentive, in addition to the MSP, has further boosted paddy cultivation. For the Kharif
season of 2025-26, the MSP for paddy was revised by the Union government to Rs. 2,389
per quintal for Grade A and Rs. 2,369 per quintal for common varieties.2 Tamil Nadu
government has supplemented this with an incentive of Rs. 156 per quintal for Grade A
and Rs. 131 per quintal for common varieties.3

2.9.2 Tamil Nadu’s rice procurement is primarily managed by the Tamil Nadu Civil Supplies
Corporation (TNCSC) through a network of Direct Purchase Centres (DPCs) across the
state. For 2025-26 procurement season, the state has set a procurement target of 42
lakh metric tonnes, comprising 30 lakh metric tonnes of Grade A and 12 lakh metric tonnes
of common varieties. According to data from the Central authorities, approximately 3.36
lakh farmers in Tamil Nadu received Rs. 6,899 crore during the first round of payments
(from September 2024 to March 2025), while around 1.95 lakh farmers received Rs. 3,835
crore in subsequent payments (as of July 2025).
2.9.3 Credit support for farmers is provided through Primary Agricultural Cooperative Credit
Societies (PACCs), the Tamil Nadu State Apex Co-operative Bank, Commercial Banks and
2 Source: Press release dated 28th May 2025, available in https://www.pib.gov.in
3 Source: Press release No. 2306 dated 9th Sep 2025, available in https://cms.tn.gov.in/cms_migrated/document/press_release/pr270925_e_2306.pdf

58

other institutions. As per the RBI’s Handbook of Statistics on Indian States, the schedule
commercial banks extended agricultural credit of Rs. 3,81,757 crore in Tamil Nadu during
2024-25. PACCs disbursed agricultural loans amounting to Rs. 46,087 crore during the
same period.4 The National Bank for Agriculture and Rural Development (NABARD) also
plays a significant role, projecting a credit potential of Rs. 10.61 lakh crore for priority
sectors in 2026-27, with a substantial allocation for agriculture amounting to Rs. 5.46
lakh crore.5
2.9.4 Food processing is a critical component of food management, as it extends shelf life,
reduces post-harvest losses, and adds economic value to agricultural produce. Districts
such as Thanjavur and Erode host major rice milling and sugar processing industries.
Infrastructure for cold storage, canning and packaging supports the efficient handling
of perishable goods. According to the ASI data, Tamil Nadu had 3,723 operating food
manufacturing units in 2023-24, with a fixed capital investment of Rs. 22,450 crore. Of
these, 1,659 units were engaged in grain mill products, starches and starch products; 294
in dairy products and 129 in processing and preserving fruits and vegetables.
2.9.5 Tamil Nadu is a leading milk-producing state, with cooperative institutions ensuring
efficient procurement and distribution. Milk production increased steadily from 87.59
lakh tonnes in 2020 to 1.03 crore tonnes in 2023, reaching an all-time high of 1.08 crore
tonnes in 2024. The Tamil Nadu Cooperative Milk Produces’ Federation Ltd (AAVIN) is
the state-owned cooperative responsible for procuring, processing, and marketing milk
and milk products. AAVIN’s product portfolio includes milk, butter, curd, ice cream, ghee,
milkshakes, khoa, Badam Milk, tea, coffee, and chocolates, serving consumers across
Tamil Nadu and other states.

2.10 Food Security and Inflation
2.10.1 Under the National Food Security Act (NFSA)-2013, up to 75% of the rural population and
50% of the urban population are legally entitled to receive food grains, free of cost, under
the Targeted PDS. Tamil Nadu maintains extensive public stocks to stabilize supplies and
prices. The population benefits from these measures through an extensive network of
wholesale markets, retail chains, and cooperative societies. The state’s PDS ensures that
subsidized food grains reach economically vulnerable populations efficiently.
2.10.2 Tamil Nadu’s PDS has evolved into one of the most efficient, inclusive and well-governed
systems in India. By maintaining robust public food stocks and distributing them through
an extensive network of fair price (ration) shops, the state ensures access to essential
commodities such as rice, sugar, kerosene, pulses and millets. This system plays a critical
role in reducing hunger, malnutrition, and economic vulnerability (Table 2.4)
4 Source: Registrar of Cooperative Societies

5 Source: NABARD’s State Focus Paper (2026-2027): Tamil Nadu State

59

2.10.3 As of 31 October 2025, Tamil Nadu has a total of 26,787 fair price shops, of which 24,929
were operated by co-operative societies 2,417 function as mobile shops. In addition, the
state operated 11,061 part-time shops to ensure last-mile delivery in remote and sparsely
populated areas.6 Food subsidy expenditure in Tamil Nadu increased from Rs. 5000 crore
in 2014-15 to over Rs. 10,000 crore in 2024-25.
2.10.4 Tamil Nadu operates a universal PDS, covering 2.25 crore families-over 7 crore
beneficiaries-across both rural and urban areas (Figure 2.10). The state provides free rice
over and above NFSA entitlements, along with pulses, and edible oils at subsidized rates.
Altogether, more than 37,000 ration outlets support this extensive distribution network.
To further strengthen transparency and minimize leakages, the state has adopted biometric authentication for beneficiaries and digital stock monitoring across the supply
chain.

2.10.5 From 2020-21 to 2024-25, the quantity of rice supplied through the PDS increased from
38.975 lakh MTS to 43.569 lakh MTS, recording 11.8% increase (Figure 2.11). However,
the supply of other products declined during this period, especially the distribution of
kerosene declined from 1.29 lakh KL to 12,947 KL, registering about 90% fall.
2.10.6 Tamil Nadu produces a range of oilseeds, including groundnut (peanut), sesame,
sunflower, and coconut, which are processed to extract edible oils. The state also has a
well-developed edible oil processing industry, including (i) small-scale oil expellers and
refineries for groundnut and sesame seeds, (ii) large, refined oil units in Coimbatore,
Chennai and Erode, and (iii) retail distributional channels including supermarkets,
retail outlets, and government supported fair price shops for rural areas. Despite local
production, Tamil Nadu imports substantial quantities of palm oil, soybean oil, and
sunflower oil to meet consumption demand, as domestic oilseed production is insufficient
to satisfy consumption requirements of the state. These combined supply-side measures
and distribution mechanisms help contain edible oil prices, thereby contributing to a
moderation of food inflation towards near zero levels in 2025-26.
