2.11 Conclusions
2.11.1

Tamil Nadu Government has implemented a range of measures to strengthen food
management and promote agricultural sustainability. Investments in infrastructure such
as silos, cold chains and modern wholesale markets have enhanced the efficiency of food
storage and distribution. In addition, the state provides income support and essential
services to help households manage inflation and maintain access to nutritious food
through flagship schemes like Kalaignar Magalir Urimai Thittam, Mahalir Vidiyal Payanam
Thittam, and the provision of 100 units of free electricity for domestic households.

2.11.2 While the principal responsibility for inflation management rests with the Union
Government and the Reserve Bank of India, Tamil Nadu has developed a robust and
inclusive framework to support price stability and food security at the subnational level.
By effectively balancing economic management with social welfare interventions, Tamil
Nadu has demonstrated its ability to shield households from inflationary pressures. At
62

the core of this framework is Tamil Nadu’s Universal Public Distribution System, one
of the most comprehensive in India. It serves over 2.3 crore families with essential
commodities, including free rice over and above NFSA entitlements, along with pulses
and edible oils at subsidised rates. Complementary welfare programmes such as the
Nutritious Meal Scheme, the Chief Minister’s Breakfast Scheme, and the Thayumanavar
Programme further reinforce nutritional security and social equity.
2.11.3 Tamil Nadu has achieved a notable moderation in headline inflation alongside robust GSDP
growth of 11.19% in 2024-25. Retail inflation has followed a clear downward trajectory,
reaching 2.4% in 2025-26, driven by a significant decline in food prices, especially cereal,
vegetables and pulses. Recent GST rate reductions and targeted supply-side interventions
contributed to retail inflation falling to a record low in December 2025. Consequently, CPI
inflation in the state is projected to remain within the range of 2–3% during 2025–26,
underscoring the effectiveness of Tamil Nadu’s multi-dimensional approach to economic
stability and social protection.

63

Chapter 3

Employment: Inclusion and Diversification
3.1 Introduction
3.1.1

Broad-basing of education and employment in modern sectors, has been a key plank of the
state’s path of social justice. In particular, the state’s development policies have focused
on transitioning its workforce from traditional to modern sectors through structural
transformation of the economy. This effort in restructuring of the economy has been
supported by a set of pioneering interventions in education. From ensuring universal
enrolment in primary education to now having the highest share of youth in tertiary
education in the country1, the state has opened up a range of economic opportunities for
youth from marginal socio-economic backgrounds. Further, this investments in education
have led to the state having the highest share of graduates in its workforce 2. Emphasis
on technical and medical education has been particularly high with one of the largest
number of students enrolled in engineering and polytechnic courses3.

3.1.2

Recovering from the pandemic, over the last four years, the state has initiated a set
of measures that have sought to improve both demand for employment and supply
of skilled workers. In particular, the state has sought to increase enrolment of girls in
higher education as well as boys from marginalized social backgrounds through financial
incentives and other measures. Nevertheless, in a macro-economic regime premised
on inter-state competition to attract private investments and open to global economic
fluctuations, ensuring ‘decent’ employment continues to be a challenge. This chapter maps
the trends in employment across the rural-urban divide, gender and caste groups since
2021. It also highlights the pioneering initiatives undertaken by the state government to
improve the learning outcomes of formal education as well as establish a closer linkage
between supply and demand for skills.

3.1.3

The chapter is based on a comparative perspective, using the respective metrics for
the other south Indian states, Gujarat and Maharashtra. The non-south Indian states
are chosen because of their growth dynamism. Given that a substantial share of the
state’s youth pursues higher secondary and tertiary education, we primarily use the 2059 age group as constituting the state’s labour force. The chapter examines both the

1 https://www.indiabudget.gov.in/economicsurvey/doc/stat/tab8.3.pdf accessed on 10/01/2026.
2 Kalaiyarasan, A., & Vijayabaskar, M. (2021). The Dravidian model: Interpreting the political economy of Tamil Nadu.
Cambridge University Press.

3 State Planning Commission. (2017). Tamil Nadu Human Development Report. Government of Tamil Nadu.
https://spc.tn.gov.in/hdr-reports-v2/

64

quantitative and qualitative aspects of changes in the labour market with attention to
rural-urban differences as well as differences in terms of gender and social groups. Based
on this, the chapter identifies a set of strategies to further strengthen and broad-base
employment creation. To begin with, the chapter focuses on the quantitative aspect by
looking at the working age population of the state that are entering into the workforce,
using the changes in worker population ratios and labour force participation rates since
2021-22.

3.2 Worker Population Ratio (WPR) and Labour Force
Participation Rate (LFPR)
3.2.1

Overall, the worker-population ratio, i.e., the share of employed workers in the total
population has increased between 2021-22 and 2023-24 from 67.9 to 69.6 indicating a
greater share of working age population employed in the state. This share is higher
than the all-India average though the relative position of the state has fallen among the
states under comparison (Figure 3.1).

3.2.2 The state’s labour force participation rate (i.e., percentage of persons in the labour force
in the population) too has increased during this period and is higher than the all-India
average. The gap between LFPR and WPR worker population ratio has reduced during
this period, indicating that a greater share of those seeking work have been employed
by 2023-24 (Figure 3.1).
3.2.3 There has also been a decline in the unemployment rate (UR-percentage of persons
unemployed among persons in the labour force) from 5.1% to 3.6 % as well. The UR is
more than the all-India average. However, this UR needs to be understood in the Indian
context4. Given the large-scale distress induced low-quality employment in the country,
relatively higher UR is also a reflection of the ability of the labour force to wait for better
employment opportunities. This can be attributed to relatively better household incomes
in the state on the one hand and better social protection measures on the other.

4 Ghatak, M., Jha, M., & Singh, J. (2024, January). Quantity vs Quality: Long-term Trends in Job Creation in the Indian Labour
Market. In The India Forum.

66

3.2.4 Those not seeking employment due to attendance in educational institutions (NILF_E)
has increased while those not seeking work due to domestic responsibilities (NILF_D) has
fallen marginally (Table A1). However, this share continues to be high at 22%, particularly
among women, highlighting the need for policies that increase the entry of women
into the workforce. The chapter examines the gender-based differences across these
parameters next, specifically among the younger cohort (in the age group 15-29).

3.3 Gender Differences in WPR and LFPR
3.3.1

The share of women in the 15-29 age group who report to be out of the labour force due
to being enrolled in education has increased from 35.3% to 37.8 from 2021-22 to 202324 (Table A2). This indicates that a larger share of young women entering the labour
market are better skilled. This is also true of young men. The challenge for employment
therefore lies in ensuring that jobs generated can take advantage of the investments
made by the state and households in education, and ensure quality employment. Having
said that, it is also important to note that 36.3% of girls drop out of the workforce due
to domestic responsibilities in this age group. Early marriage and other factors appear to
play a role in adolescent girls dropping out of school but not willing to enter the labour
market. It is in this regard that financial incentives offered through the Pudhumai Penn
scheme coupled with sensitisation campaigns have an important role.

3.4 Rural-Urban Differences in Work Participation
3.4.1

Except for Andhra Pradesh and Telangana, the WPR and LFPR for rural Tamil Nadu
is higher among all states compared and has also increased since 2021-22 (Table 3.1).
However, while the WPR has stagnated during this period for urban Tamil Nadu, the LFPR
has fallen marginally (Figure 3.2). Further, the share of urban workers not in workforce
on account of domestic work is almost double that of rural workers. Such rural-urban
differences may partly be due to expansion of employment opportunities for rural
workers on account of spread of non-farm employment opportunities whereas higher
incomes in urban households may allow women and their households to adopt social
norms against women’s economic independence and mobility outside domestic spaces.

3.5 Youth Neither in Employment nor in Education or
Training
3.5.1

A major challenge for India in reaping the demographic dividend is finding quality
employment for its youth. This challenge is especially sharp for the advanced states like
Tamil Nadu and Kerala where the share of youth completing higher education is much
higher (All-India Survey on Higher Education 2021-22). This section maps the changes in
access to employment for youth in the state (Figure 3.2).

3.5.2 The share of youth in the age group of 20-29 who are neither in employment nor in
education or training (NEET) in Tamil Nadu declined from 42.7% in 2021-22 to 37.3% in
2023-24. Many young women in this age group are in the NEET category as they stay
out of the labour force to attend domestic duties. For young women the share declined
from 64.9% to 60.4% between 2021-22 and 2023-24. For men the share of those in NEET
declined sharply from 19.7% to 11.9% in the same period. Gender norms preferring early
marriage and attending to domestic responsibilities may cause this trend and therefore,
government initiatives like Pudhumai Penn and other schemes is more relevant than
ever in this context.

3.6 Quality of Employment
3.6.1

The quality of employment generated is particularly important for a state like Tamil Nadu
which has a large share of labour market entrants with better educational attainments.
This section portrays some dimensions of quality of employment based on secondary
sources. The PLFS classifies employment into ‘self-employment’ and waged work. Within
waged employment, the PLFS makes a distinction between those who are employed as
‘casual labour’ and those who are in ‘regular employment’. ‘Regular’ employment is an
indicator of better employment security and regularity of income compared to being
in casual work. In case of the self-employed, it has been pointed out that often such
employment is distress induced and not necessarily a sign of entrepreneurialism.

70

3.6.2 In terms of quality of employment, Tamil Nadu ranks second after Kerala. The state
has the second highest share of workers (37.2%) in salaried employment in the 20-59
age group (Figure 3.4). This share has increased considerably from 32.8% in 2021-22
suggesting an expansion of better-quality employment opportunities during this period.
This increase implies a corresponding fall in shares of casual work and self-employment.
There has been a slight fall in the share of those in casual employment and a larger fall
in the share of those in self-employment.
3.6.3 A gender-wise break up indicates that this trend is true of both male and female workers
(Figure 3.4). The state has the highest share of male workers in regular employment and
second highest share of women workers in regular employment after Kerala, with the
share of both male and female workers increasing in the two-year period. The fact that
the state has 12% more female workers in regular employment compared to the allIndia average is particularly noteworthy. However, the gender differences within Tamil
Nadu requires attention. While close to 42% of male workers are in regular employment
according to the latest data, only close to 30% of women workers are in this category.

3.6.4 The state, along with Kerala, however, has a higher share of those in casual employment
compared to the all-India average, and a lower share of workers in self-employment. The
decline in agricultural employment in both states is an important factor that explains the
decline in share of self-employment. According to the Annual Survey of Unincorporated
Sector Enterprises (ASUSE) of 2023-24, nearly 23 % of unincorporated enterprises in the
state relied on hired labour for functioning, indicating economic dynamism. This was the
second highest share among the states being compared, with the all-India average being
less than 14%.
3.6.5 While there is a case for reducing the share of those in casual work, especially for women
workers, the state has sought to address this issue by ensuring livelihood security through
a range of welfare schemes such as universal PDS, effective MGNREGA implementation
and the Kalaignar Mahalir Urimai Thittam (KMUT).
3.6.6 The quality of employment is also shaped by the degree of structural transformation.
Ensuring structural transformation to move workers from their traditional caste-ascribed
roles to modern occupations has been fundamental to the state’s vision of social and
economic justice.

3.7 Structural Transformation and Employment
3.7.1

As the table 3.3 indicates, with a quarter of its workforce in agriculture in 2023-24, the
state has the highest share of workers outside agriculture after Kerala.

3.7.2

In fact, this share has dropped from close to 28% in 2021-22. This share is much lower than
the all-India average and that of other fast-growing states suggesting a more successful
structural transformation in the state. This fall in share of agricultural employment has
been backed by robust manufacturing employment. The state has the second highest
share of its workers in manufacturing employment with close to 17% after Gujarat. There
has however been a fall in manufacturing employment share compared to 2021-22 by
about one percentage point. While this decline is in line with trends observed in most
states with a good manufacturing base, the state’s higher exposure to global trade
fluctuations is likely to explain this decline. The post-pandemic shifts in trade policies
globally and logistic frictions in movement of goods may have contributed to this.

3.7.3

The increase in share of workers in more value-adding services such as in information
and communication services and education also augurs well for the state and suggestive
of dynamic diversification. The small share of workers in these sectors however suggest
that while expanding the share of employment in these sectors is important, it is also
important to pay attention to the construction sector which is the largest employer
outside agriculture along with manufacturing in the state. Given the informal nature of
most construction work, there is a strong case to strengthen the construction welfare
board, a pioneering initiative of the state government. Trade, transport and storage are
other major sources of employment.

3.7.4

It is important to note that, unlike the all-India pattern where the service sector
contributes the most to GDP, but agriculture employs the largest share of workers, Tamil
Nadu shows a different trend. In the state, the service sector has the largest share in
both GDP and employment. In 2023–24, the service sector accounted for around 40 % of
total employment, followed by the industry sector at 32 % and the agricultural sector at
25 %.

3.7.5

Structural gap is defined as the difference between the share of the agriculture sector
in employment and GDP5. The argument is that the structural gap widens at the initial
stages of the structural transformation as the agriculture sector’s share in GDP declines
faster than its share in employment. The economy eventually reaches a turning point
where the structural gap in the economy starts to decline. The Tamil Nadu economy
has been experiencing a decline in the structural gap since 2004-05 (Mandela and Niyati
20206). The trend continued during the period between 2021-22 and 2023-24 as well
even as it widened in most states. As stated earlier, the agriculture sector’s share in

73

employment was about 28% and in NSDP was about 11% in 202%1-22. Therefore, the
structural gap in the economy was 17% in 2021-22, which declined to 15% in 2023-24
(Table 3.4).

3.8.2 The share has however fallen from more than 42% in 2021-22. In fact, at the all-India level,
the share of workforce in agriculture has marginally increased and so it has in several
states under comparison. This phenomenon of increased share of workers in agriculture
has led to scholars pointing to a process of reversal of structural transformation. The
fact that Tamil Nadu has bucked the all-India trend is significant.
3.8.3 At the household level, rural Tamil Nadu is highly diversified. According to the National
Sample Survey report titled ‘Situation Assessment of Agricultural Households and Land
and Livestock Holdings of Households in Rural India, 2019’ conducted during July 2018June 2019, the state was home to an estimated 97,69,400 rural households, out of which
25,82,500 (26.4%) are agricultural households and 71,86,900 (73.6%) are non-agricultural
households. An agricultural household is defined as a household producing agricultural
output of a value more than Rs. 4,000 per annum and having at least one member
reporting to be self-employed in agriculture either in the principal status or in subsidiary
status during last 365 days. The agricultural activities include horticulture and livestock
as well.
3.8.4 Even by such a generous definition, only around one in four households in rural Tamil
Nadu are agricultural households, with the remaining three fourths of them deriving
income primarily from non-agricultural activity. The dependence on agriculture is much
lower compared to the all -India average of 50% of all rural households being agricultural
households. However, there are differences across social groups.
3.8.5 SC households account for almost 30% of rural households in Tamil Nadu but constitute
only around 20% of agricultural households. On the other hand, around 68% of rural
75

households are OBC households, but account for around 78% of agricultural households.
This shows that a slightly larger share of rural OBC households is engaged in selfcultivation. However, both sets of households have diversified considerably out of
agriculture. Only 18% of SC households and 30.6% of OBC households are found to be
agricultural households, indicating a highly diversified rural economy. Nevertheless, the
continued dependence of a significant share of rural population on agriculture means that
policy efforts need to be further directed towards improving productivity in agriculture
and ensuring better returns, apart from ensuring diversification out of agriculture.
3.8.6 Outside agriculture, while the share of workers in construction in rural Tamil Nadu has
increased, of note, is the increase in share of workers in manufacturing employment by
two percentage points from 11.4% to 13.5% during the two years. It is especially significant
given the slight decline in overall manufacturing employment in the state and hence,
suggests a ‘ruralisation’ of manufacturing employment. Policy efforts in this direction are
worth mentioning in this regard (Madhavan 2025).7 Though the state has a long history of
attempting to set up industries in backward regions, it has particularly met with success
in recent years. The government has actively sought to nudge and incentivize investors
to set up factories in industrially backward districts like Thoothukudi, Tirunelveli8,
Krishnagiri and Perambalur. The state has classified districts into three categories based
on the extent of industrial backwardness and provides more incentives and concessions
for firms willing to move to the most backward districts.
3.8.7 Importantly, most factories moving to backward districts are in labour intensive sectors
like footwear, electronics and automobile factories and employ a substantial share
of women employees. Madhavan points out that the entry of women into modern
employment has incentivized households in such regions to invest more in their girl
children’s education and postpone the year of marriage.
3.8.8 Other than manufacturing, trade, transportation and storage are other major sources of
rural employment. The relatively higher share of those employed in the education sector
in rural Tamil Nadu is also a notable trend.

3.9 Gender and Sectoral Diversification
3.9.1

In 2023-24, while the share of male workers in agriculture is only 18%, the share of female

workers is close to 37% (Table 3.6). However, the latter share is much lower than the
all-India average of 63% and lower than all states except Kerala. The same holds good
for male workers as well. The share of female workers in construction sector in Tamil
Nadu is the highest among all states and accounts for a much larger share than any of
them. While the all-India average for women workers in construction is less than 4 %,
more than 16% of women workers in the state are in construction. This observation has
implications for ensuring social security of women workers in this sector.
3.9.2 With regard to manufacturing, the sector accounts for a similar share as that of construction
in 2023-24 (more than 16%). According to the latest Annual Survey of Industries (ASI) data,
40.3% of all directly employed women in the Indian organized manufacturing sector in
2023-24 are employed in Tamil Nadu. The second largest employer of women, Karnataka,
has less than 14% of women workers, indicating Tamil Nadu’s success in absorbing women
in modern manufacturing employment. Trade accounts for the next largest share of
women workers, but the share of women workers in education at 7% is relatively high.
In fact, after Kerala, TN has the largest share of women workers in the education sector.
Finally, though not high in terms of absolute share, a relatively larger proportion of
women workers are employed in information and communication services compared to
most states.

3.10 Notable Interventions for Inclusive Employment
3.10.1 The state’s effective and more broad-based social welfare system along with robust
public health care system ensures a higher degree of real income for labour than most
states. In addition, the state government has taken significant measures to attract
employment generating investments especially in industrially backward areas as stated
earlier. Apart from incentives for labour-intensive manufacturing, the government has
also launched neo-TIDEL parks which are mini-IT parks in tier 2 locations across the
state to attract investments in high end services and hence decentralise high quality
employment creation. Apart from such demand boosting measures, the state has also
initiated a set of interventions to improve the quality of skill endowments of youth
entering into the labour market. The Pudhumai Penn and Thamizh Pudhalvan schemes
are directed towards improving the secondary to tertiary education transition rate
for male and female students completing school education in government schools and
from government aided schools in rural Tamil Nadu. The government has also worked in
partnership with Tata Technologies to upgrade 71 industrial training institutes (ITIs) into
centres for training in industry 4.0 technologies.
3.10.2 The Naan Mudhalvan Scheme, launched by the Government of Tamil Nadu in 2022, is a
flagship and pioneering initiative aimed at equipping students with essential, industryaligned skills to address the growing gap between academic learning and labour-market
requirements. Conceived as part of the state’s long-term human capital development
strategy, the scheme seeks to prepare a workforce capable of responding to rapid
technological advancements and globalisation. As of 2025, the scheme has reached over
2.7 million students, making it one of India’s most expansive skill development efforts.
78

Along with imparting technical skills in cutting edge areas such as robotics, data analytics,
structural design and machine learning, internet of things, the programme emphasises
soft skills such as communication in English which are found to be particularly lacking
among first generation learners.
3.10.3 An evaluation of the scheme by the State Planning Commission, Government of Tamil
Nadu, in engineering colleges and polytechnics found that it has had a huge impact on
students from backward areas where they hardly have any opportunity to interact with
industry partners. Further, given the fast-changing frontiers of several technologies,
faculty members in these institutions find it hard to keep themselves updated. The
scheme, importantly, has a ‘Training of Trainers’ component that seeks to equip faculty
members with such skill sets so that they can impart such skills to students over time.
Faculty members as well as employers report that the scheme has indeed been very
helpful. Such efforts need to be strengthened further to sustain and increase the pace
of creation of quality employment for youth from marginalised sections, especially for
young women in the years to come.

3.11 Conclusions
3.11.1

Some key policy measures are highlighted below, which is also in line with the State
Planning Commission’s Draft Tamil Nadu Employment Policy (2023)9. First, regionally
balanced employment creation requires focused infrastructure-related interventions.
Predictable access to water and power, along with improved physical and digital
connectivity, is essential, particularly in the backward districts of the State. Reducing
inter-district disparities in access to public agencies and government incentives is also
necessary.

3.11.2 Second, investment promotion needs to be explicitly linked to inclusive employment
creation. Incentivisation frameworks can incorporate employment generation potential
by accounting for direct and indirect employment, the nature of the workforce likely
to be employed, and the scope for future value addition. Measures to promote socially
inclusive employment include targeted incentives such as time-bound wage subsidies
for first-generation graduates, particularly women, entering white-collar employment.
Beneficiaries of the Pudhumai Penn and Tamizh Pudhalvan schemes can be prioritised,
with the Naan Mudhalvan scheme aligned to support this objective.
9 https://spc.tn.gov.in/policy/draft-tamil-nadu-employment-policy-2032/ accessed on 11/1/2026.

79

3.11.3 Third, improving real wages of workers requires interventions beyond nominal wage
growth. In sectors with limited wage progression, high urban living costs reduce labour
mobility, particularly among youth and women. Expansion of public housing and hostel
facilities, including the Thozhi Hostel Scheme, can support this objective. Fourth,
retention of women in paid employment depends on the availability of affordable and
quality childcare services. Investments are required in childcare for infants and toddlers
as well as after-school care for children above the age of three. Such facilities should be
accessible within or near schools and residential neighbourhoods to support continued
workforce participation by women. Finally, enhancing human competencies remains
central to improving labour market outcomes. The State’s educated workforce can yield
higher returns through improvements in the quality of formal education.

80

Chapter 4

Industrial Policy and Development
4.1 Introduction
4.1.1

Tamil Nadu has emerged as a global manufacturing hub within India. State policies
have played a significant role in transforming it into a manufacturing powerhouse. The
evolution of its industrial policy is notable for its consistency and stability, even across
changes in political regimes. Until 1991, industrial policy in India was primarily managed
by the Union Government, with states playing a minimal role. However, post-1992, many
states, including Tamil Nadu, began forming their own investment promotion agencies.
Tamil Nadu has had three industrial policies since 1992, introduced in 2008, 2014, and 2021,
respectively. These policies have remained consistent and non-contradictory, providing
stability to investors. For instance, the state’s ability to attract large global investments
like Apple iPhone suppliers - Foxconn, among others under the current political leadership
demonstrates such consistency.

4.1.2

The current state’s investment policy targets three main sectors: companies entering
Tamil Nadu, South Asian companies looking to invest after disruptions - ‘China plus one
strategy’ in global value chains, and companies already invested in the state. The recent
policy note (2025) reflects Tamil Nadu’s recalibration to align with its global position,
focusing on both capital-intensive and employment-intensive industries.1 Tamil Nadu’s
industrial policy thus prioritizes balanced growth and job generation as core objectives.
It also demonstrates a consistent and stable approach, focusing on sector-specific
inventions in electronics hardware manufacturing, R&D, EVs, semiconductors, and
other advanced electronics, all of which play a key role in deepening the manufacturing
ecosystem in the state. Notwithstanding the pressure of competitive subsidies and tax
breaks to attract private investment among states, Tamil Nadu has leveraged its unique
advantages of its governance, infrastructure and geography.

4.1.3

Tamil Nadu was one of the few states that established its own agency to negotiate
with private investors, both domestic and foreign, in the early 1990s as India embraced
economic liberalization. Guidance—originally known as the Tamil Nadu Industrial Guidance
and Export Promotion Bureau—was established in 1992 as a parastatal nodal agency.
The agency operates under the Department of Industries, Investment Promotion, and
Commerce (DIIPC) and has an official mandate to promote ease of doing business within
the state and to attract investment. It was further empowered under the Tamil Nadu
Business Facilitation Act 2018, provides support to prospective investors by operating
and managing the single-window portal for clearances and approvals. As a result, the
state’s improved Ease of doing business ranking, from 14th(in 2019) to 3rd (in 2022),
indicates positive changes based on industry feedback. In terms of both social and

1 See for the details: ind_e_pn_2025_26.pdf

81

physical infrastructure, the state has been leading in most of these indicators.2 The
role of human capital in driving both economic growth and entrepreneurship is well
established.3 The state has come to be recognized for its ability to generate one of the
largest pools of technically skilled labour in the country. The NCAER index ranks the state
at the top in this regard.4 The state’s early creation of higher education infrastructure,
ensuring higher and more broad-based enrolment, with a particular focus in technical
education, drove this process.
4.1.4

Tamil Nadu stands apart in its physical infrastructure, as well. The state had achieved
100% rural electrification by the 1970s, while Gujarat and Maharashtra achieved a similar
feat only in the 1990s. As per the NCAER index, it has the narrowest gap between supply
and demand for power and also ranks third in the IT readiness index.5 The state has the
fourth largest (after Maharashtra, Gujarat and Rajasthan) installed capacity of power with
44,538.61 MW in November 2025. Tamil Nadu’s well-established road network has a road
density that is 2.5 times that of all-India. Following the process of ‘responsibilisation’ of
state governments regarding resource mobilization after economic reforms of the early
1990s, the state has managed to sustain its productive dynamism through mobilization
of foreign capital as well.

4.1.5

According to Tamil Nadu Industrial Guidance & Export Promotion Bureau, the FDI share
of the state has seen consistent growth during 1990s and 2000 approximating to 8.56%
and ranking third next only to Maharashtra and Delhi in 2021. In the 2024-25 financial year
alone, Tamil Nadu has attracted $3,861 Million of FDI. From October 2019 to December
2024, there was a cumulative FDI equity flow of USD 13.84 billion towards Tamil Nadu,
and it holds 5% of the total FDI in India. It has successfully managed to attract several
multinational firms to set up operations in the state and also attracted capital into urban
infrastructure development through promotion of public private partnerships.6

4.1.6

Particularly noteworthy is the Sriperumbudur case – an industrial cluster close to Chennai,
which has been nationally recognized as a successful instance of state policies enabling
the attraction of foreign investments. Leading global firms specializing in production of
hardware and electronic goods like Nokia, Samsung, Motorola, Dell and Foxconn have
set up productive capacities in the region over the last 15 years responding to a slew
of government incentives. This led to the growth of an electronic hardware segment
virtually from scratch in the state.

3 Marvel, M. R., Davis, J. L., & Sproul, C. R. (2016). Human capital and entrepreneurship research: A critical review and future

4 The National Council of Applied Economic Research (NCAER) has been publishing an investment potential index for states

4.1.7

This is clearly a case of a successfully ‘induced’ cluster that has witnessed remarkable
growth despite the recent closures of Nokia followed by Foxconn and a bunch of
component suppliers. Though it had a strong auto production base even in the 1990s,
Chennai has emerged as India’s leading automobile, now EVs, auto components exporter
in India and is often referred to as ‘Detroit of India’. The region accounts for 35% of
the auto component production in India and is home to the world’s leading automobile
producers like Hyundai, Ford, Daimler Benz, Yamaha, BMW and Mitsubishi.7 Hyundai has
made Chennai the manufacturing and export hub for its small cars and is Hyundai’s
largest base outside South Korea. The state now exports high value electronics and
engineering goods, alongside goods such as readymade garments, handloom products,
and leather. It stands among the top three states, along with Gujarat and Maharashtra,
together accounting for more than half of India’s merchandise exports. Tamil Nadu alone
recorded exports of USD 52.1 billion in 2025, giving it a 13.34% national share. The latest
data from the Engineering Export Promotion Council of India (EEPC India) shows that
Tamil Nadu’s engineering exports reached USD 18.1 billion in FY 2024–25.

4.1.8

Industrial growth is not only broad-based sectoral, but also spatially more evenly
distributed and socially inclusive. This becomes evident when we look at spread of
factories per given population. As per the economic census of 2015-16, in the size category
of 20-99 workers, the state has 34,599 units as compared to 16,236 units in Gujarat,
30,301 units in Maharashtra and 24,732 units in West Bengal. In the category of 100 and
above workers, Tamil Nadu has 3,219 units while there are only 2,675 units in Gujarat

7 https://www.business-standard.com/article/economy-policy/tamil-nadu-eyes-major-investments-in-state-s-auto-auto-components-industry-119010400335_1.html

83

and 4,678 units in Maharashtra. If we standardize this into enterprise (size of 100 and
above) per population, the state has an enterprise per 22,684 persons while it is 24,021 in
Maharashtra, 22,927 in Gujarat and 51,031 in West Bengal.
4.1.9

Given the size of India in terms of population and of late economic growth, sub-national
states proximate the size of many nations in both global south and north. In fact, in
purchasing power parity terms, the size of Tamil Nadu’s economy is equivalent to that
of the UAE and higher than many Asian economies such as Vietnam and Lao PDR.8
Tamil Nadu has been one of the fastest growing states in the post-reforms period
backed by high growth in both manufacturing and the services sector9. The state has
managed to achieve such sustained economic growth with relatively fair distribution
and improvements in health, education, and nutrition compared to many other states in
India.

4.1.10 The state has witnessed sustained economic growth and has clocked higher growth
than all India average in the post-reform period. In the 1960s, Tamil Nadu was one of
the poorest states in the country going by poverty levels (Dreze and Sen 2013) though it
had relatively higher per capita income. The decadal average per capita income for the
state in the 1960s was Rs. 10,303 (at 2004-05 price) while the corresponding figures for
Maharashtra, Gujarat and West Bengal were Rs. 11,294, Rs.10,303 and Rs. 9,133 respectively.
Income for Bihar was Rs. 5,108. By 2010s (decadal average), the average per capita income
in Tamil Nadu has gone up to Rs. 54,093 while the corresponding figures for Maharashtra,
Gujarat and West Bengal are Rs.60,454, Rs. 54,957 and Rs. 32,334 respectively. The
working paper “Relative Economic Performance of Indian States: 1960–61 to 2023–24”,
published by the Economic Advisory Council to the Prime Minister attributes this rapid
rise to economic liberalisation. Bihar’s per capita income for the corresponding year was
Rs.12,802. In other words, while Tamil Nadu had a per capita income twice (203%) that of
Bihar in the 1960s, it has gone up to four times (423%) that of Bihar in 2010s. The gap
has further widened up to five times in 2024-25 and the per capita income of Tamil Nadu
(at 2011 prices) is Rs. 1,97,747 and Rs. 36,342 for Bihar. Such remarkable increase in income
is also borne out with a comparison with the all-India average. If we compare with all
India average, the state had an income marginally (by 12%) higher than that of India’s per
capita income (Rs. 9,129) in the 1960s but has gone up to 148% of all India average (Rs.
32,297) in 2010s and it further rose to 178% in 2024-25. In terms of position, the state
stood at fourth place among 12 large states in the 1960s, it has steadily improved its
position from fourth to third in 1990 and to second in 2014-15 and further down to 4th
in 2024-25.

8 https://statisticstimes.com/economy/comparing-indian-states-and-countries-by-gdp.php
9 Kalaiyarasan, A. (2014). A comparison of developmental outcomes in Gujarat and Tamil Nadu. Economic and Political Weekly,
49(15), 36–45.

4.1.11 However, as the Handbook of Statistics on Indian States 2024–25 released by the RBI
suggests, the state stands as the second largest economy after Maharashtra. Its per
capita income has risen steadily across the consecutive three years, recording highest
growth of 13.59% in 2022–23, 13.83% in 2023–24, and 15.41% in 2024-25. Manufacturing is
main driver of growth. The sector grew at 12.59% in 2023-24 and 14.74% in 2024-25. The
performance of the manufacturing exceeds that of the national average consistently for
the last 10 years except in 2020-21 and 2021-22.

4.1.12 What is also significant is the nature of economic growth. The increase in GSDP has been
accompanied by structural transformation of a degree that is higher than any other state
in the country. Not only does the state have the second-lowest share of its population
dependent on agriculture, but agriculture also contributes less than 8% to the state’s
income, which is less than half of the all-India average (GoTN 2025). Importantly, it has
one of the largest shares of GVA in manufacturing and has built a vibrant manufacturing
base particularly in labor-intensive sectors like textiles, garments, leather goods and
automobile manufacturing. The manufacturing share in the state GDP has been rising
from 19.9% in 2014-15 to 24.5% in 2024-25 when the national average stagnating around
17.2 %.

4.2 Organised sector performance
4.2.1

Tamil Nadu has become a global manufacturing hub within India. Estimates show that
the share of manufacturing in Tamil Nadu’s Gross State Value Addition (GSVA) stands at
over 24 % – one of the highest in the country. As per the Annual Survey Industries (ASI)
2023-24, Tamil Nadu has highest (15.4 %) share of registered factories in India. In terms
of large enterprises with size of 1000 and above, the state has 1,735 units accounting
for 28% of India’s factories in this size cohort. The corresponding figures for Gujarat
and Maharashtra are 679 and 742 accounting for just 11% and 12% of total respectively.
In the size category 5,000 and above, the state has 311 units accounting for 35% of total
units in India while Gujarat and Maharashtra have 66 units accounting for 7.3% of the
total enterprise in the country. There has been a wave of new investments in building
large factories such as Foxconn plant which employs about 18,000 workers and a Tata
plant employing about 40,000 workers. The state also has the highest (15.2%) share of
persons engaged in factory operations. In India’ total manufacturing GVA, the state
stands (10.3%) third after Maharashtra (16%) and Gujarat (14%) despite having the largest
share of its labour in manufacturing indicating the relatively higher labour intensity of its
manufacturing base.10

10 Kapoor, R. (2015). Creating jobs in India’s organised manufacturing sector. Indian Journal of Labour Economics, 58(3),
349–375.

86

4.2.2 According to Amirapu and Subramanian (2015), Tamil Nadu has reached as high as 18% of
Manufacturing GDP second only to Gujarat’s 22%. However, it had the highest share of
organized factory employment to total employment in the country. No major state has
even crossed this figure in the last 30 years. The dynamism of the state’s manufacturing
is also evident when we look at the unorganized sector. Of the total 33 lakhs registered
units in India, Tamil Nadu also has about 5.4 lakhs units. Though Gujarat has a higher
manufacturing base in terms of GVA and fixed capital, it is not as broad based as Tamil
Nadu. As Nagaraj and Pandey (2013) show, export-oriented petroleum refining alone
accounts for about a quarter of gross value added in registered manufacturing in
Gujarat state which may not offer enough by way of employment. On the other hand,
labour intensive sectors such as automobile (18%), textiles (11%) and food products (9%),
constitute more than 40% of the output in the factory sector in Tamil Nadu.

4.2.3 In terms of sub-sectors, as shown in the table 4.2, Tamil Nadu continues to top in most
labour-intensive industries in the country. It accounts for about one-third of national
gross value added in wearing apparel, followed by 29% in leather and related products.
The state also occupies the second position in several major manufacturing segments,
including textiles accounting for 18.4%, other transport equipment (17.8%), computer,
electronic and optical products (16.6 %), and fabricated metal products excluding
machinery and equipment (13.4%).
4.2.4 Tamil Nadu ranks third in several key industries, notably motor vehicles, trailers and
semi-trailers (20.9%), other manufacturing (16.4%), machinery and equipment (15.9%),
rubber and plastics products (12.7%), and paper and paper products (10.3%). The
state stands fourth in seven key sectors including electrical equipment, non-metallic
minerals, and chemicals. These trends show the state’s diversified industrial base and
its strong presence across both consumer goods and intermediate goods industries. This
diversified structure also contributes to strengthening local supply chains and enhances
the resilience of Tamil Nadu’s vibrant manufacturing ecosystem.

4.2.5 As shown in the figure 4.4, the pattern of manufacturing growth in Tamil Nadu broadbased and more balanced relative to other industrially advanced states such as Gujarat
and Maharashtra. With 2014–15 as the base year, real gross value added (GVA) in Tamil
Nadu shows a strong and sustained increase, rising more rapidly than in Gujarat and
Maharashtra over most of the period. Employment growth, while positive, has been more
moderate, whereas real emoluments have increased at a faster pace, particularly after
2018–19, indicating rising real earnings per worker. Unlike Gujarat, where fixed capital
growth outpaces GVA for much of the period, Tamil Nadu exhibits a pattern in which
GVA growth keeps pace with, and in later years slightly exceeds, the growth of fixed
capital. This suggests relatively higher capital productivity in Tamil Nadu and a stronger
translation of investment into value addition. Maharashtra, by contrast, shows weaker
and more volatile growth across GVA, employment, and fixed capital, especially around
the COVID years, with a slower recovery thereafter. Tamil Nadu’s manufacturing growth
thus combines productivity and rising real emoluments, showing a relatively inclusive
manufacturing growth process.

90

4.3 Wages and Wage Share
4.3.1

The wage share reflects how much of national value added accrues to labour as opposed
to capital. The analysing wage share also reflects the classic concern -whether the return
to owning capital exceeds the returns to labour. Similarly, the effect of technology is not
neutral between capital and labour. For instance, if productivity gains do not translate
into higher wages, this provides insight into the ways in which power dynamics or the
nature of technology in the economy translate into differing outcomes, often favouring
capital.

4.3.2 The wage share in national income has been falling across the world due to increases in
capital intensity as well as a policy regime that privileges labour market flexibility (OECD
and ILO11 2015). Going by the wage share in organised manufacturing in the state, Tamil
Nadu is no exception (see Figure 4.6). In relative terms, however, the state has a higher
share of wages in gross value added (GVA) in the factory sector than most states in India.
The average wage share in GVA for the period 2014-24 is 15% in the state which is about
twice that of Gujarat (8%) and Maharashtra (9%). The share of total emoluments in GVA
too is the highest. For instance, the share of emoluments in GVA for the state is 30%
while corresponding figures for Gujarat and Maharashtra are 18% and 24% respectively.
This higher share accruing to labour in the state can be attributed to two possible factors.
First, wage levels are higher because of lower levels of contractualisation12 and the better
bargaining strength of labour. Or, it may have to do more with the changing sectoral
composition of manufacturing in the state, with a higher share of labour-intensive
sectors. While it is true that the state has a number of labour-intensive sectors like
textiles, garments and leather goods, the wage share is higher even in these sectors than
in Gujarat or Maharashtra suggesting relatively better bargaining strength in the formal
sector. Second, a larger share of workers in the factory sector are directly employed
against the prevailing trend of contractualisation in the country. Both of these factors
are changing today.

employment and economic growth (pp. 292–318). Cambridge University Press.

4.4 Industrial shifts
4.4.1

As shown in table 4.3, there has been a marked shift in industrial composition in the state
in the last decade (2010-24). The automobile–transport cluster has further gained
at the expense of the labor-intensive industries such as textiles. The GVA share of
motor vehicles, trailers and semi-trailers rises by 2.7 percentage points (from 15.8% to
18.5%), while other transport equipment increased by almost 4 percentagepoints and
rubber and plastics products by about 3 percent points. Taken together, these three
industries account for roughly a ten-percentage-point increase in GVA share, signaling
a further consolidation of Tamil Nadu as an auto and transport-equipment hub with an
expanding base of auto component manufacturing.

92

4.4.2 On the other hand, the share of GVA in textiles declined from 10.9% to 7.0%. This perhaps
indicates a shift in value added from upstream textile production to downstream
garmenting because the apparel has gained from 4.4% to 7.1% perhaps in export-oriented
apparel but losing relative weight in basic textiles. Food products, computers, electronic
and optical products, electrical equipment, other manufacturing, coke and refined
petroleum products and pharmaceuticals also registered moderate gains in GVA share.

4.4.3 However, there is a pronounced decline in several intermediate and metal-based industries
that are relatively labour intensive. The GVA share of fabricated metal products fell by 4.6
percentage points and machinery and equipment by 3.9 points, while other non-metallic
mineral products and basic metals also lost ground. These shifts signal a relative weakening
of traditional engineering, metal, and material industries that historically catered to both
93

domestic construction and capital-goods demand. Further, a few smaller industries also
experienced marginal contractions in GVA share, such as printing, beverages, and leather
and related products, while very small but fast-growing segments like furniture, wood
products, and tobacco products registered small increases from a low base. Labourintensive industries such as textiles, fabrication, and leather are giving way to the
manufacturing of computer and electronic goods, electrical equipment, and rubber and
plastics. Fabrication’s share has come down from about 8.3% in 2010 to 3.7% in 2023–24,
textiles from 10.8% to 7%, while computer and electronic goods have increased from 3.5%
to 4.6%. Overall, the pattern indicates a move away from basic metals, machinery, and
traditional materials toward transport equipment, pharmaceuticals, plastics, electronics,
and apparel, with auto–transport and electrical and electronics emerging as the central
drivers of manufacturing value added in Tamil Nadu.

4.5 In-house Training, Skills and R&D
4.5.1

Lack of adequate and up-to-date skilled industrial workers is now widely acknowledged
as one of the constraints in revival of manufacturing industries. This is often contrasted
with the abundance of IT skills for the outsourcing industry. However, as per ASI data,
Tamil Nadu has largest number factories – 2,801, providing in-house formal training to
their workers vis a vis 1,931 units in Maharashtra and 1,877 units in Gujarat.

4.5.2 The state has the largest number of factories with an R&D unit (906), slightly ahead of
Maharashtra (858) and Gujarat (821) and the R&D expenses incurred by firms in 202324 are rupees 1,143 crores while the corresponding figures for Maharashtra and Gujarat
rupees 2,243 and rupees 1,814 crores.

94

4.6 Public Sector
4.6.1

Despite the disinvestment drive across India, the state continues to have a significant
presence of public sector manufacturing units across heavy engineering, defence, and
power equipment. For instance, the state has the largest number of public sector
manufacturing units — 163 units, the same as its neighbour Kerala. These are followed by
Gujarat (139) and Maharashtra (98). The state also leads in public sector manufacturing
employment with 38,876 workers, compared with 20,321 in Gujarat and 26,224 in
Maharashtra. In terms of Gross Value Added, the state contributes 5.9% of the national
total, while Gujarat accounts for 2% and Maharashtra for 12%.

4.6.2 One of interesting features of public sector in the state is its industrial diversification
spanning about 25 industries including food products, machinery and equipment, furniture,
coke and refined petroleum, electrical equipment, and fabricated metal products while
in Gujarat it is largely concentrated on electricity, gas, and fabricated metal products. In
Maharashtra, a major public sector industry is electricity and gas, along with electrical
equipment manufacturing. In other words, the public sector presence is in 25 industries
while they are limited to 15 and 19 industries in Gujarat and Maharashtra, respectively.

95

4.7 The MSME
4.7.1

As argues earlier, Tamil Nadu’s industrial story rests on a simple idea: using state policy
to grow both territorially rooted MSMEs and mobile large firms, and then stitching
them together so that learning, jobs, and value creation spread across regions. This
requires two layers working in tandem. Horizontal interventions—such as physical and
social infrastructure, skills, and institutions—raise sets wider context for take-off.
Vertical interventions target specific sectors and clusters, recognising agglomeration
benefits and the role of place-based industrial estates and SEZs. The growth process
thus is socially embedded, implying better jobs and wider spatial distribution, rather
than a narrow focus on agglomeration in a few metropolitan centers. Industrialisation
in Tamil Nadu thus is relatively more widespread than in other states with similar levels
of industrialization such as Maharashtra or Gujarat. Besides the organised large-scale
manufacturing sector, the state is known for widely distributed clusters across regions,
each specialising in sectors undergirded by MSMEs. Tiruppur has textiles; Coimbatore has
pumps and paper; Sivakasi has the match and cracker industry; Karur has vehicle body
building; Tiruchengode has the rig industry; Chennai and Hosur host automobile and
electronics clusters; and Ambur and Ranipet have leather and leather-related clusters.
Apart from historical factors, as stated in the Introduction section ---the diffusion of
physical infrastructure such as electricity and transport connectivity, along with the
broad-basing of human capital in the 1980s, has made this process possible.

4.7.2

As per the Udyam Portal and the Udyam Assist Platform (UAP), from July 2020 to
November 2025, the total number of registered MSMEs in Tamil Nadu stands at 57,86,295,
covering both manufacturing and services. Of these, 14,39,196 firms are in manufacturing
(24.9%). In 2025–26, up to November, 7.8 lakh new MSMEs were registered under Udyam
and UAP. In 2024–25, the number of registered MSMEs was 16.7 lakh. Almost 66% of all
registered firms under Udyam or UAP fall under the category of Micro enterprises.

4.7.3

However, mere registration does not reveal the operational characteristics of these
enterprises, nor does it allow for differentiation between manufacturing and services.
Therefore, this paper relies on NSSO survey data, which are more robust in their
estimation, for the analysis. Also, since the factory sector (ASI) does not classify firms by
Micro, Small, and Medium categories, but the NSSO’s unorganised sector survey does, a
common definition was needed for both sectors in terms of the number of employees.
To maintain consistency, we use the Mehrotra & Giri (201913) formula to assign the
number of workers to each MSME category, enabling the integration of both organised
and unorganised sectors to estimate the full universe of MSMEs.

4.7.4

distinctly different from all India trend. The state has about 1.42 million enterprises,
of which 1.39 million are located in unorganised sectors. The state has relatively equal
proportion of both formal and informal sectors. While substantial enterprises are in
the unorganized sector, they are registered with some state’s regulatory authorities.
For instance, about 40% of total unorganized enterprises are registered in Tamil Nadu
as against 16% for all India. The organised factories too double of the share of national
average.

4.7.5

Among MSMEs, Micro units still constitute most establishments (96%). They account for
40.5% of employment and 10.2% of manufacturing GVA. Small and Medium units form
about 3.2% of enterprises but account for 17% of employment and about 13% of GVA.
Large enterprises across both the factory and unorganised segments account for less
than one per cent of establishments but employ about 42% of workers and generate 77%
of manufacturing GVA.

4.7.6

The size, employment, and GVA distribution of Tamil Nadu stands out from all India. At the
all-India level, Micro units constitute about 98% of establishments and account for 57%
of employment and about 12% of GVA. Large units make up just 0.2% of establishments
yet employ about 29% of workers and generate over three-fourths of manufacturing
GVA.

4.7.7

Within the organised (ASI) segment, this concentration is even sharper: large factories
make up about 23% of ASI units, but account for 80% of ASI employment and 88% of
ASI GVA, while micro and small factories are numerous but economically marginal. In the
unorganised (ASUSE) segment, micro enterprises dominate, accounting for almost all
units and close to 90% of employment and 80% of GVA.

4.7.8

In Tamil Nadu’s organised sector, large factories form 28% of ASI units but capture 82%
of ASI employment and 88 % of ASI GVA, again indicating a large-firm-centred pattern of
organised manufacturing. In the unorganised sector, micro enterprises dominate numbers
(97.7%), but their shares in employment (81.6%) and GVA (74.6%) are somewhat lower
than the all-India averages, with small and medium enterprises playing a significant role.
While Tamil Nadu shares the dualism of Indian manufacturing, it still enjoys employmentintensive large, organised factories alongside with substantial number of micro units in
the unorganised segment.

98

4.7.9 Hence, disaggregating the unorganised sector is key to understanding the state’s
industrial structure. In 2023-24, there were 1.39 million unorganised manufacturing units,
of which 5.44 lakh were registered14—39.1% of its unorganised enterprises. This is more
than twice the national share. Tamil Nadu alone contributes about 16.3% of all registered
unorganised manufacturing firms in India, the highest among states. A slightly larger
share of unorganised firms in the state is registered under GST (2.37%) compared to the
national average (1.62%), signalling a relatively faster movement toward formalisation.
As in organised manufacturing, the state stands out in unorganised manufacturing with
higher rates of registration and formalisation. While 39% of Tamil Nadu’s unorganised
enterprises are registered, the corresponding figures for Gujarat and Maharashtra are
26% and 19%.

4.8 Formalising Informal Enterprises
4.8.1

Formalisation of MSMEs is often seen as a necessary step for scaling up and securing
access to finance, markets, and infrastructure.15 For MSMEs intending to scale or raise
funds through debt or equity, formalisation is considered essential. It enables access
to the banking system, integrates firms into the GST ecosystem as both providers and
procurers of services, and allows faster growth. As shown in the table 4.8, Tamil Nadu
has been moving toward formalisation within the unorganised sector much faster than
India as a whole.

99

4.8.2 In 1999–2000, registered enterprises formed 10.8% of unorganised firms in Tamil Nadu,
almost identical to the all-India figure of 10.6%. From 2010–11 onwards, however, the
trajectories diverged: the share of registered units in Tamil Nadu rose from 22.1% (2010–
11) to 28.9% (2015–16), 35.5% (2022–23), and 39.1% (2023–24). This suggests a strong
process of “formalisation from below,” with a rising proportion of small, unincorporated
enterprises entering the regulatory and fiscal net.
4.8.3 Within the unorganised sector, hired-worker enterprises—distinct from own-account
enterprises—are considered more entrepreneurial. They employ non-family labour
and exhibit potential for scaling up. Tamil Nadu has the largest number of unorganised
hired-worker enterprises among all states and the highest employment in such units. It
hosts about 2.73 lakh hired-worker enterprises employing roughly 1.43 million workers.
Their gross value added (GVA) is substantial, though slightly below that of Gujarat and
Maharashtra. This indicates that the state has a large segment of employment-intensive
MSMEs—small but non-subsistence firms that could be targeted for upgrading and
eventual graduation into the organised sector.

100

4.9 Formalising Employment
4.9.1

Following the trend in the enterprises, the share of unorganised employment has been
declining in the state, as shown in the Table 4.1017. The share of unorganised employment
in manufacturing fell from about 65% in 2010–11 to 49.1% in 2023–24, while the
corresponding figures for all India are 74% to 64%. The share of unorganized employment
in Maharashtra has increased from 45.5% in 2010–11 to 56% in 2023-23, but it has declined
1.7 percent points in the very next year. Meanwhile, Gujarat shows a decline in unorganized
manufacturing employment18. However, at 54.7% of total manufacturing employment, its
share remains higher than that of both Tamil Nadu and Maharashtra. This implies that
half of Tamil Nadu’s manufacturing workforce is now in the organised sector, compared
to all India where it is still one –third.

4.9.2 There has also been a clear trend toward the concentration of employment in large
factories (those employing 100 or more workers). Large factories accounted for about
72% of employment in 2010–11, rising to 82% in 2023–24. The corresponding all-India
figures are 73% and 80%. These figures indicate that the “organised turn” in Tamil Nadu
is not evenly distributed across firm sizes. Medium and small factories are less central
to employment than they were a decade ago. In other words, the state displays a new
dual pattern: an expanding group of relatively formalised unorganised enterprises, and a
highly concentrated, large-firm-dominated organised sector.

4.10 Financing MSMEs
4.10.1 Despite their spread, productivity and contribution to employment, MSMEs still struggle
to sustain and scale up in value chains. The most important constraint is the timely
and adequate availability of finance. MSMEs—especially micro enterprises and startups—often lack collateral, have limited credit histories, and may not have the capacity
to produce the financial statements required by formal lenders. Evidence shows that
the cost of credit for MSMEs in all over India, not just in Tamil Nadu, is higher than for
international peers, and banks are generally reluctant to lend due to the higher risk
associated with limited operating histories and absence of collateral.19

4.10.2 In the unorganised sector, in 2023-24, about 75% of MSMEs are self-financed in the state,
15% access formal finance, and about 10% rely on informal sources. This is slightly better
than the national average, where formal access is about 10% and 90% rely on informal
or self-finance. In summary, the overwhelming majority of MSMEs are self-financed.
Many argue that a transparent credit-rating system, simplification and reduction in
documentation requirements, and interest-rate subvention as essential to sustain and
expand the MSME sector

102

4.11 Sub-regional Entrepreneurship
4.11.1 Another significant aspect is the spatial dimension of industrialization in the state.
The enterprises are fairly distributed across sub-regions within the state. Though it
is true that western (Coimbatore and Tiruppur regions) and northern (Chennai and
Kancheepuram) parts are the most industrialized regions, they are still spatially diverse
if one compares with Gujarat and Maharashtra. Each region has certain specific industrial
clusters dominated by small scale enterprises and localized entrepreneurship20. The
automobile cluster in Chennai, the readymade garments and textiles cluster in Tirupur, the
steel and power loom cluster in Salem, the automotive and electronics cluster in Hosur,
the fireworks cluster in Sivakasi on the back of industrial policies that have prioritized
sector-specific manufacturing. (Figure 4.7)

Figure 4.7: Spatial Dimension of Industrialisation in Tamil Nadu

4.11.2 Beside new industries that came up with increased private investment, Tamil Nadu also
saw the movement of industries from urban to rural or semi-urban cities21. Some of these
new, semi-urban cities became growth centres, shifting growth poles from metropolitan
centres to small towns accelerating a broad-based urbanisation model with multiple
nodes and clusters. We use the Herfindahl–Hirschman Index (HHI)22 to measure the
20 Damodaran, H. (2017, September 27). How Tamil Nadu’s rural industry model can keep farm unrest at bay https://indianexpress.com/article/explained/tamil-nadu-farmers-protests-maratha-protests-patel-protests-kapu-protests-3051642/

21 Ghani, E., Goswami, A. G., & Kerr, W. R. (2012). Is India’s manufacturing sector moving away from cities? (Working Paper No.
12-090). Harvard Business School.

22 Hirschman, A. O. (2013). The passions and the interests: Political arguments for capitalism before its triumph. Princeton
University Press.

103

spatial concentration of industrial activity across districts. The index for the state is 796,
compared to 867 in Maharashtra and 1,076 in Gujarat. This indicates that enterprises are
relatively better distributed across sub-regions, reflecting a broader spatial spread. The
western region accounts for about 40% of all MSMEs and 46% of registered enterprises,
while the northern region remains vibrant, accounting for 24% of all MSMEs and 21% of
registered units. The southern region has steadily emerged as a site of entrepreneurship,
accounting for 26% of all MSMEs and 22% of registered units. The coastal and central
regions, known for their agricultural base, continue to function as agrarian enclaves with
limited industrial presence, accounting for 11% of units.
4.11.3 There are, however, greater variations within regions than between them. Coimbatore,
Tiruppur, Salem, and Erode constitute about 80 % of western-region entrepreneurship,
while Chennai, Thiruvallur, Kancheepuram, and Vellore account for about 75% of northern
activity, with Viluppuram and Cuddalore remaining relatively backward. In the southern
region, Madurai, Virudhunagar, Kanyakumari, and Thoothukkudi account for about 70%,
leaving Theni, Ramanathapuram, and Tenkasi behind. In the coastal and central regions,
Tiruchirappalli and Thanjavur dominate industrial activity, whereas Perambalur, Ariyalur,
and Mayiladuthurai lag.

4.11.4 A significant but less noticed aspect is therefore its more decentralized nature and more
broad-based entrepreneurship. Besides Chennai, most of the regional clusters are known
for its agro-commerical based entrepreneurship drawn from middle castes. Many of
them are from ordinary peasant and provincial mercantile castes as opposed to big MNC
or pan-Indian Bania-Marwari capital as in other regions 23. While Tamil Nadu along with
Gujarat and Maharashtra acquires the badge of most industrialized state in the country,
what makes it distinct from the other two is this labour intensive, spatially and socially
inclusive nature of industrialization which has drawn a greater share of population out
of agriculture.

4.12 Industrial Corridors
4.12.1 Tamil Nadu has two broad kinds of clusters. As UNIDO notes that a well-performing cluster
should have strong backward and forward linkages across the value chain and effective
connections between firms or MSMEs and the support institutions around them. First,
there are MSME clusters built around relatively modern technologies. Examples include
the garments cluster in Tiruppur, the poultry and lorry-body-building clusters around
Namakkal, and the fireworks, matches, and printing clusters in Sivakasi. The Coimbatore
pump industry arose from the region’s maturing textile machinery and engineering
ecosystem. Proximity to urban hubs, particularly Bengaluru, has shaped cluster formation
in places such as Hosur.
4.12.2 Second, there are ancillary MSME clusters supplying to large industries: pressure-vessel
ancillaries around BHEL in Tiruchirappalli and the automotive ancillary ecosystem in
Chennai – Ambattur and Guindy - around major OEMs. Their production processes and
quality systems follow the rigorous standards set by the large industries they supply,
and they tend to adopt international best practices earlier.
4.12.3 Tamil Nadu is expanding its network of industrial corridors. Current initiatives include:
a) The Tamil Nadu Defence Industrial Corridor, with nodes in Chennai, Tiruchirappalli,
Coimbatore, Salem, and Hosur. b) The Chennai–Bengaluru Industrial Corridor (CBIC). c)
The Kochi–Coimbatore–Bengaluru Industrial Corridor. d) The Chennai–Kanniyakumari
Industrial Corridor, with six nodes spanning Madurai–Dindigul–Virudhunagar–Theni;
Thoothukudi–Tirunelveli; Ramanathapuram; Cuddalore–Nagapattinam; Tiruchirappalli–
Pudukottai–Sivagangai; and Ariyalur–Perambalur. If the state develops a Coimbatore–
Madurai–Rajapalayam–Tenkasi–Tirunelveli Industrial Corridor, nearly every district in
Tamil Nadu would fall within the influence zone of at least one corridor. This would
deepen industrial integration, strengthen supply chains, and widen the spatial spread of
economic activity.
23 Chari, S. (2004). Fraternal capital: Peasant-workers, self-made men, and globalization in provincial India. Stanford University Press

105

4.12.4 With respect to MSMEs, Tamil Nadu has depth across agro-processing, textiles, auto
components, and even robotics. Some of these clusters evolved sui generis. There have
also been attempts to support MSMEs by linking them with large firms and academic
institutions to enable scaling up, technology access, and productivity improvements.
Upgradation of production methods has taken place through vendor development,
linkages with large companies, and inter-firm ties, supported by a few standouts public or
public–private institutions such as SITARC in pumps and NIFT–TEA for training. However,
the methods adopted and their success have been uneven. Some older institutions, such
as SITRA and CLRI, have lost momentum, while newer platforms—AMHUB in collaboration
with Guidance Tamil Nadu, Tata Technologies’ 71 Industry 4.0 centres, and TANSAM with
Siemens—aim to rebuild capabilities. Bottlenecks persist in terms of the strength of
linkages and success rates. Similarly, initiatives such as FaMe TN, which digitise facilitation
and offer subsidies, have made some attempt to reduce transaction costs.
4.12.5 Yet SMEs continue to struggle with market access beyond their regions, input price
volatility, rents, the cost of credit, and shortages of skilled labour, all of which squeeze
margins and discourage risk-taking. This became evident when tariffs imposed by the
Trump administration affected textiles: much of the sector was operating on wafer-thin
margins, and even small exogenous shocks were sufficient to push firms out of global
value chains.
4.12.6 In this sense, industry–academia collaboration remains weak, cross-sector learning
platforms are sporadic, and governance is fragmented across departments. These
challenges persist in a context where returns to real estate are higher than those in
manufacturing, potentially crowding out productive investment.
4.12.7 There have also been efforts to support new firms. Through TANSIM, the state now
has over 10,000 DPIIT-recognised startups, largely concentrated in software rather
than manufacturing, supported by a wide network of incubators anchored in academic
institutions and spread across five regional hubs. However, public procurement from
startups has not taken off, and government departments have not opened demand
pipelines. Evidence suggests that for MSMEs, beyond easing credit constraints and
improving market access, opening state procurement to startups and creating explicit
incentives and capability programmes for local sourcing and value-chain upgrading would
go a long way.

4.13 Conclusions
4.13.1 Tamil Nadu remains a leading centre of industrialisation in India, shaped by sustained
industrial policy, institutional capacity, and political commitment. The state has built
a diversified manufacturing base spanning traditional and high-technology sectors
and geographically distributed across regions through the coexistence of territorially
106

embedded MSMEs and large domestic and multinational firms. This ecosystem has
integrated local production systems into global value chains and enabled the state to
account for roughly one out of every seven factory jobs in the country.
4.13.2 Despite rising capital intensity, Tamil Nadu continues to exhibit relatively labour-intensive
industrialisation compared with states such as Gujarat and Maharashtra, reflected in
deeper MSME formalisation, comparatively higher wage shares, and lower levels of
contractualisation. It also hosts a large concentration of factories with R&D and training
facilities and maintains a significant public-sector presence in manufacturing, while the
state has actively facilitated investment and mediated industrial relations. Together,
these features underscore a distinctive trajectory in which industrial expansion has been
embedded within institutional support and a social-development orientation.
4.13.3 Nonetheless, structural pressures are becoming increasingly visible. Relative to its
own historical trajectory, contractualisation has risen, wage shares have declined,
and employment elasticity has weakened as capital deepening accelerates. MSMEs—
particularly own-account and unregistered enterprises—are losing ground, and many
traditional labour-intensive clusters face survival challenges without pathways for
technological and organisational upgrading. 4.13.4 Persistent skill deficits and limited
absorptive capacity constrain learning within firms and policy institutions even as global
integration intensifies, while manufacturing and services have expanded in parallel
rather than through mutually reinforcing linkages. Addressing these challenges requires
reorienting industrial growth toward employment generation and learning through MSME
revitalisation, cluster upgrading, skill formation, and stronger manufacturing–services
integration, while preserving regulatory commitments that have historically anchored
Tamil Nadu’s socially grounded industrial development.

107

Chapter 5

Agriculture

5.1 Introduction
5.1.1

Tamil Nadu’s agricultural sector while being relatively more modernized and more
commercialized compared to other parts of India is losing its importance as a major source
of sustenance for the rural households, due to the growth of non-farm employment in a
significant way in the rural areas of the state. Further, the internal composition of the
agricultural sector has experienced major changes wherein the importance of the crop
sector has declined and that of the livestock sector has increased with respect to the
gross value added in agriculture. This chapter maps changes in the agricultural sector
over the last two decades considering the relatively low growth of the sector in the last
couple of years. To begin with, the changes in land use are examined.

5.2 Land Use Classification
5.2.1

The land use classification of Tamil Nadu over two decades, from 2001-02 to 2023-241,
indicates some important changes (see Figure 5.1 and Table 5.1):

1 Unless specified all reference years in this chapter denote the value for triennium centered around the given year.

*Definitions:
1. Gross Cropped Area represents the total area sown once and/or more than once in a particular year, i.e., the area is counted
as many times as there are sowings in year. This total area is also known as total cropped area or total area sown.
2. Net Sown Area represents the total area sown with crops and orchards. Area sowed more than once in the same year is
counted only once.
3. Gross Irrigated Area is the total area under crops, irrigated once and/or more than once in a year. It is counted as many
times as the number of times the areas are cropped and irrigated in a year.
4. Net Irrigated Area is the area irrigated through any source once in a year for a particular crop.

5.2.2 First, out of a total geographical area of roughly 1.3 crore hectares, land under nonagricultural uses has showed an increase of 2 lakh hectares, that is from 15% of the
geographical area in triennium centred around 2001-02 to 17% in 2023-24; second, the net
sown area (NSA) has declined by an almost equivalent volume of land, 2 lakh hectares,
declining at an annual compound growth rate of -0.2% over this period; however, the
gross cropped area (GCA) has registered an increase because the intensity of cropping has
registered a growth with area cropped more than once growing at an annual compound
growth rate of 2%. Third, while current fallows have shown a decline, other fallows have
shown an increase from 11% to 14% of geographical area. Total fallows have therefore
registered an increase. All in all, while there has been a tendency to withdraw land from
cultivation since 2001-02 there has also been a simultaneous tendency to intensify
cultivation in cropped area, in Tamil Nadu. Next, the inter-district differences in cropping
intensity are mapped (Table 5.2).

5.2.3 Fourteen out of the 38 districts have a cropping intensity that is above the state average
of 1.29 in 2024-25. Mayiladuthurai has the highest cropping intensity with 2.31, followed
by another district in the Cauvery delta zone, namely, Tiruvarur at 1.78. The high cropping
intensity indicates the higher extent to which cultivable land is utilized for multiple crops
in these areas. Tamil Nadu’s cropping intensity is however lower than states such as
Punjab and Haryana that records a cropping intensity of 2. But Tamil Nadu’s cropping
intensity is similar to its neighbouring states of Karnataka and Kerala while Telangana
has a much higher cropping intensity at 1.5 and Andhra Pradesh lower at 1.2.4

5.3 Status of Irrigation
5.3.1

Over the two decades since 2001-02, the net irrigated area (NIA) has fluctuated and
has grown (see Table 5.3). There appears to be stagnancy with regard to the irrigation
potential that is tapped for cultivation over the two decades under consideration. We
have noted above that net sown area has registered a decline over 2001-02 to 2022-23
but the gross cropped area has registered a positive growth. This pattern of growth
suggests that the incidence of irrigation is more or less the same with respect to net
and gross area. In the triennium centred around 2001-02 the percentage of NSA irrigated
is 53% while the percentage of GCA irrigated is also around the same. This has been the
situation throughout the two decades under consideration. This pattern suggests that
the cropping intensity may not be very different between the irrigated and unirrigated
tracts in Tamil Nadu. As can be seen from table 5.3 and figure 5.2, the cropping intensity
between irrigated and unirrigated area is not vastly different except in the last triennium.
This is perhaps a reflection of the stability (or stagnancy) that has been achieved with
regard to irrigation and cropping intensity in the recent decades. This could also be a
reflection of the poor quality of irrigation that restricts rapid growth of area cropped
more than once in the irrigated regime or the adequacy of rainfall that permits double
cropping in rain-fed regions.

5.3.2 The data on rainfall for the years 2001-02 to 2023-24 provided by the Department of
