tract of Tamil Nadu. While the net irrigated area has not experienced much change over
the two decades since 2001-02, there are considerable changes with regard to sources of
irrigation (see Figure 5.3).

5.3.3 Wells remain the most important source of irrigation in Tamil Nadu and its importance
has further increased steadily. Nearly half the irrigated area in Tamil Nadu in 2001-02 was
irrigated by wells and this increased to 63% in 2022-23. Increase in area irrigated by wells
over the last two decades have come about essentially by an increase in tube well and
bore well irrigation. Area under tube and bore well irrigation increased to cover irrigating
2.3 lakh hectares to 5.5 lakh hectares. There were 2.6 lakh tube wells in Tamil Nadu in
2022-23 and about 99 % of these tube wells are energised by electricity. As regards
surface sources such as canals and tanks, area irrigated by these sources have declined
sharply since 2006-07 and the percentage of net irrigated area by surface sources have
also registered a decline. Area irrigated by canals has declined by more than 90000
hectares while area irrigated by tanks has declined by 1.1 lakh hectares over the two
decades under consideration.

5.4 Cropping Pattern
5.4.1

Cropping pattern of Tamil Nadu has been dominated by food crops, and this pattern has
got further accentuated over the two decades under consideration. In 2001-02, 72% of
the gross cropped area was under food crops and 28% was under non-food crops and
this pattern changed to 78% under food crops and 22% under non-food crops by 2023-24
(see Table 5.4 and Figures 5.4, 5.5 & 5.6).

5.4.2 The increase in area under food crops has essentially come about by an increase in area
under cereals, pulses, fruits and vegetables while food crops such as sugarcane, spices
and condiments have lost area over the two decades under consideration.

5.4.3 Paddy, cholam, maize, cumbu and ragi are the major cereals in Tamil Nadu with paddy
being the most predominant cereal in terms of area under cultivation. While paddy
accounted for 72.4 % of total area under cereals in 2001-02, this declined to 69.3% in 202324. However, absolute area under paddy cultivation increased from 18.8 lakh hectares in
115

2001-02 to 21.3 lakh hectares in 2023-24, an addition of 2.5 lakh hectares over the 22
years. Area under cholam remained more or less the same occupying 12.4% of total
cereals area in 2001-02 and 11.3% in 2023-24. Importance of cumbu and ragi declined over
the two decades from 5% of total cereal area to about 2% of total cereal area. Within
cereals, the crop that has experienced rapid growth has been the maize crop, growing
at an annual compound growth rate of 7.5% over 2001-02 to 2023-24. Area under maize
has increased by 3.6 lakh hectares from less than one lakh hectare in 2001-02, primarily
because of its demand as poultry feed. All other cereal crops such as cumbu, ragi, kora,
varagu, samai have seen an absolute decline in area under cultivation over the period
2001-02 to 2023-24.
5.4.4 Analysing the district wise cultivation of paddy, it is clear that while paddy is grown in all
the districts of Tamil Nadu, the major contribution to area and production of paddy come
from the delta districts of Thanjavur, Thiruvarur, Mayiladuthurai and Cuddalore, in 202324. However, Thiruvannamalai district accounting for 9% of paddy area has emerged
as an important centre of paddy production contributing 10% of paddy production of
Tamil Nadu which is equivalent to the production contribution from Thanjavur district.
Tiruvannamalai district is also in the forefront with regard to yield of paddy per hectare
with a yield of 3885 kg/ha, much higher than what is reported from the delta districts.
Paddy yield is however the highest in Tirunelveli district that accounts for 2% of
paddy production. The emergence of Thiruvannamalai and Thiruvallur as major paddy
growing regions is suggestive of successful adoption of appropriate seeds and irrigation
technologies for growth.

Box 5.1 Cropping Pattern in Tamil Nadu 2024-2025: An Overview
Though Tamil Nadu’s agriculture remains predominantly paddy-centric, horticulture
has expanded steadily, covering 16.3 lakh ha, while maize and other high-value crops
are increasingly grown in both rainfed and irrigated regions. To support this transition,
the state implements crop support and procurement incentives for paddy, promotes
millets, pulses, and horticulture through subsidies, improved seeds, and technology
support, and invests in micro-irrigation, tank/canal desilting, watershed management,
and other water-conservation measures. Crop insurance schemes with state subsidies
mitigate climate-related risks, while value-chain and market interventions, including FPO
strengthening, cold storage, and processing facilities, ensure better returns. Extension
services and digital advisories facilitate climate-smart and high-value crop cultivation,
reinforcing sustainability and farmer resilience.Overall, Tamil Nadu’s cropping pattern
reflects a gradual but decisive shift from paddy dominance toward diversified, waterefficient, and economically rewarding crops, supported by a combination of climate
adaptation strategies and market-oriented policies.
117

5.4.5 Black gram and green gram are the most important pulses in Tamil Nadu and area under
pulses has experienced a positive growth over the 22 years under consideration. All other
pulses-Bengal gram, red gram and horse gram-have experienced an absolute decline in
area under cultivation over the two decades since 2001-02. In 2001-02, just about 8% of
area under pulses was under irrigation and this changed to 14% in 2021-22.
5.4.6 Area under fruits increased from 3.2 lakh hectares in 2001-02 to 4 lakh hectares in 202223. Mango and banana are the two most important fruit crops in Tamil Nadu and the area
under these two crops have grown steadily over the two decades at an annual compound
growth rate of 1.4%. Guava, pineapple, jack fruit and ‘other fruits’ have also registered an
increase in area under cultivation during this period.
5.4.7 Area cultivated with vegetables has increased rapidly from 1.9 lakh hectares in 2001-02
to 3.1 lakh hectares in 2022-23, growing at an annual compound growth rate of 2.3%.
While tapioca, onion and tomato remain the three most important vegetables in Tamil
Nadu, over the two decades many other vegetables have gained importance such that
area under ‘other vegetables’ has increased more than threefold from 25000 hectares to
90000 hectares.
5.4.8 Area under sugarcane has nearly halved from 2.9 lakh hectares in 2001-02 to 1.3 lakh
hectares in 2024-25. Area under spices and condiments has also registered a decline of
40000 hectares, from 1.6 lakh hectares in 2001-02 to 1.2 lakh hectares in 2022-23. As
regards non-food crops, groundnut, coconut, cotton and gingelly are the important crops
in Tamil Nadu. Area cultivated with coconut has increased over the two decades from 3.3
lakh hectares to 4.7 lakh hectares. Area under cotton has remained more or less stable
while groundnut and gingelly have lost area rapidly. In sum, over the two decades since
2001-02, area under paddy, maize, fruits, vegetables and coconut have increased while
sugarcane, groundnut and coarse cereals (other than maize) have lost out.

5.5 Growth in Yields of Major Crops
5.5.1

Over the time 2001-02 to 2023-24, the yield of maize has grown at a high rate of 6.26%
(Table 5.6). Paddy’s yield has registered only a modest growth however. The same holds
true of pulses as well. This calls for policy attention given the decline in the total area
under cultivation.

5.5.3 Table 5.8 indicates that the two major fruit crops of Tamil Nadu, banana and mango, have
done poorly in terms of productivity growth over the two decades under consideration
since 2001-02. While banana has registered a low, positive growth in productivity mango
has experienced a negative rate of growth in productivity. These again require policy
attention. Lemon, grapes, jackfruit and guava have all registered fairly high growth in
productivity resulting in high increases in production of these fruits.

5.6 Use of Inputs by Agricultural Households
5.6.1

Use of chemical fertiliser, NPK, per unit of gross cropped area has increased over the two
and a half decades under consideration from about 152 Kg per hectare of gross cropped
area in 2000-01 to nearly 183 Kg per hectare of gross cropped area in 2024-25 (see table
5.9). While the use of nitrogen and phosphorous have increased over the years, that
of potassium has declined over the two and a half decades since 2000-01. The survey
conducted in 2018-19 by the National Sample Survey Organisation on the agricultural
households notes that in Tamil Nadu, bio-inputs are used by a considerable section of
agricultural households: 20% of agricultural households report use of bio-fertilisers, 10%
report use of bio-pesticides while 46% use manure. Further, during 2018 Kharif season,
77% of agricultural households procured paddy seeds from the local market, while the
corresponding percentage for maize was 97% and groundnut 70%5. This report further
notes that all the major agricultural inputs, except manure are procured from the local
markets and sale of produce also happen essentially in the local markets.

Table 5.10 indicates that fruits and vegetables contribute the most to total value of

that is prevalent between high value crops and low value crops in agriculture.

5.7.2

The declining importance of agriculture in the overall economy of the state comes out
clearly from table 5.11. The already low share of agriculture, forestry and fishing at 13%
to the total gross value added from all sectors in Tamil Nadu in 2011-12 has declined
gradually and is 10% of total GSVA by 2024-25. While the contribution of agricultural
sector to overall GVA of the state has declined, we find that within the agricultural
sector there have been major changes. The contribution from the crop sub-sector has
declined sharply while there has been a corresponding rise in the contribution of the
livestock sub-sector. The gross value addition from the crop sector has declined from
61% to 39% while that of the livestock has increased from 30% to 53% of the overall GVA
from agriculture, forestry and fishing. Thus, the livestock sector appears to be making
significant contributions to the overall growth of the agricultural economy of the state.
Given this pattern, the next section examines production in the livestock sector.

122

Box 5.2 Inland Fisheries: Turning Water into Prosperity
Inland fisheries in Tamil Nadu have a long and significant history, starting from the
colonial period when regulatory frameworks such as the Indian Fisheries Act of 1897
were introduced. After independence, the state emphasized organized development of
freshwater fisheries by promoting scientific fish culture, improving seed production, and
making efficient use of the many tanks, reservoirs, rivers, and ponds available. Tamil
Nadu has a total geographical area of 130,058 sq km, of which about 3.83 lakh hectares,
or roughly 3%, are inland water bodies suitable for fisheries. Although this is a relatively
small portion of the state’s land, these water resources have the potential to generate
significant fish production if managed effectively. Currently, the state produces around
2.40 lakh tonnes of inland fish annually, providing livelihoods to approximately 2.3 lakh
fisherfolk who are often organized through cooperative societies. Despite this, the actual
production is only about half of the estimated potential, indicating substantial room for
growth. Technological interventions are increasingly being applied to improve productivity
and sustainability, including cage culture in reservoirs, recirculatory aquaculture systems
(RAS), biofloc technology, integration of fish farming with agriculture, and large-scale
high-quality seed production. The sector faces challenges such as seasonal dependence
on rainfall, underutilization of water bodies, and ecological pressures on native fish
species. Looking ahead, inland fisheries in Tamil Nadu offer immense opportunities to
expand production, strengthen rural livelihoods, enhance nutritional security, and even
promote eco-tourism around water bodies.

5.8 Production of Eggs, Meat and Milk
5.8.1

Production of meat has increased manifold from 39 thousand tonnes in 2001-02 to 758
thousand tonnes in 2023-24, growing at an annual compound growth rate of 14.4% over
the two decades since 2001-02 (see Figure 5.7). Production of eggs has also increased
rapidly from 39249 lakhs in 2001-02 to 2.2 lakhs in 2023-24, growing at 8.2% annual
compound growth rate over the 22 years since 2001-02 (see Figure 5.8). Milk production
has also grown consistently over the two decades from 4836 thousand tonnes in 20012002 to 10856 thousand tonnes in 2023-24, growing at a positive rate of growth of 3.7
per annum (see Figure 5.9). A recent research study attributes the rapid increase in
milk production in Tamil Nadu to the state’s proactive initiatives, effective execution of
cooperative models, as also technological advancements7.

5.9 Production of Fish
5.9.1

The composition of fish production has undergone changes in Tamil Nadu over the two
decades since 2001-02. In 2002-03, the contribution of marine fish production to overall
fish production was 83% (3,78,074 out of 4,53,904) and it declined to 72% (6,38,463 out of
8,86,837) by 2023-24. As a corollary the contribution of inland fish production increased
from 20% to 30 % to total fish production over the two decades under consideration.
While the marine fish production has grown at an annual compound growth rate of 2.6%
over 2000-01 to 2024-25, the inland fish production has grown at a much higher rate of
4.4%.

Box 5.3 Agricultural Credit Trends in Tamil Nadu (Last Five Years)
Tamil Nadu has a strong rural credit architecture supported by 13,528 scheduled
commercial bank offices and 4,474 PACS, (as of 31 March 2024) ensuring wide outreach
to farmers. Official NABARD and Ministry of Finance data show that agricultural credit
disbursal has more than doubled from Rs. 2.23 lakh crore in 2019-20 to Rs. 4.52 lakh
crore in 2023-24, registering a CAGR of ~19%. Annual growth remained high (15–23%)
driven by institutional lending, expansion of Kisan Credit Cards, and deeper rural banking
penetration.
Inclusive Credit delivery: In 2023-24, around 299 lakh farmer loan accounts were served, of
which 78% belonged to small and marginal farmers. NABARD’s State Focus Paper (202425) estimates an agriculture credit potential of Rs. 3.75 lakh crore, signalling sustained
demand and scope for scaling long-term and value-chain financing. Portfolio quality has
improved, with agricultural NPAs in scheduled commercial banks declining from ~10% to
~6% over the same period.
SLBC data indicate a gradual deepening of rural financial intermediation in Tamil Nadu,
with district-level credit–deposit ratios suggesting that a rising share of rural deposits is
being redeployed locally. NABARD assessments further note a diversification of agricultural
lending toward allied activities such as dairy, fisheries, poultry, and horticulture, reducing
exclusive reliance on seasonal crop loans. This shift reflects changing rural livelihood
patterns and improves income stability, while also supporting year-round employment

126

and value addition within the agrarian economy
The Government of Tamil Nadu has supported this credit expansion through measures
such as free crop insurance for farmers, interest subvention for cooperative crop loans,
PACS computerisation, revitalisation of cooperative credit institutions, and targeted
schemes promoting micro-irrigation, horticulture, mechanisation, dairy and fisheries.
The state’s initiatives in linking credit with climate-resilient agriculture, millet promotion,
FPO strengthening, and digital service delivery have further improved formal credit
absorption.
Overall, Tamil Nadu’s agricultural credit landscape shows rapid growth, deeper inclusion,
and improving quality, positioning the state to shift from predominantly short-term crop
loans toward greater term lending for irrigation, mechanisation, allied sectors and postharvest infrastructure, aligned with future demand and climate-resilience priorities.

5.10 Some Aspects of Land Holding8
5.10.1 Table 5.13 shows that nearly 93% of households in 2018-19 in Tamil Nadu possess
marginal holdings of less than one hectare and another 5% possess small holdings of 1
to 2 hectares, indicating the predominance of small and marginal holdings in rural Tamil
Nadu. Of the 97 lakh rural households in Tamil Nadu possessing land, 30% of households
are the Scheduled Castes and 68% are the Other Backward Classes. About 26% of rural
households that report ownership of land are classified as agricultural households and
the remaining 74% are classified as non-agricultural households by the 2018-19 survey9.

5.10.2 The 2018-19 survey further estimates the average monthly income per agricultural

5.11 Key Areas that Need Attention
5.11.1 Several key areas in Tamil Nadu’s agricultural sector require immediate attention to ensure
sustainable growth and resilience: The net sown area has declined by approximately 2
lakh hectares since 2001-02, accompanied by a rise in fallows indicating underutilized
cultivable land and the need for policies to reclaim and optimize fallow lands. Decline in
surface irrigation sources, such as canals and tanks, alongside stagnancy in net irrigated
area, highlights the urgent need to rehabilitate traditional water infrastructure while
simultaneously promoting efficient groundwater management as wells now dominate
net irrigated area. Heavy dependence on local markets for inputs and outputs underscore
the necessity for enhanced sustainable input systems and market infrastructure
development.​ Stagnation in yields of key crops like paddy, pulses and important fruits
need to be addressed to improve livelihoods of agricultural households. The livestock
sector in Tamil Nadu’s agriculture has shown remarkable growth, emerging as a key driver
amid the declining fortunes of the crop sub-sector. Its contribution to gross value added
from agriculture, forestry, and fishing has risen sharply from 30% in 2011-12 to 53% by
2024-25. This sector needs attention through cold storage infrastructure development
state-wide to curb wastage in marketing perishable products like eggs, meat, milk, and
fish, alongside strengthened support for sustainable practices and market linkages.

11 State Planning Commission. (2025). Rural Non-Farm Employment in Tamil Nadu. State Planning Commission, Government
of Tamil Nadu

129

5.12 Conclusions
5.12.1 Over the past two decades, Tamil Nadu’s agricultural sector has exhibited relative
stability alongside notable structural shifts. A key development in the recent decade has
been the rapid expansion of livestock activities, underscoring the growing importance
of cold storage and allied infrastructure across the state. Enhanced post-harvest and
value-chain facilities are essential to minimise losses in the production and marketing of
eggs, meat, milk, and fish, and thereby strengthen farm incomes. While paddy remains
the dominant cereal crop, maize cultivation has expanded significantly; yet awareness
of the Minimum Support Price (MSP) remains limited. Survey evidence from 2018–19
suggests that around 68 % of agricultural households were unaware of the MSP for
paddy and nearly 90 % unaware of the MSP for maize, highlighting the need for improved
information dissemination and assured procurement to mitigate market risk. The same
evidence shows that close to 80 % of households rely on local markets for both input
procurement and output sales, underlining the centrality of these market spaces and
the urgency of investments in storage, transport, grading, and trading infrastructure to
enhance efficiency and farmer welfare.
5.12.2 At the production level, the adoption of bio-inputs by a considerable share of farmers
indicates an emerging orientation towards environmentally sustainable cultivation
practices. This trend can be reinforced through policies that promote the development
and distribution of locally appropriate bio-inputs, thereby reducing dependence on
chemical inputs. However, despite the institutional presence of government extension
agencies, Krishi Vigyan Kendras, and agricultural universities, their outreach remains
limited, signalling the need to strengthen extension systems to ensure timely access to
reliable technical knowledge. In addition, only a small proportion of households possess
soil health cards, even though soil-specific nutrient management is central to both
productivity and sustainability. Expanding soil testing capacity and strengthening soil
health laboratory networks are therefore essential for promoting efficient input use and
safeguarding the long-term ecological viability of agriculture in Tamil Nadu.

130

Chapter 6

Social Sector
6.1 Introduction
6.1.1

The social sector comprises key pillars of human development—health, education,
nutrition, water and sanitation, poverty eradication, and social justice. Owing to an early
start and a sustained commitment to progressive, inclusive, and people-centred policies,
Tamil Nadu has made remarkable strides in these areas. Most of the State’s social sector
programmes have, by default, embedded mechanisms to include and uplift historically
disadvantaged and marginalized sections.

6.1.2

Development is neither linear nor isolated. Progress in one area of the social sector
often triggers positive outcomes in others, creating a web of mutually reinforcing
improvements. For example, better nutrition improves health and enhances learning
outcomes in education; improved education leads to human capital formation and
employability; gender equity expands the productive labour force; and so on. Together,
these advancements have enhanced human capabilities, improved productivity, and
raised household incomes.

6.1.3

This interplay between social development and economic growth has created a virtuous
cycle: investments contribute to economic expansion, which increases state revenue,
enabling greater public investment in social sectors. This cycle of mutual reinforcement
is the essence of the state’s distinctive approach that achieves simultaneous progress in
both social and economic spheres.

6.1.4

Moreover, the social sector in Tamil Nadu has exerted a deep and enduring influence
on the State’s development trajectory. Rather than viewing education, health, and
representation solely as instruments for economic growth, social reform movements in
Tamil Nadu have emphasised these as intrinsic social entitlements.

6.1.5

Over the past five years, this virtuous cycle has accelerated. Improvements in the social
sector have driven further economic momentum, and vice versa. This chapter presents
an overview of each component of the social sector—health, education, nutrition, water
and sanitation, gender equality, social inclusion, poverty eradication, and social justice.
It analyses key trends over the last five years, evaluates budgetary allocations, reviews
major schemes and achievements, explores intra-state disparities, and identifies current
challenges and the outlook for the coming year.
131

6.2 State Spending on Social Sector
6.2.1

Tamil Nadu’s expenditure on key social sector programmes has expanded significantly
in recent years, reflecting the State’s sustained commitment to social welfare and
human development. Between 2021-22 and 2025-26 (RE), the total expenditure on all
social sectors (SSE) increased from Rs. 1,13,268 crore to Rs. 1,57864 crore, registering an
average annual growth of about 8.72% (Table A 1 in Appendices) indicating that while the
State continues to prioritise social spending. The composition of spending shows a shift
towards welfare and nutrition-related programmes and a revival of health investment in
the post-pandemic years.

i.

Health and Family Welfare: Expenditure rose sharply from Rs. 19,012 crore in 2021-22
to Rs. 20595 crore in 2025-26 (RE) recording the second highest among major heads.
This increase reflects renewed focus on strengthening primary-health infrastructure,
upgrading medical colleges and public hospitals, and implementing flagship initiatives
such as Makalai Thedi Maruthuvam. This trend highlights the Government’s strong postCOVID commitment to health-system resilience.

ii.

Social Welfare and Nutrition: Expenditure rose steeply from Rs. 24,087 crore in 202122 to Rs. 36,977 crore in 2025-26 (RE), the highest growth among all sectors (average
growth of 15.0%). The increase underscores the expansion of social-security pensions,
marriage and maternity-assistance schemes, and nutrition programmes such as ICDS,
Chief Minister’s Breakfast Scheme and Nutritious Meal Schemes.

iii.

Water Supply, Sanitation, Housing and Urban Development: Spending increased from
Rs. 17,556 crore in 2021-22 to Rs. 17198 in 2024-25 with a 11.84.% growth in 2024-25. The
growth reflects continued emphasis on drinking-water supply, sanitation, and sustainable
urban-service delivery through flagship programmes such as, Combined Water Supply
Schemes, Underground Sewerage Schemes, Namma Kudiyiruppu, and the Tamil Nadu
Urban Habitat Development Board schemes. For 2025-26, the revised estimate was Rs.
13,826 crore.

iv.

Civil Supplies: Expenditure increased from Rs. 9534 crore in 2021-221 to Rs. 21224 crore
in 2025-26 (RE), recording an average growth rate of 28.2%. The allocation largely covers
food subsidies and price-stabilisation measures under the Public Distribution System
(PDS), ensuring continued access to essential commodities including food security for all
sections of society.

132

v.

Welfare of SCs, STs and OBCs: Expenditure increased from Rs. 3269 crore in 2021-22 to
Rs. 6023 crore in 2025-25 (RE) with an average growth rate of 16.9%, indicating renewed
attention to scholarships, hostels, and livelihood programmes for such marginalised
groups, though the overall growth pace remains moderate.

vi.

Labour and Labour Welfare: Expenditure grew from Rs. 597 crore in 2021-22 to Rs. 3344
crore in 2025-26 (RE). The focus appears to be on skill development, social-security
measures for unorganised and migrant workers.

vii.

Education, Sports, Art and Culture: Expenditure increased from Rs. 38,678 crore in
2021-22 to Rs. 54113 crore in 2025-26 (RE), registering an average growth rate of 9%.
This increase reflects stable yet moderate growth, consistent with ongoing support for
school infrastructure, teacher recruitment, and higher education expansion

6.2.2 In the absence of the Union’s committed share, the State has had to finance nearly the
entire outlay in the interest of students and spending on other expansion initiatives may
have certainly been constrained.
6.2.3 During the past five years, the state witnessed successive disasters - spanning two
pandemic waves, frequent cyclones, and recurring floods explain the non-linear spikes
observed in certain budget heads, especially relief and natural calamities, health, water,
sanitation, housing, urban development, and social welfare and nutrition. While COVID-19
dominated expenditure in 2020-21 and 2021-22, the subsequent years witnessed major
relief and rehabilitation spending, including investments in storm-water drainage, repair
of roads, and restoration of water bodies, validating why some sectors’ year-on-year
growth in 2025-26 exceeds their long-term growth.
6.2.4 The changing composition of social sector spending is being shaped by mounting fiscal
pressures faced by the State on two major fronts. The continuous decline in Tamil Nadu’s
share of federal taxes and grants—including its legitimate entitlements under Centrally
Sponsored Schemes (CSS) and disaster-relief assistance—has compelled the State to
bear a growing share of both contingent and regular welfare expenditures, thereby
constraining its fiscal space to an unprecedented degree. This strain has been further
aggravated by the loss of revenue following the expiry of GST compensation and the
recent reduction in GST rates, both of which are expected to further narrow the State’s
already limited fiscal capacity for social investment.

133

6.2.5 Thus, all social sectors recorded positive long-term growth between 2021-22 and 202526 (RE). The highest expansion in Health and Social Welfare reflects a post-pandemic
reprioritisation of human development, while programmes for SCs/STs and Labour
Welfare show stable but slower growth. Education spending, though substantial, has not
kept pace with the overall economic expansion.
6.2.6 Importantly, the fiscal and disaster-related pressures that shaped expenditure patterns
in recent years underscore the need for a resilient social investment strategy - one that
maintains continuity in essential welfare and health spending while improving quality,
efficiency, and outcome-based monitoring of social-sector outlays to maximise social
impact within a constrained fiscal envelope.

6.3 Demographic, Health and Poverty Transitions in Tamil
Nadu
6.3.1

Tamil Nadu has entered a mature Stage-4 demographic regime1, characterised by
low fertility and low mortality, marking a decisive shift from population growth–led
development to productivity- and quality-led development. This transition has important
implications for labour supply, ageing, health expenditure, and the design of social
protection systems.

6.3.2 Declines in mortality preceded reductions in fertility, driven by improvements in public
health, nutrition, female education, and access to healthcare services.
6.3.3 These demographic shifts have interacted closely with social sector investments to
influence poverty outcomes. Improvements in education, health, housing, sanitation, and
access to basic services—key dimensions captured under the Multidimensional Poverty
Index—have reduced both the incidence and intensity of deprivation. As a result, Tamil
Nadu has recorded a steep decline in MPI over the past decade.

1In Stage 1 (applied to most of the world before the Industrial Revolution), both birth rates and death rates are high; popula-

tion size remains fairly constant but can have major swings with events such as wars or pandemics.
In Stage 2, the introduction of modern medicine lowers death rates, especially among children, while birth rates remain high;
the result is rapid population growth. Many of the least developed countries today are in Stage 2.
In Stage 3, birth rates gradually decrease, usually as a result of improved economic conditions, an increase in women’s status,
and access to contraception. Population growth continues, but at a lower rate. Most developing countries are in Stage 3.
In Stage 4, birth and death rates are both low, stabilizing the population. These countries tend to have stronger economies,
higher levels of education, better healthcare, a higher proportion of working women, and a fertility rate hovering around two
children per woman. Most developed countries are in Stage 4.
A possible Stage 5 would include countries in which fertility rates have fallen significantly below replacement level (2 children)
and the elderly population is greater than the youthful population.

134

6.4 Population Dynamics and Demographic Transition
6.4.1

Tamil Nadu’s demographic indicators reflect a sustained and long-term transition towards
low fertility and low mortality. The decline in crude birth rates and total fertility rates
has been gradual and broad-based, reflecting improvements in female education, access
to reproductive healthcare, and declining infant and child mortality due to investments
in public health. These trends are consistent with a mature Stage-4 demographic regime

6.4.2 Table 6.1 shows a steady decline in fertility and mortality indicators over time alongside
continued improvements in life expectancy at 73.4 years – well above India’s 70.3 years.
Fertility rates have reached or fallen below replacement level, while mortality reductions
reflect long-standing investments in public health and healthcare access. While population
momentum continues to influence absolute numbers, the pace of growth has moderated
significantly, reducing pressure on basic services while increasing the importance of
productivity-led growth strategies.

6.5 Changing Age Structure and Labour Force Implications
6.5.1

The demographic transition has altered the age composition of Tamil Nadu’s population.
The share of the working-age population remains relatively high, while the proportion
of children has declined. Simultaneously, improvements in life expectancy have led to a
gradual increase in the elderly population.

6.5.2 Table 6.2 highlights a declining dependency ratio driven by a reduced child population
and a relatively stable working-age share. Tamil Nadu now has the highest proportion
of working-age population in India (Figure 6.1), reflecting its advanced demographic
transition. However, the rising proportion of elderly persons and their deaths signals the
onset of population ageing. While the current demographic structure offers conditions
135

conducive to a demographic dividend, this window is time-bound and contingent upon
the State’s capacity to expand employment opportunities, enhance skill development,
and raise labour productivity.

6.5.3 Population ageing will increasingly shape public expenditure priorities, particularly in
healthcare, pensions, and social security. Anticipating these shifts is essential for ensuring
fiscal sustainability and inclusive growth.

6.6 Health Transition: Mortality Decline and Emerging Morbidity Patterns
6.6.1

Parallel to demographic change, Tamil Nadu has undergone an advanced health transition.
Mortality from communicable diseases, maternal causes, and nutrition-related conditions
has declined significantly, reflecting improvements in immunisation coverage, institutional
deliveries, sanitation, and access to healthcare services.

6.6.2 As mortality from infectious diseases declined, the disease burden has shifted towards
non-communicable diseases such as cardiovascular conditions, diabetes, cancers, and
chronic respiratory illnesses. This epidemiological transition is closely associated with
ageing, urbanisation, food and lifestyle changes, necessitating a reorientation of health
systems towards chronic disease management, early screening, and continuity of care.
6.6.3 In response to the evolving morbidity profile, Tamil Nadu has initiated targeted interventions
aimed at strengthening the management of non-communicable diseases. The ‘Makalai
Thedi Maruthuvam’ scheme represents a strategic shift towards community-based
and home-oriented healthcare delivery, with a focus on NCD screening, continuity of
medication, palliative care, and preventive guidance. By extending healthcare services
beyond institutional settings, the programme aligns with the requirements of an ageing
population and a rising burden of chronic conditions.
6.6.4 As the burden of chronic and age-related conditions increases, strengthening and scaling
such primary-care–centred approaches will be critical to sustaining health gains and
preventing emerging forms of health-related deprivation.

6.7 Multidimensional Poverty Reduction and Human Development Outcomes
6.7.1

The demographic and health transitions have interacted closely with sustained social
sector investments to reduce multidimensional deprivation in Tamil Nadu. Improvements
in education, health, housing, sanitation, and access to basic services have reduced both
the incidence and intensity of poverty, as captured by the latest Multidimensional Poverty
Index (MPI) estimates (for the year 2022-23) made by NITI Aayog.

6.7.2

Tamil Nadu’s trajectory has been impressive. Starting from 7.16% in 2013–14, the state
reduced its MPI to 1.43% in 2022–23 (NITI Aayog estimate), with projections (made by SPC)
indicating a further decline to 0.72% by 2026–27 (Table 6.3).

6.7.3

District-level disparities however persist. According to the Tamil Nadu State Planning
Commission’s MPI report (2022), districts such as Dharmapuri, Perambalur, and Ariyalur
record higher levels of multidimensional deprivation relative to the State average.
Vulnerable groups, including Scheduled Castes, Scheduled Tribes, and women-headed
households, experience a higher incidence and intensity of deprivation, particularly in
rural areas.

6.7.4

To address these gaps, targeted action plans are required. The State Government,
through SPC, is implementing the Focus Blocks Development Programme (FBDP) precisely
to advance such regionally balanced and inclusive development (see Box 6.1).

138

Box 6.1 Focus Blocks Development Programme (FBDP)
– An SPC-Led Strategy towards Inclusive Development
Despite Tamil Nadu’s faster and overall development outcomes, significant interregional
disparities persist at the block level, particularly in remote, tribal, and socio-economically
disadvantaged areas. Recognising the limitations of uniform State-wide interventions
in addressing these spatial inequities, the Government of Tamil Nadu, conceived and
launched the Focus Blocks Development Programme (FBDP) in 2024–25 as a missionmode initiative for inclusive and balanced inclusive development.
FBDP identifies 50 development-deficit blocks through an objective, evidence-based
framework using multi-dimensional indicators covering social and economic dimensions.
Anchored institutionally in the SPC, the programme adopts a convergence-led planning
approach, coordinating 65 schemes across 19 departments, and is supported by dedicated
catalytic funding of Rs. 5 crore per block over three years to address critical development
gaps. Each focus block prepares a Block Development Strategy Paper (BDSP), ensuring
decentralised, context-specific planning aligned with State priorities.
6.7.5

Sustaining ongoing development gains high economic growth will require continued
attention to health system strengthening and inclusive social protection frameworks.

6.8 Housing and Urban Development
6.8.1

Tamil Nadu is among the most urbanised States in India, with nearly half of its population
residing in urban areas, reflecting sustained economic diversification, industrialisation,
and migration. While urbanisation has driven growth and productivity, it has also created
structural pressures on housing affordability, urban infrastructure, environmental
sustainability, and governance systems.

6.8.2 A persistent shortage of affordable housing for Low Income Groups (LIG), informal workers,
and migrants remain a major challenge. Escalating land prices, limited availability of
serviced land, financing constraints, and regulatory rigidities have widened the demand–
supply gap, resulting in continued prevalence of slums and informal settlements.
6.8.3 Urban infrastructure deficits compound housing stress, with pressure on water supply,
sewerage, stormwater drainage, solid waste management, transport, and public spaces,
and uneven service levels across urban local bodies (ULBs). Climate change has further
intensified such vulnerabilities disproportionately affecting low-income households.
139

Institutional constraints—limited fiscal autonomy, capacity gaps, fragmented planning,
and rising operation and maintenance liabilities—add to these challenges.
6.8.4 The State’s housing and urban development strategy is anchored in a welfare-oriented,
inclusive framework combining public housing, slum redevelopment, and infrastructure
upgrading. The Tamil Nadu Urban Habitat Development Board (TNUHDB) plays a central
role through in-situ redevelopment and rehabilitation housing, with resettlement where
in-situ solutions are not feasible.
6.8.5 Urban infrastructure development has been scaled up through Singara Chennai 2.0 and
Smart Cities Mission. A coordinated institutional framework supports these efforts, with
the Tamil Nadu Housing Board (TNHB), TNUHDB, CMDA, and CUMTA playing complementary
roles in land development, housing for the poor, spatial planning, and integrated mobility.
However, housing demand continues to outpace supply in high-growth regions, while
land acquisition challenges, rising construction costs, capacity gaps in smaller ULBs, and
climate risks persist.
6.8.6 Going forward, the strategy must shift from a project-centric approach to a systemsoriented urban transformation framework. Priorities include expanding affordable
and rental housing, improving land and housing market efficiency, embedding climate
resilience into planning and building norms, strengthening ULB fiscal sustainability, and
enhancing metropolitan governance.

6.9 Rural Water Supply, Sanitation and Water Security
6.9.1

Water security remains a central development challenge for Tamil Nadu. With access
to only about 3 % of the country’s water resources and high dependence on monsoon
rainfall, the State faces inherent structural constraints in ensuring reliable and equitable
water availability. Climate change has intensified these pressures through increasing
rainfall variability, placing growing stress on both surface and groundwater systems and
widening regional disparities in water access.

6.9.2 The State’s water resources strategy focuses on conservation of surface water,
groundwater recharge, equitable irrigation up to tail-end areas, and meeting rising
domestic, agricultural, and industrial demand. Investments have been scaled up in
irrigation infrastructure through new reservoirs, barrages, check dams, canals, and
rehabilitation of dams, tanks, and channels. River interlinking and flood mitigation works
are being undertaken to address regional imbalances and strengthen climate resilience.
Reflecting this scale-up, expenditure in the water resources sector has been increasing

140

from Rs. 7,338 crore in 2022–23 to Rs. 8,232 crore in 2023–24, to Rs. 8,398 crore in 2024-25,
to Rs. 9,460 crore in 2025-26 (BE) driven by river interlinking projects, NABARD-funded
schemes, and externally aided programmes.
6.9.3 Rural water supply and sanitation are central to inclusive and climate-resilient
development. The state is transforming Village Panchayats into clean, green, liveable,
and water-secure rural hubs through expanded drinking water infrastructure, sanitation
facilities, solid waste management, and decentralised water conservation. Rejuvenation
of ponds and ooranies, with community participation, remains a key strategy.
6.9.4 The Jal Jeevan Mission (JJM) has strengthened household-level water security by providing
functional household tap connections. Bulk water transfer systems and retrofitting of
existing schemes are addressing water stress in vulnerable regions. Climate resilience
is increasingly embedded in the State’s water governance framework through storage
augmentation, groundwater recharge, tank desilting, ecosystem-based interventions,
and inter-basin transfers.
6.9.5 Tamil Nadu’s water strategy must move from fragmented interventions to an integrated,
climate-resilient water security framework. Priorities include strengthening source
sustainability, improving real-time hydrological monitoring, enhancing community
stewardship of water assets, and aligning investments with district-level vulnerability
profiles.

6.10. Education: Building a Just, Knowledge-Rich, and
Future-Ready Tamil Nadu
6.10.1 Education has long been the foundation of Tamil Nadu’s social development. Despite
education being a concurrent subject, Tamil Nadu continues to shoulder a disproportionately
large share of responsibility in the present fiscal-federal context, spending nearly 2 % of
its GSDP from its own resources on education.
6.10.2 Over the decades, the State has expanded access to near-universal schooling, achieved
one of the highest Gross Enrolment Ratios (GER) in higher education nationally, and
pioneered welfare-centric educational reforms that have shaped policy thinking across
India. Women, as well as socially and educationally backward communities, enjoy
significantly greater access to all levels of education in Tamil Nadu compared to most
other States, reflecting the State’s long-standing commitment to equity and inclusion.

141

6.10.3 Yet the State now stands at a pivotal juncture. Rapid shifts in technology, artificial
intelligence, climate risks, labour markets, and demographic patterns demand a new
generation of reforms—reforms that move beyond access towards strengthening quality,
equity, digital readiness, research capacity, skilling, and employability. The State’s policy
approach outlines strategic directions and commitments for both school and higher
education, aiming to sustain Tamil Nadu’s leadership in inclusive human development.

6.11 School Education
6.11.1 The recent Survey by NSSO (The Comprehensive Modular Survey: Education, NSSO 80th
Round, 2025) shows that Tamil Nadu’s school enrolment pattern differs markedly from
the national profile, reflecting the State’s long-standing public provisioning as well as its
extensive aided and recognised private school network. While 55.9 % of students in India
are enrolled in government schools, the corresponding share in Tamil Nadu is significantly
higher at 64.1 %, indicating the continued centrality of the public school system in the
State. At the same time, the state has a substantially larger aided-sector footprint, with
29.2 % of students enrolled in private aided schools, nearly three times the all-India
average of 11.3 %. The State also reports a higher proportion of students in recognised
private unaided institutions (46.6 %) compared to 31.9 % nationally, underscoring the
coexistence of a strong private sector alongside a robust public system. This diversified
schooling landscape—combining larger public, aided, and recognised private institutions—
continues to provide wide-ranging access and choice for households, while reinforcing
the State’s commitment to expanding educational opportunities.

6.11.2 As per the latest UDISE+ 2024-25 data, Tamil Nadu excels in school infrastructure and
teacher resources. With 57,935 schools, 12.52 million enrolments, and 549,850 teachers,
Tamil Nadu represents ~3.9% of India’s schools, 5.1% of enrolments, and 5.4% of

rates. As fewer children are born, fewer enter the schooling system, resulting in lower

6.11.4 Redeployment of teachers from zero enrolment and filling up of vacancies will further
ensure quality and enhance outcomes.

6.11.5 A year-on-year comparison of enrolment ratios shows a moderate but notable decline
between 2023–24 and 2024–25. The sharpest fall occurs at the elementary level (a
reduction of 4.5 percentage points), followed by a 2-point drop at the secondary level. The
decline is consistent across boys and girls, indicating systemic factors—most importantly
demographic changes—rather than gender-driven disparities. However, such a rapid
demographic shift is not reflected in the UDISE+ data (as explained in Box A6.1), which
relies on projections rather than actual counts of children in these age groups, owing to
the absence of Census data after 2011.
6.11.6 Encouragingly, despite the limitations of this data, higher secondary enrolment shows
a marginal improvement, signalling stable retention beyond Class X. Importantly, Tamil
Nadu continues to perform far above the national average at all stages of schooling.

6.11.7 School infrastructure in Tamil Nadu is both comprehensive and adequate, with nearly
all schools equipped with classrooms, drinking water, electricity, libraries, and functional
toilets, supported by low student–classroom ratios and strong digital access as per the
last UDISE+ data (See Box A6. 1 in Appendices).
6.11.8 Nevertheless, the downward movement at the foundational and secondary stages
warrants attention. It underscores the need for more precise population projections to
ensure that GER and enrolment metrics reflect actual demographic patterns and betterinformed policy decisions.

6.11.9 At the higher secondary stage, the GER stands at 83.4 %. Across all levels of schooling,
girls’ enrolment exceeds that of boys, indicating sustained progress in gender equity.
6.11.10 There is also a growing preference for government schools. In 2025–26, government
primary and middle schools enrolled 3.7 lakh new students (LKG–VIII), with many shifting
from private unaided schools. This trend is driven by improved infrastructure, earlyadmission campaigns, and welfare programmes such as the Chief Minister’s Breakfast
Scheme. A strong ecosystem of support—including the Nutritious Meal Scheme, free
uniforms, textbooks, and free bus pass—continues to reinforce equity and access.

144

6.11.11 Post-pandemic learning recovery initiatives, such as Illam Thedi Kalvi along with the Ennum
Ezhuthum Mission, have been instrumental in restoring foundational competencies.
Simultaneously, continuous enhancement of school infrastructure through the Perasiriyar
Anbazhagan School Development Scheme has contributed to renewed public confidence
in government schools.
6.11.12 However, gaps persist in learning outcomes, teacher capacity building, filling up teaching
vacancies and digital inclusion. The policy approach therefore focus quality, inclusive
education for the 6–18 age group, with a renewed emphasis on foundational learning,
digital infrastructure, and inclusive school environments. Besides, the teacher vacant
positions need to be filled.
6.11.13 Finally, ASER illuminates foundational gaps in rural areas to spur advocacy, while SLAS
diagnoses curriculum performance across the state’s school system for precise reforms—
together providing complementary perspectives on Tamil Nadu’s educational landscape
(see Box A6.3 for a comparison between ASER and SLAS findings).

6.12 Higher Education
6.12.1 The state continues to be a leader in higher education, with a diverse ecosystem of 61
universities, 1,632 Arts and Science colleges, 463 engineering and architecture colleges,
and 492 polytechnic institutions and 503 ITIs. The State’s Gross Enrolment Ratio (GER)
remains among the highest in the country reflecting its sustained emphasis on access,
equity, and quality.
6.12.2 Of the many equity-enhancing initiatives, the 69 % reservation policy—distributed
proportionally among Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes, Backward Classes, Most
Backward Classes, women, and religious minorities—has played a significant role in
ensuring fair access to higher education for all sections of society.
6.12.3 Flagship schemes such as Pudhumai Penn, which supports nearly 6.92 lakh girl students,
and Tamil Pudhalvan, which benefits 5.40 lakh boys (as of December 2025), have further
expanded enrolment from government schools into higher education. In addition, the 7.5
% preferential reservation for government school students in professional courses has
strengthened equity.
6.12.4 Through the Perunthalaivar Kamarajar College Development Scheme, investments of
nearly Rs. 980 crore have been made since 2022–23 to upgrade academic buildings,
laboratories, and digital resources. The Chief Minister’s Research Fellowship and Research
145

Grant have provided targeted support, with Rs. 9.61 crore sanctioned for 180 fellows and
Rs. 50 crore allocated to universities in 2024–25. Incubation centres and entrepreneurship
programmes have further strengthened innovation ecosystems.2
6.12.5 Fee waivers, scholarships, and concessions for first-generation graduates, SC/ST students,
and economically weaker sections have widened participation. Post-Matric Scholarships
benefitted over 1.3 lakh engineering and polytechnic students in 2023–24. Government
polytechnics are being upgraded into Centres of Excellence under Industry 4.0 standards,
with Rs. 2,783 crore sanctioned for infrastructure and curriculum modernization.
6.12.6 Strengthening internationalisation, research output, and the global ranking performance
of State universities will be central to positioning higher education as a driver of Tamil
Nadu’s trillion-dollar economy vision by 2030 and to anchoring the State’s transition
towards a high-value, innovation-led knowledge economy, particularly in emerging
domains such as artificial intelligence, advanced manufacturing, biotechnology, clean
energy, and data sciences.
6.12.7 The outlook for the sector underscores the need to enhance quality while deepening
research and innovation through stronger industry–academia collaboration, accelerated
digital transformation, and expanded global partnerships. Scaling up LMS–ERP platforms,
e-resources, and digital libraries will integrate technology more fully into teaching and
research, while collaborations with international universities will support exchange
programmes, joint research, and the adoption of global curricula.
6.12.8 Tamil Nadu’s higher education system must decisively shift towards quality, global
competitiveness, and measurable outcomes. This will be essential to ensure that
expanded access translates into empowerment, and that higher education continues to
serve as a driver of inclusive growth and knowledge-led economic transformation.

6.13 Social Welfare Schemes in Tamil Nadu
6.13.1 The Tamil Nadu is known for implementing many social welfare schemes. for the protection
and promotion of vulnerable and marginalised sections of the society. These schemes
aim to support infants, children, adolescent girl, lactating mothers, women, transgender
and senior citizens.

2 https://www.dtnext.in/news/tamilnadu/higher-edu-in-tn-gets-a-boost-with-naan-mudhalvan-cmrf-834706?utm_
source=chatgpt.com

146

6.13.2 Budgetary allocations for this sector have remained robust, rising from Rs. 3,048 crore
in 2021-22 to Rs. 3,433 crore in 2024–25, with State schemes forming the backbone
of expenditure. Union and CSS contributions, though modest, supplement the State’s
efforts, underscoring Tamil Nadu’s reliance on its own fiscal space to maintain welfare
priorities.
6.13.3 Apart from Pudhumai Penn and Tamizh Pudhalvan scheme, the Chief Minister’s Breakfast
Scheme, which now benefits 20 lakh children (including an additional 3.05 lakh children
after the scheme expanded to 2430 urban government aided primary schools in 2025) has
been a pioneering initiative, complementing the long-standing Noon Meal Programme.
Child welfare has seen notable progress through.
6.13.4 Support for senior citizens remains steady, with pensions and old-age homes providing
critical relief. The Social Security Pension Scheme continues to reach nearly 3.4 million
beneficiaries annually, though certain categories such as old-age pensions show gradual
decline (Table A 3 in Appendices) reflecting demographic shifts.
6.13.5 Transgender welfare has achieved incremental gains, particularly in pension coverage,
yet livelihood-oriented schemes such as self-employment grants remain limited in reach.
Overall, Tamil Nadu’s welfare architecture demonstrates resilience and inclusivity, but the
data point to emerging constraints in scheme uptake, infrastructure adequacy, and fiscal
challenges.
6.13.6 Infrastructure gaps in hostels and elder care facilities require targeted investment and
better planning. Most importantly, monitoring outcomes and providing course correction
will be critical to ensure that welfare translates into empowerment.

6.14 Welfare of Senior Citizens
6.14.1 The population aged 60 years and above accounts for 14 %, the highest in India next only
to Kerala at 15.1 % (SRS 2023). This share is projected to rise further, exceeding one-fifth
of the State’s population by the turn of the next decade, with women forming a slightly
higher proportion among the very old. This demographic transition will progressively
reshape the State’s expenditure profile through higher pension commitments, increased
public health spending, and expanding care economy needs of the aged.
6.14.2 The State has implemented a range of initiatives to strengthen social protection for
senior citizens. Financial security is provided through the Indira Gandhi National Old Age
Pension Scheme, which extends monthly pensions to destitute persons above 60 years
147

from Below Poverty Line households, and the Annapoorna Scheme, which supplies 10
kilograms of rice free of cost every month to indigent elders not covered under the
pension scheme. In addition, livelihood and skilling programmes, including the formation
of Elders Self Help Groups (AGRASR Groups), are being introduced to promote active
engagement and self-reliance among the aged. Of these schemes old age pensions
schemes alone benefits above 13.68 lakh aged people. Tamil Nadu government launched
Anbu Solai (garden of love) on November 10, 2025, featuring 25 day care centres for
senior citizens to their well being.
6.14.3 Healthcare and nutrition have been prioritized through the establishment of geriatric
care centres in all medical colleges and district hospitals, the Makkal Thedi Maruthuvam
scheme that delivers medicines for chronic diseases at the doorstep, and the proposed
Integrated Geriatric Management Scheme to provide nutritious meals and vaccines
through Anganwadi and noon meal centres. Assistive devices such as walking sticks and
hearing aids are also distributed to enhance mobility and daily living, particularly for
those from economically weaker sections.

6.14.4 Shelter and safety measures include grants-in-aid to NGOs for running old age homes that
provide food, shelter, medical care, and recreation, as well as integrated complexes that
house both senior citizens and destitute children to foster inter-generational bonding. In
2024–25, 2,627 beneficiaries were covered under these two schemes, which need to be
further strengthened and expanded.
6.14.5 The state has recently launched the Chief Minister Thayumanavar Scheme, anchored
in the slogan எல்
� ்லலோோருக்ககும் எல்லாமும் (“Everything for Everyone”), to provide
assured access to essential commodities for vulnerable households. The scheme targets
senior citizens aged 70 years and above and persons with disabilities, enabling them to
receive doorstep delivery of monthly PDS ration items free of cost.

148

6.14.6 An estimated 21.7 lakh beneficiaries were identified during the launch, all of whom were
auto-enrolled based on existing ration card data, eliminating the need for applications
or verification. By reducing the physical burden of visiting PDS outlets/Fair Price Shops,
the scheme enhances dignity, convenience, and social protection for elderly citizens
and persons with disabilities. It represents a significant step in advancing inclusive and
humane governance, ensuring that essential entitlements reach those who need them
most. However, the scheme has not pushed the targeted population, and official data
shows that only 40-45% received the commodities through door delivery in August and
September 20253.
6.14.7 In the coming years, as the share of older persons continues to rise, the State will need
to strengthen and expand its pension architecture to ensure comprehensive old-age
income security. Further, in the context of persistently high food inflation and rising
healthcare costs, the pension amount presently provided to over 14 lakh beneficiaries
warrants periodic upward revision and may be supplemented with a Dearness Allowance
(DA) component, aligned with State Government revisions, to protect the real value of
benefits.
6.14.8 As the demographic profile shifts, strengthening institutional capacity, expanding
geriatric and long-term care services, and ensuring gender-sensitive interventions will
be critical to safeguarding the dignity and well-being of senior citizens—reinforcing the
State’s commitment to inclusive and humane development.

6.15 Welfare of Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes
6.15.1 Tamil Nadu has consistently prioritised the socio-economic upliftment of Scheduled
Castes (SCs) and Scheduled Tribes (STs) through a dedicated Adi Dravidar and Tribal
Welfare Department. Despite literacy levels among SCs (73.26 %) and STs (54.34 %)4
remaining below the State average, sustained policy focus has been placed on education,
infrastructure development, economic empowerment, and social justice enforcement to
bridge historical disadvantages.
6.15.2 The Scheduled Caste Sub-Plan and Tribal Sub-Plan provide the institutional backbone for
a comprehensive set of interventions, including welfare schools and hostels, scholarships,
house-site pattas, housing and basic amenities, skill development training, and
enforcement of protective legislations such as the Prevention of Atrocities Act. In 2024–
25, over 1,138 Adi Dravidar schools served more than 77,000 students, supported by 1,331
hostels accommodating nearly 99,000 beneficiaries. Tribal education was strengthened
3 https://www.newindianexpress.com/states/tamil-nadu/2025/Sep/30/pds-on-doorsteps-only-50-received-it-at-home
4 2011 Census
149

through 328 schools, including eight model schools. Post-matric scholarships amounting
to Rs. 1,203.52 crore supported over 1.36 lakh SC students, alongside pre-matric, higher
education scholarships and free bicycles for higher secondary students.
6.15.3 A significant recent initiative is the Annal Ambedkar Higher Education Assistance
Scheme, which supports SC/ST students pursuing postgraduate and research programmes
in leading foreign universities with assistance of up to Rs. 36 lakh per annum. Launched
in 2021–22, the scheme has expanded rapidly, with around 385 students supported by
2025-26 marking a shift from access-based welfare to aspiration-led empowerment and
global human capital formation.
6.15.4 Habitation-level infrastructure has been strengthened under the Iyothee Thass Pandithar
Habitations Development Scheme through roads, water supply, housing facilities, and
free house-site pattas. Budgetary support remains substantial, with allocations of Rs.
3,233.85 crore for SCs and Rs. 681.83 crore for STs in 2024–25 and utilisation nearing 100
%, reflecting strong administrative capacity.5
6.15.5 Going forward, the focus must move from expenditure to outcomes as ensuring
measurable empowerment will be critical to aligning SC/ST advancement with Tamil
Nadu’s overall development trajectory.

6.16 Welfare of Backward Classes, Most Backward Classes,
and Minorities
6.16.1 Tamil Nadu’s welfare framework for Backward Classes, Most Backward Classes, Denotified
Communities, and Minorities is among the most comprehensive in the country, anchored in
a robust 69 % reservation system and supported by steadily rising budgetary allocations.
A wide range of scholarships, hostels, educational institutions such as Kallar Reclamation
Schools, and targeted welfare boards have significantly expanded access to education,
housing, and social support. Institutional mechanisms such as TABCEDCO, TAMCO, and
TALMEDCO have strengthened economic empowerment through loans, entrepreneurship
support, and self-employment schemes, while minority-specific initiatives, including
hostels, pilgrimages, and religious infrastructure support, have reinforced inclusivity.
Going forward, the focus must again shift from coverage to outcomes to strengthen the
socio-economic advancement for these communities.
5 Adi Dravidar and Tribal Welfare (ADTW) Department Policy Note

150

6.17 Conclusions
6.17.1 Tamil Nadu’s development experience demonstrates that social justice has functioned
not as a by-product of growth but as one of its central drivers. By institutionalising
education, health, food security, and social protection as enforceable social rights rather
than residual welfare provisions, the State has enabled historically excluded groups—
including women, Scheduled Castes and Scheduled Tribes, first-generation learners,
and rural households—to participate in modern economic sectors. Sustained public
investment in schooling, higher education, nutrition, and healthcare has expanded
human capabilities at scale, reflected in near-universal school enrolment, broad-based
access to tertiary education, and extensive nutrition programmes. These interventions
have done more than alleviate deprivation: they have reduced household risk, stabilised
consumption, broadened aspirations, and strengthened labour productivity. In turn, they
have supported structural transformation and sustained domestic demand, challenging
the conventional dichotomy between equity and efficiency by demonstrating that rightsbased social policy can serve as foundational economic infrastructure.
6.17.2 As the state confronts demographic ageing, rapid urbanisation, climate stress, and
technological change, the central challenge lies in transitioning from access-led
expansion to quality-driven and productivity-enhancing social investment. This entails
strengthening public health systems for chronic and geriatric care, expanding life-cycle
skill formation and digital literacy, and managing urban transitions through investments
in housing, mobility, and basic services, while simultaneously securing fiscal space for
inclusive welfare amid evolving intergovernmental fiscal constraints. Meeting these
demands requires not retrenchment but strategic renewal: programmes must become
more outcome-oriented, resilient to shocks, and aligned with emerging socio-economic
realities, with greater emphasis on effectiveness and long-term returns rather than
coverage alone. The coming decade will test the adaptability of Tamil Nadu’s social
development model; yet its experience continues to affirm that social justice is not a
fiscal burden on development but a core catalyst of productivity, dignity, and shared
prosperity.

151

Chapter 7

Infrastructure and Energy
7.1 Introduction
7.1.1

Tamil Nadu has been experiencing rapid urbanization. Urbanization presents a structural
challenge for infrastructure provision in so far as the rapid concentration of population
and economic activity tends to exceed the absorptive capacity of existing planning and
financing systems.

7.1.2

The expansion of urban economies generates simultaneous demand for transport
networks, housing, water and sanitation systems, energy supply, and digital connectivity,
requiring coordinated, capital-intensive investments. However, fiscal constraints and
market rigidities impede timely infrastructure delivery. The problem is compounded
by uncertainty surrounding migration flows and environmental risk, which complicates
demand forecasting and investment prioritisation.

7.1.3

Infrastructure remains a core driver of Tamil Nadu’s economic growth, supporting
productivity, mobility, urbanisation, and service delivery. The State continues to prioritise
capital investment to strengthen sectoral infrastructure. Capital expenditure for 202526 is projected at Rs. 57,231 crore, a 22.4% increase over 2024-25, covering transport,
energy, roads and bridges, urban development, water supply, water bodies, and social
infrastructure.

7.1.4

With expanding infrastructure networks, the focus is shifting from individual assets to
integrated systems. Rapid urban growth, rising mobility demand, climate-risks, and service
expectations require resilient, efficient, and adaptive infrastructure. Digital tools, data
systems, and coordinated planning are increasingly critical for design, implementation,
and maintenance.
This chapter reviews 2025-26 infrastructure performance, highlighting capital expenditure,
key initiatives, and implementation progress.

152

I. Urban Infrastructure
7.2 Industrial & Economic Corridors
7.2.1

Tamil Nadu is the only State in India with all its districts covered under industrial corridor
projects. Tamil Nadu Industrial Development Corporation Ltd. (TIDCO) is the nodal agency
for the development of the Chennai–Bengaluru Industrial Corridor, Chennai–Kanyakumari
Industrial Corridor, Kochi–Coimbatore–Bengaluru Industrial Corridor and the Defence
Industrial Corridor in the State. As the designated nodal agency, TIDCO coordinates
industrial and infrastructure development activities across these corridors with various
Government agencies.

7.3 Road Based Public Transport
7.3.1

Tamil Nadu prioritises transport infrastructure for economic development and social
inclusion, emphasising sustainability, affordability and institutional coordination to build
a future ready transport ecosystem.

7.3.2

State Transport Undertakings (STUs) provide road-based public transport within Tamil
Nadu and to neighbouring states. In Chennai, services are operated by the Metropolitan
Transport Corporation (MTC), while six regional transport corporations manage inter
district and municipal services. SETC (State Express Transport Corporation) is a specialized
corporation within the Tamil Nadu STU system that exclusively focuses on long-distance,
inter-city, and interstate travel (typically routes over 300 km).

7.3.3

Modernisation and greening of the public transport fleet are ongoing priorities. As of
January 2026, MTC has put 780 new buses on road. MTC operates 255 electric buses. By
February 2026, the target is 625 electric buses across five depots, featuring cameras,
panic buttons, and USB charging. MTC plans to strengthen first and last mile connectivity
to Metro stations, operating small buses on 25+ routes. Feeder services have been
expanded via the mini-bus scheme, covering approximately 2,000 routes. Digital payment
facilities were introduced across multiple districts in 2025-26, enabling cashless ticketing
and improving commuter convenience.7.3.4 In November 2025, MTC launched the “Bus
First” campaign to promote bus travel in Chennai, aiming to reduce congestion and
pollution. The campaign includes a mobile bus exhibition across the city.

153

7.3.4

In November 2025, MTC launched the “Bus First” campaign to promote bus travel in
Chennai, aiming to reduce congestion and pollution. The campaign includes a mobile bus
exhibition across the city.

Box 7.1 Comprehensive Mobility Reforms in Chennai
The Government of Tamil Nadu has approved Rs. 2.5 lakh crore Comprehensive Mobility Plan
for Chennai Metropolitan Area, with a 25-year horizon. The plan prioritizes people over vehicles,
expand public transport, and promote seamless multimodal integration. A central initiative is
the Chennai One Mobility App, a unified digital ticketing platform supporting QR-based travel
across MTC buses, Chennai Metro and suburban rail. Within days of launch, the app recorded
more than 1 lakh downloads and 4,500 ticket bookings in a single day. All 2,900 MTC buses are
integrated into the system, making a major step toward digital-firs, multimodal public transport
in Chennai.
7.3.5

As part of its modernisation programme, the Metropolitan Transport Corporation has
computerised bus scheduling at depots, enabled real-time data relay through the
mobile application, and created commuter-facing infrastructure by installing digital
display boards at key bus stops to provide real-time arrival and departure information.
In parallel, GPS devices have been installed in phases across the entire fleet following
the implementation of intelligent transport software. The commissioning of a modern
Command and Control Centre has enabled commuters to track buses in real time through
the Chennai Bus mobile application.

7.3.6

The World Bank report Chennai’s Urban Mobility Transformation commends MTC for
operational and service delivery reforms. Under the Chennai City Partnership, MTC aims
for financial sustainability by improving fleet utilisation to 87%, deploying 1,000 electric
buses under the GCC model, and modernising 3,833 buses with enhanced multimodal
integration.

7.4 Expressways
7.4.1

The 10.1 km G.D. Naidu flyover in Coimbatore was inaugurated in October 2025. The flyover
was constructed over a period of five years at a cost of ₹1,791.23 crore. The project
comprises a four-lane main carriageway and a six-lane expanded road at ground level,
along with eight up and down ramps located at four points. Being the longest flyover
in South India, the structure incorporates features such as sinus plate expansion joints,
rainwater harvesting systems in every span to enhance groundwater recharge, drip
irrigation on the flyover, sprinkler irrigation at ground level, and energy efficient lighting.
154

7.4.2

Chennai - Bengaluru expressway project is expected to be completed this year.

7.4.3 The Central Government has approved a realignment of the Chennai–Surat Expressway
under the Bharatmala Pariyojana, reducing its planned length from about 1,271 km to
nearly 900 km, with the corridor now proposed to end at Nashik rather than Surat. This
modification has been attributed to land acquisition constraints and delays in securing
environmental clearances in the Nashik–Surat stretch. Notwithstanding the shorter
alignment, the six-lane, access-controlled expressway continues to be envisaged as a key
corridor for enhancing high-speed connectivity between southern and central India.
7.4.4 The Chittoor–Thatchur Expressway (NH-716B) is a 116.1 km, six-lane access-controlled
greenfield corridor linking Chittoor in Andhra Pradesh with Thatchur in Tamil Nadu and
connecting to the Chennai Peripheral Ring Road. Developed at a cost of ₹3,998 crore under
Bharatmala Pariyojana Phase I, the project is intended to provide direct, congestion-free
access to the Kamarajar and Kattupalli ports. As of early 2026, around 80% of construction
has been completed, with most stretches nearing readiness for traffic. The expressway
is expected to be fully operational by December 2026, with select sections likely to open
earlier.
7.4.5 The Tamil Nadu State Highways Authority has proposed a 14.2 km four lane elevated
corridor between Thiruvanmiyur and Uthandi along the East Coast Road at an estimated
cost of ₹2,100 crore. International tenders have been floated for the toll-based corridor,
which is designed to cover 13 major congested junctions and reduce travel time on the
stretch from about one hour to 15 to 20 minutes, while also providing an alternate route
to ease traffic pressure on the OMR.
7.4.6 The double-decker elevated corridor between Maduravoyal and Chennai Port is under
construction and is expected to be completed in the first half of 2027. The project aims
to ease traffic congestion along a key urban freight and commuter corridor in Chennai.

7.5 Electric Vehicle Infrastructure
7.5.1

Tamil Nadu is India’s fastest-growing EV manufacturing hub. Global players such as Ola
Electric, Ather Energy, BYD, TVS, Vinfast, Ampere, and several battery companies have set
up factories in the state.

7.5.2

According to India Electric Mobility Index (IEMI) by Niti Aayog, Tamil Nadu ranks second
in EV Research and Innovation Status while ranking 12th in Charging Infrastructure and
readiness and 14th in Tranport Electrification Progress.
155

7.5.3

Tamil Nadu has revised its Electric Vehicle Subsidy Policy this year, bringing major updates.
The policy provides subsidies of up to ₹10,000 for electric scooters and ₹20,000 for highperformance electric motorcycles, along with road tax and registration fee exemptions
for electric cars, special incentives for women, students, and delivery workers, a fiveyear interest subsidy on commercial EV loans, and additional support for fleet operators
such as taxis and logistics services. In 2024-25 Tamil Nadu ranked fourth in number of
electronic vehicles registrations with 61,344 vehicle registrations, next only to Maharashtra
(1,45,576), Uttar Pradesh (1,02,197) and Karnataka (85,765) .
