7.5.4 Greater Chennai Corporation has launched a major initiative to promote clean mobility by
installing electric vehicle (EV) charging stations in prominent public spaces. Implemented
in partnership with Tamil Nadu Green Energy Corporation Limited and Relux Electric
Private Limited, the first phase will establish 9 stations in high-footfall areas, each
accommodating 2 cars and 3 two-wheelers simultaneously, with fast charging facilities
for four wheelers. This marks the start of a broader State plan to deploy 500 EV
charging stations across Tamil Nadu, prioritising accessible public locations to encourage
widespread EV adoption.

156

Box 7.2 Support for Electric Mobility and Gig Workers
To promote EV adoption among informal sector workers, the State has launched a scheme
through the Tamil Nadu Platform Based Gig Workers Welfare Board and has allocated a total
of Rs. 4 crore. The scheme provides Rs. 20,000 subsidy to 2,000 registered gig workers for
purchasing new electric two-wheelers, reducing the upfront cost of transition. Additionally, the
State plans roadside facilities at 10 locations on State highways, offering EV charging, power
supply, food stalls, restrooms, first aid, and basic accommodation to support long-distance EV
travel and commercial logistics.

7.6 Vehicle Registrations
7.6.1

According to Vahan Portal, till 2025, Tamil Nadu recorded 3.51 crore new vehicle
registrations, underscoring strong demand from rising incomes, urbanisation, and
increased goods movement. The State also processed 1.01 crore service transactions
during the year. Revenue collection reached Rs. 10,679 crore in 2025, highlighting the
transport sector’s significant fiscal contribution. Registrations, transactions, permits and
revenue were concentrated in major hubs such as Chennai (West, South and Central),
Tambaram, Poonamallee, Coimbatore (North and South) and Salem, driven by industrial
activity and high population density.

7.7 Railways and Metrorail
7.7.1

Tamil Nadu’s railway sector is expanding rapidly, driven by rising passenger demand,
investment in new lines and doubling projects, improved suburban connectivity, and
modern logistics services. The Southern Railway zone, headquartered in Chennai, oversees
extensive operations with nearly 1,000 trains running daily from Chennai, including over
170 passenger services.

7.7.2

Southern Railway carried 57.9 crore originating passengers during 2025-26, registering
a 6.2% increase over the previous year, while maintaining 92% punctuality. During the
year, 3,570 special train trips were operated in addition to regular services. The Southern
Railway revenue had a steady growth during 2025-26 (9 months up to December), with
gross earnings of Rs. 9,846 crore, an increase of 7.5% over the corresponding period of
the previous year.

157

7.7.3

Chennai Division spans 699 kms with 157 stations (117 km and 15 stations in AP). Freight
handled is about 14.868 million tons annually. The division operates 837 trains daily,
including EMUs, serving 1.2 million passengers, with 213 suburban trains catering to
commuters. The Chennai Division has launched a dedicated Parcel Cargo Express Train
connecting Royapuram and Patel Nagar (Delhi) under a six-year contract with Rail
Transport Corporation of India. Initially planned for two round trips per month, the service
now operated twice weekly (Wednesdays and Sundays), covering 2,195 km carrying auto
components, leather goods, handloom products, tyres and courier consignments.

7.7.4

Southern Railway has also launched a first of its kind coast to coast parcel express service
linking Tamil Nadu and Kerala. Operating Royapuram and Mangaluru Central, it uses ten
highcapacity parcel vans and two luggage-cum brake vans to transport white goods,
perishables, consumer products, and industrial materials. The service offers faster, costeffective and eco-friendly alternatives to road logistics.

7.8 Chennai Metro Rail Phase II
7.8.1

Chennai Metro Rail Phase II is India’s largest ongoing metro rail project, spanning 118.9 km
at an estimated cost of Rs. 63,246 crore. It comprises three corridors: Madhavaram-SIPCOT
(45.8 km), Lighthouse-Poonamallee Bypass (26.1 km), and Madhavaram-Sholinganallur (47
km), with a total of 128 stations.

7.8.2 Metro usage continue to grow, with 93.27 lakh passengers in October 2025 and 9.32
crore passengers in the financial year up to October. As on February 01, 2026, stationwise passenger flow on Metro Line 02 shows a clear concentration of ridership at major
interchange and terminal stations, with Chennai Airport recording the highest passenger
volume.
7.8.3 Chennai Metro Rail Limited is revising the commissioning plan for Corridor 5 under Phase
II following the completion of trial runs on the Vadapalani to Poonamallee depot section,
which is proposed to enter commercial service by February. Corridor 5, also known as the
Red Line, is a 47 km corridor connecting Madhavaram and Sholinganallur.

158

Box 7.3 Metro and Regional Rapid Transit Expansion
In Tiruchirappalli, Chennai Metro Rail Limited has prepared two metro corridors totalling 45 km.
The State also submitted Detailed Project Reports for metro projects in Coimbatore (Rs. 10,740
crore) and Madurai (Rs. 11,368 crore). The Union Government, however, cited the 2017 Metro
Rail Policy population requirement of 2 million, noting Coimbatore (15.84 lakh) and Madurai (15
lakh) fall short. Tamil Nadu argued that metro projects were approved for smaller cities like
Agra, Patna, and Bhopal, and for Tier II cities such as Kanpur, Nagpur, Pune, Indore, and Surat,
highlighting the need to consider Coimbatore and Madurai due to their rapid growth. The
State also plans regional rapid transit solutions with semi-high-speed rail services (160km/h).
Feasibility studies by Chennai Metro Rail Limited will cover: Chennai-Villupuram via Chengalpattu
and Tindivanam (167 km); Chennai-Vellore via Kanchipuram (140 km); and Coimbatore-SalemTiruppur-Erode (185 km). Studies will also assess ropeway-based transport in Mamallapuram,
Udhagamandalam and Kodaikanal to improve tourism, while safeguarding ecological zones.

7.9 Merger of MRTS with Chennai Metro Rail Limited
7.9.1

In 2025, a major institutional reform was approved with the transfer and merger of
the Chennai Mass Rapid Transit System (MRTS) with Chennai Metro Rail Limited (CMRL).
The MRTS operates 20 km corridor from Chennai Beach to Velachery, along with a 5 km
extension to St. Thomas Mount, carrying about one lakh passengers daily. The transfer
covers all MRTS assets, including tracks, bridges, signaling and electrification systems,
land and buildings. State-funded assets are being transferred directly to the Government
of Tamil Nadu and CMRL, while Southern Railway land will be leased to the State.

7.10 Civil Aviation
7.10.1 Tamil Nadu’s airport network is relatively well distributed regionally, with major facilities
spread across the north, west, centre, and south of the State rather than being
concentrated in a single corridor.
7.10.2 Tamil Nadu plays a key role in India’s aviation network, with four international airports
(Chennai, Trichy, Coimbatore, Madurai) and two domestic airports (Salem and Tuticorin),
providing strong regional and international connectivity. These airports support industrial
exports and passenger mobility, positioning the State as a major aviation hub in southern
India.
7.10.3 To support airport expansion and modernisation, land valued at Rs. 2,938 crore has been
acquired and handed over to the Airports Authority of India (AAI) for ongoing projects at
Chennai, Coimbatore, Madurai, Tiruchirappalli, and Thoothukudi airports. Additional land
159

acquisition worth Rs. 350 crore is underway for Salem Airport. Work on the proposed
Greenfield Airport at Parandur, near Chennai, has been fast-tracked and a new airport
has been proposed at Rameswaram – where AAI has begun the pre-feasibility study and
two sites have been shortlisted (Uchipulli and Keelakarai).

7.10.4 Chennai International Airport’s Phase II expansion, costing Rs. 1,207 crore, is scheduled for
completion and includes a new 86,135 sq. m terminal with upgraded passenger processing
systems. Phase I, covering 1.49 lakh sq. m at a cost of Rs. 1,260 crore, was commissioned
in April 2023. Upon completion of both phases, the airport will operate four integrated
terminals with a capacity of over 3.5 crore passengers annually and more than 500
aircraft movements per day.
7.10.5 At Tiruchirappalli International Airport, nearly 90 % of land acquisition has been completed
for extending the runway from 8,136 ft to 12,500 ft. Thoothukudi Airport has completed a
major upgrade, including extension of the runway to 3,115 metres, introduction of nightlanding facilities and commissioning of a 17,340 sq. m Chettinad-style terminal with 21
check-in counters, 3 aerobridges and an increase in passenger handling capacity from
156 to 1,400 passengers per hour. At Coimbatore International Airport, AAI is developing
a 75,000 sq. m terminal, expanding aircraft bays and taxiways, and proposing a runway
extension to 3,800 metres.
160

7.10.6 The Tamil Nadu government has suffered a setback in its plans to establish an airport
in Hosur, as the Ministry of Defence has once again denied airspace clearance citing
operational requirements of Hindustan Aeronautics Limited, which controls the region’s
airspace. The State had earlier sought relaxation in June last year and later submitted
a detailed technical justification in November, but the request was rejected without
discussions. Meanwhile, the Ministry of Civil Aviation has sought an impact study to
assess whether the proposed airport would affect passenger traffic at Bengaluru or
Salem airports, to be conducted by TIDCO.
7.10.7 Based purely on connectivity gaps and economic geography, priority should be given to
developing new airports in the east coast delta region (Nagapattinam-MayiladuthuraiKaraikal) and the far south-west belt (Tenkasi-Sankarankovil-Western Ghats edge). While
these regions may not generate immediate international traffic, they present strong
potential for regional airports to support balanced development and improve accessibility.
7.10.8 Bengaluru Airport continues to exceed Chennai International Airport’s international
passenger traffic in South India, handling 694,550 passengers in December 2025.

7.11 Ports
7.11.1

Tamil Nadu’s port sector continued to expand steadily, supported by improvements in
cargo handling, hinterland connectivity and logistics infrastructure. Chennai Port Authority
and Kamarajar Port Limited together handled 103.37 million metric tonnes (MMT) of
cargo, in FY 2024-25, registering a combined year-on-year growth of 6.7 %. Chennai Port
handled 54.96 MMT, recording a 6.5 % increase, while Kamarajar Port handled 48.41 MMT,
continuing its positive growth momentum.

7.11.2 V.O. Chidambaranar Port Authority has entered into a joint venture with the Government
of Tamil Nadu to develop a large shipbuilding and heavy industries cluster at Tuticorin,
marking a significant step towards strengthening India’s domestic maritime manufacturing
capabilities.The port authority announced that a special purpose vehicle titled National
Shipbuilding and Heavy Industries Park, Tamil Nadu has been incorporated as a 50:50
partnership between VOCPA and the State Industries Promotion Corporation of Tamil
Nadu. The proposed cluster will focus on the development of commercial shipbuilding and
ship repair facilities, along with allied heavy industries, with the objective of enhancing
domestic capacity and reducing dependence on overseas shipyards.

7.11.3 The financial performance of Tamil Nadu’s major ports remained strong in 2024-25, with
combined operational income exceeding Rs. 2,200 crore. Chennai Port recorded revenues
of Rs. 1,094.34 crore, while Kamarajar Port generated Rs. 1,138.42 crore. Kamarajar Port
also achieved a record Profit After Tax of Rs. 539.33 crore, crossing the Rs. 500 crore
mark for the first time, supported by higher capacity utilisation, improved efficiency and
sustained demand from energy and bulk cargo segments.
7.11.4 A major initiative is the Multi-Modal Logistics Park at Mappedu in Tiruvallur district, being
developed over 184.27 acres at a cost of Rs. 1,424 crore. The project will provide integrated
freight handling, warehousing, value-added services and road-rail connectivity. Phase 1
is expected to reduce logistics costs for industrial clusters in Chennai, Sriperumbudur,
Oragadam and Tiruvallur, while strengthening export competitiveness through Chennai
and Kamarajar ports.
7.11.5 Overall, the maritime sector’s performance in 2024–25 reflects sustained investment
in port efficiency, rising industrial output and strengthening of logistics network.
With ongoing port expansions, the upcoming Mappedu Multi-Modal Logistics Park and
improvements in operational capacity, Tamil Nadu is well positioned to consolidate its
role as a leading gateways for India’s international trade.

7.11.7 Vessel Turnaround time at Chennai Port has improved significantly from 2.5-3 days
historically to 38.34 hours during April-October 2025. Under the Maritime India Vision
2030, the port targets turnover around time of under 20 hours. Container handling
productivity averages 20-25 crane moves/hour, compared to 40-45 moves in Singapore.

container handling capacity to 2.2 million TEUs.

7.12 Digital Infrastructure: TANFINET
7.12.1 The BharatNet project is being implemented in the State by TANFINET with the support
of the Union Government. The project envisages providing Optical Fibre Cable (OFC)
connectivity to 12,525 Gram Panchayats. Connectivity has been extended to around
11,800 villages, with the entire network completed in approximately 10,000 villages. The
Chief Minister, while addressing the Gram Sabha meetings held on October 11, utilised the
BharatNet-TANFINET.

7.13 Urban water supply
7.13.1 In Chennai, 50 free smart water ATMs were launched in June. Equipped with IoT based
technology, the water dispensing units worth ₹6.04 crore have been set up at public places
including bus stands, government hospitals, commercial spaces and near educational
institutions.
7.13.2 Under Jal Jeevan Mission-aligned schemes, 1,11,45,898 rural households have been
provided with drinking water pipeline connections, with 13,80,767 more in progress. The
status of drinking water supply in corporations and municipalities is detailed below.

7.14.2 Mamallan Drinking Water Reservoir Project: Given the limited scope for developing
additional surface water sources in Chennai and much of Tamil Nadu, the State
Government has proposed the ₹342.6-crore Mamallan Drinking Water Reservoir Project
to augment Chennai’s water supply. The proposed reservoir, located near Mamallapuram
in Chengalpattu district, is designed to harness surplus flows from the Manamathy group
of tanks and the Kovalam sub-basin.

165

7.15 Artificial Groundwater recharge Programme
7.13.1 According to the Tamil Nadu Groundwater Resource Assessment 2024, 392 out of 1,202
firkas are classified as over-exploited, with groundwater extraction exceeding 100 % of
annual recharge, marginally lower than 395 firkas in 2023. Districts with high extraction
rates, including Ranipet, Vellore, Tirupattur, Salem, Dharmapuri and Villupuram, have been
prioritised. The Water Resources Department (WRD) has approved a large-scale project
for the installation of artificial recharge shafts to enhance groundwater percolation and
stabilise the water table in over-exploited and critical blocks across the State. Under the
project, about 576 recharge shafts will be installed in 23 districts. These structures are
expected to minimise evaporation losses and accelerate recharge compared to natural
processes. The impact of each recharge shaft is expected to extend over a radius of 500
metres to 1 kilometre.

7.16 Streetlights
7.16.1 A total of 9.5 lakh streetlights are maintained across 24 corporations (excluding Greater
Chennai Corporation) and 137 municipalities. Additional streetlights are being provided
to meet the standard of one streetlight every 30 metres. Considering the LUX values,
wattage, safe installation, maintenance and environmental safety, energy efficient LED
streetlights are being installed in ULBs to reduce energy consumption by 40%.
7.16.2 A total of 32,364 km of roads are maintained in corporations and municipalities. Funds
under various schemes are allotted for priority restoration of roads older than 5 years.

7.17.2 Panjappur Integrated Bus Terminus, Tamil Nadu’s first and largest fully air-conditioned
bus terminus, was inaugurated in 2025 and is now operational for both mofussil and city
buses. Additionally, between 2022-25, 18 new bus stands (Rs. 176.27 crore) and 45 upgrades

7.18 Parks
7.18.1 During the period 2021-22 to 2024-25, 466 parks were improved under Kalaignar
Nagarpura Mempattu Thittam, Namakku Naame Thittam, Capital Grant Fund, 15th Finance
Commission Grants and AMRUT 2.0 at a total cost of Rs. 182.13 crore.

167

Box 7.4 Climate Resilient Infrastructure: Sponge Parks
Sponge parks are green spaces that absorb rainwater and stormwater through permeable
surfaces, helping recharge aquifers and reduce waterlogging and flash floods.
In Chennai, 57 sponge parks were constructed in 2023–24, with 30 more planned for 2024-25.
The 2025-26 Budge allocated Rs. 88 crore for seven additional parks, including one at R.A.
Puram Playground with permeable pavements, drains, and filtration systems.
The Chennai Metropolitan Development Authority plans sponge parks at Thoraipakkam
Corporation Park and Elango Nagar playground (Rs. 9.45 crore) to manage rainwater, recharge
groundwater, and reduce flooding, supporting long-term water resilience and climate adaptation.

II. Rural Infrastructure
7.19 Basic Infrastructure
7.19.1 Tamil Nadu has long prioritized rural development, aiming to provide rural residents
with infrastructure and amenities comparable to urban areas. Recent initiatives cover
roads, drinking water, housing, governance, sanitation, social equality, and employment,
positioning the State as a leader in rural transformation. For rural development, the
state had allocated Rs. 13,131.8 crore for 2025-26 (BE), which is 15% more than the actual
expenditure for 2024-25, Rs. 11,403 (and Rs. 8,922 crore in 2023-24).
7.19.2 To strengthen local governance, administrative sanction limits have been raised: Village
Panchayats from Rs.2 lakh to Rs.5 lakh, Block Panchayats from Rs.10 lakh to Rs.20 lakh,
and District Panchayats from Rs.20 lakh to Rs.50 lakh. This empowers local bodies,
reduces delays, and supports participatory planning.
7.19.3 A total of 600 Village Secretariats have been established to coordinate public service
delivery. Special grants of Rs.69 crore have been provided to 690 panchayats near urban
areas, and Rs.30 crore to 278 hilly panchayats to improve infrastructure and service
delivery.
7.19.4 Anaithu Grama Anna Marumalarchi Thittam (AGAMT), inducted in 2006, aims to improve
the overall basic infrastructure facilities in all village panchayats in a phased manner. It
was relaunched in 2021-22 as AGAMT-II. Rejuvenation of ponds, upgrading streets and
lanes, installing streetlights, ensuring livelihood and marketing facilities, among others,
are undertaken under this initiative. The second phase of the Anaithu Grama Anna
168

Marumalarchi Thittam was sanctioned Rs. 1,087 crore during 2025-26 to be implemented
in 2,329 village panchayats – aiming to allocate a minimum of 30% funds for SC/ST
habitation areas. The Revival of Rural Libraries Scheme renovated 7,924 libraries at
Rs.176.02 crore, supporting literacy, education, and community access to knowledge,
especially for students and women.

7.20 Rejuvenation of Minor Irrigation Tanks
7.20.1 Water management has been strengthened through revitalization of minor irrigation
tanks. Of the 5,000 tanks targeted for 2024–25, 3,194 are being restored with State
funding (Rs.213.07 crore) and 706 through NGOs and voluntary organisations (Rs.45.39
crore), improving irrigation, groundwater recharge, and climate resilience.

7.21 Rural Roads and Connectivity
7.21.1 Rural connectivity has expanded significantly. Under Mudalvarin Grama Saalaigal
Membattu Thittam, 12,572 km of roads across 9,696 projects have been improved. Under
the Pradhan Mantri Gram Sadak Yojana, 4,182 km of roads, 947 segments, and 83 bridges
were undertaken. Under the NABARD Rural Infrastructure Development Fund 505 km of
roads and 308 bridges have been completed..

7.22 Housing Infrastructure
7.22.1 Under Kalaignarin Kanavu Illam, 2 lakh concrete houses have been sanctioned (Rs.7,000
crore), with over 1 lakh houses completed. Hon’ble Chief Minister has announced the
sanctioning of additional 1 lakh houses in the current year. Repairs to 1,48,634 rural
houses were sanctioned (Rs.1,041.32 crore), with 1,03,177 completed. Reconstruction of
pre-2000 houses under Mudhalamaicharin Veedugal Marukattumana Thittam is underway
for 15,485 houses.

7.23 Sanitation and Drinking Water Supply
7.23.1 Since 2021, 3,38,357 Individual Household Latrines have been constructed. For households
without space, 2,123 Community Sanitary Complexes (Rs.127.05 crore) and 6,684 Mini
Community Sanitary Complexes near schools and public spaces (Rs.195.43 crore) have
been established.

169

III. Energy
7.24 Power Demand
7.24.1 Tamil Nadu’s energy sector is expanding to meet rising household, industrial, and
service electrification demands. The State emphasizes reliable, high-quality power and
leverages substantial renewable energy potential. It ranks third in India for total installed
renewable capacity and second in wind power. The Government targets 50% of total
energy consumption from renewables and a 70% reduction in carbon emissions by 2030,
aiming for a resilient, low-carbon energy ecosystem.
7.24.2 Power demand continues to rise with industrial growth, urbanisation, and household
electrification. Peak demand and daily energy consumption have increased year on year.
To meet this, Tamil Nadu has expanded its electrical infrastructure—adding substations,
HT and LT lines, and distribution transformer capacity. Initiatives such as the Mass
Maintenance Programme and conversion of overhead lines to underground cables
have improved service quality, reduced outages, and ensured voltage stability in low
voltage and overloaded areas, strengthening the distribution network for reliable and
uninterrupted power supply.

172

7.25 Renewable Energy Status in Tamil Nadu
7.25.1 Over the years, Tamil Nadu has been able to make remarkable progress in the energy
sector, emerging as one of the leading states contributing significantly to the expansion
of renewable energy generation, as well as installed capacity in the country. As of 31st
March 2025, the total installed capacity of the state stands at 25241.36 MW, placing
it third among the states in the country after Gujarat and Rajasthan (see Figure 9.4).
According to the Renewable Energy Statistics 2023-2024, the installation of Renewable
Energy (RE) capacity exceeded that of the non-RE sector in Tamil Nadu as early as 202021. The contribution of RE to the state’s total installed capacity significantly increased
from 44.7% in 2017-18 to 55.49% by 2023-2024. Over the past decade, the renewable
energy sector has experienced a remarkable transformation, with solar power assuming
a central role overall with an increase in installations from 156.83 MW to 8,211.38 MW,
marking an expansion of 5,135.85% and a Compound Annual Growth Rate (CAGR) of 55.24%
between 2014 -15 and 2023 - 24. Wind energy also constitutes a major share of total
renewable energy capacity in Tamil Nadu, with 11739.91 MW generated from wind power,
making the state one of the largest contributors to wind power in the country. Table 9.2
below shows the renewable energy installed capacity in the state as of 30.11.2025.
7.25.2 Tamil Nadu stands as a leader in India’s energy transition, with an increasing renewable
energy capacity, which stands at 25,241 MW as of late 2025. Yet, a significant disparity
exists between potential power generation and actual generation. While renewable energy
constitutes a major part of the state’s total installed capacity, they typically contribute
only 25% to 27% of the total electricity consumed annually. This gap is primarily driven by
the Capacity Utilization Factor (CUF). Unlike thermal power plants, which provide steady
baseload power, solar and wind energy sources are intermittent, with solar energy supply
being constrained by daylight and wind energy being susceptible to high seasonality.
Furthermore, the state faces infrastructure bottlenecks where the existing grid cannot
always transport surges of power from southern districts to northern industrial hubs.
Since large-scale battery storage is still in its early stages of development, the state
must maintain coal and nuclear plants to ensure a stable power supply. Additionally, the
system often prioritizes stable power sources to prevent blackouts.

Wind Energy
7.25.3 Wind energy forms the backbone of Tamil Nadu’s renewable portfolio, with an installed
capacity of 12014.34 MW and an estimated onshore potential of 95 GW. Annual generation
is around 13,000 million units, meeting nearly 10% of the State’s consumption. The State
recorded its highest wind generation peak of 5,899 MW on 30 July 2024.
7.25.4 Tamil Nadu leads India in wind repowering, being the first State with a structured
programme. One hundred older turbines have been repowered to 27.67 MW, and a total
of 2,113 turbines (1,514.77 MW) will benefit from the 2024 Repowering, Refurbishment, and
Life Extension Policy, expected to boost generation by 25%. The Muppandal Wind Farm
remains one of the world’s largest onshore wind farms, highlighting the State’s longterm leadership in wind power.

Box 7.5 Renewable Energy Transition in Tamil Nadu
Tamil Nadu leads in wind and solar energy but faces structural and technological challenges.
In wind, ageing low-capacity turbines from the 1990s occupy high-potential sites; repowering
these requires upgraded infrastructure and clear decommissioning guidelines. Offshore wind
potential is estimated at 35 GW, needing improved assessment, grid strengthening, and
integration with storage and green hydrogen. Solar capacity is growing, yet remains a fraction
of potential. Emerging technologies—bifacial modules, perovskite, dye-sensitized, nanocrystalbased, concentrated, and transparent solar cells—can improve efficiency and applications, but
adoption depends on grid integration and commercial viability.

175

Energy storage is critical for managing renewable variability. Tamil Nadu has approved 500 MW
(1,000 MWh) of standalone grid-scale Battery Energy Storage Systems (BESS) under TNGECL with
Union Viability Gap Funding and plans an additional 1,000 MW. With a total installed capacity of
~44 GW (renewables ~55%), studies indicate ~13 GW of storage will be needed by 2035. Storage
helps balance supply and demand, easing operational stress on thermal plants and supporting
Renewable Purchase Obligation compliance through wind, solar, hybrid, and round-the-clock
renewable procurement.

Offshore Wind
7.25.5 Tamil Nadu has one of the world’s strongest offshore wind zones, with an estimated
35 GW potential between Kanyakumari and Nagapattinam. The State has approved
procurement of 2,000 MW at Rs.4/unit, with the first 500 MW (3 MW turbines) under
implementation. Offshore wind is expected to become a key component of the State’s
long-term energy mix as grid integration and transmission capacity expand.

Solar Power
7.25.6 Tamil Nadu ranks fourth in India for solar capacity, with 10,159.61 MW installed (rooftop
and grid-connected) as of March 2025. The State recorded its highest solar peak of
6,579 MW on 16 February 2025 and generated 13,216 million units between April 2024
and February 2025. Rooftop solar continues to expand under net-metering frameworks,
supported by Rs.28.40 crore in incentives, though production remains limited to daylight
hours.

Battery Energy Storage Systems (BESS)
7.25.7 BESS enables time-shifting of solar generation, reducing dependence on thermal power.
Two projects with combined investment of Rs.200 crore are expected to deliver a levellized
tariff of Rs.6.49/unit, saving Rs.90 crore over 25 years. TNGECL plans 1,000 MWh under
viability gap funding and an additional 4,000 MWh over the medium term. Storage is
critical for grid stability, peak load management, and extending solar availability beyond
daylight.

Hydro Power and Pumped Storage
7.25.8 Hydro supports reliability during peak demand and variable renewable output. In 2024–25,
hydro generation target of 4,329 million units was surpassed by January. Key assets were
restored or uprated: Kadamparai pumped storage, Kodayar II (40 MW), Moyar (12→14 MW),
Kundah II, and mini powerhouses (256.61 million units). For 2025–26, hydro generation
176

target is 4,497 million units, with ongoing upgrades including penstock replacement and
automated trash rack cleaning.

Thermal and Hydro Additions Under Execution
7.25.9 Thermal projects totaling 2,640 MW and hydro projects of 520 MW are underway to
provide baseload support, complementing renewable generation and ensuring supply
adequacy in 2025–26.

Coal Handling and Shipping Operations
7.25.10 Coal for thermal plants is transported from Paradip, Gangavaram, Dhamra, Vizag, and
Gopalpur to Ennore, Tuticorin, and Karaikal. In 2023–24, 19 million tonnes were moved
efficiently by sea, achieving a single-voyage record of 92,500 tonnes. Strategic vessel
replacement and shifting bunkering operations saved Rs.63.86 crore. A web-based
system monitors vessel movement, coal quantities, and payments, with further upgrades
planned.

Green Energy Corridor
7.25.11 The Green Energy Corridor enables renewable power evacuation to load centres. Phase
I was supported by Rs.529.31 crore from the National Clean Energy Fund and Rs.573.26
crore from KfW. Phase II, funded by Rs.237.52 crore (Union grant), Rs. 473.12 crore (KfW
loan), and Rs.296 crore TANTRANSCO equity, will establish extra high-tension lines and
substations to strengthen intrastate renewable transmission, reduce curtailment, and
support the 50% renewable consumption target by 2030.

7.26 Conclusions
7.26.1 Tamil Nadu’s infrastructure trajectory in 2025–26 reflects a shift from asset creation
to systems-based development centred on scale, sustainability, and integration. Rising
capital expenditure is strengthening transport, urban services, logistics, energy, and rural
connectivity through digital platforms, institutional reforms, and climate-responsive
design, signalling a transition toward resilient infrastructure. A people-centric mobility
strategy—marked by electric buses, metro expansion, multimodal integration, and
Chennai’s Comprehensive Mobility Plan and One Mobility App—advances clean and
inclusive transport, while rail scaling, MRTS–CMRL integration, logistics parks, port
growth, and airport expansion reinforce the State’s trade and connectivity role. Urban
investments in water, sanitation, flood mitigation, and climate-adaptive design, alongside
rural road upgrades, tank revitalisation, housing, and local governance programmes,
promote liveability and spatial inclusion. Energy initiatives—anchored in wind and solar
177

leadership, storage, offshore development, pumped hydro, and transmission upgrades—
support the transition to a reliable, renewable-heavy grid. Collectively, these efforts
constitute a coherent model linking economic expansion with environmental responsibility,
though outcomes will hinge on coordination, fiscal discipline, regulatory efficiency, and
implementation capacity; if addressed, Tamil Nadu is positioned to consolidate its status
as one of India’s most advanced and resilient infrastructure economies.

178

Chapter 8

Modern Services and Tourism
8.1 Introduction
8.1.1

India is the world’s seventh-largest exporter of services, with its share in global services
trade more than doubling from 2% in 2005 to 4.3% in 2024 – as per the Economic Survey
2025-26. Digital technologies have enabled services to be delivered remotely, and scaled
rapidly, reducing geographical constraints and generating new value, particularly in
digitally enabled and knowledge-intensive activities.

8.1.2

Southern states, especially Karnataka, Kerala, Tamil Nadu, and Telangana, are among the
most service-intensive economies. Supported by mature urban centres such as Bengaluru,
Chennai, and Hyderabad, Karnataka, Tamil Nadu, and Telangana have consolidated their
position as service-led economies. Tamil Nadu stands out for its relatively diversified
services base spanning finance, healthcare, and logistics.

8.1.3

Tamil Nadu has a strong and diversified services economy anchored in financial services,
real estate, and IT-enabled sectors, supported by higher urbanisation and relatively high
per capita income. The services share in GSVA (nominal) increased from 50.5% in 2011–12
to 54.7% in 2024–25. This reflects strong integration across cities such as Coimbatore,
Tiruppur, and Madurai, which function as hubs for technology, finance, logistics, healthcare,
and education.

8.1.4

The IT and ITeS sector, particularly in Chennai, has expanded on the back of a skilled
workforce and advanced infrastructure. Software export units, back-office operations,
and fintech firms have driven growth in high-value services. Tamil Nadu’s coastal location
has further supported logistics development through multimodal infrastructure centred
on the Chennai and Tuticorin ports.

8.2 Information Technology and Digital Services
8.2.1

Tamil Nadu continues to consolidate its position as a leading IT and ITeS destination.
Chennai, Coimbatore and Hosur anchor a large software and back-office ecosystem
supported by engineering talent robust digital infrastructure. Multiple submarine cable
landing stations position Chennai as a major digital gateway, aligning with NITI Aayog’s
assessment of Tamil Nadu as an emerging hub for data centres, cloud services and digital
innovation. These strengths have enabled growth of fintech, e-commerce, telemedicine
and online public services.
179

8.2.2 To promote the growth of the IT sector in Tier II and Tier III cities of Tamil Nadu, TIDEL
NEO Ltd. has floated tenders for the construction of mini TIDEL Parks in Tirunelveli and
Kanniyakumari districts, at an estimated cost of ₹37 crore each. These mini TIDEL Parks
are expected to generate employment opportunities for local youth in the respective
regions. A mini TIDEL Park was inaugurated in November at Abdullapuram in Vellore
district.
8.2.3 At present, Salem, Thanjavur, Thoothukudi, Villupuram, and Pattabiram near Avadi also
host mini TIDEL Parks. The foundation stone was laid for a ₹37 crore TIDEL Neo Park
in Tiruvannamalai, while mini TIDEL Parks in Virudhunagar and Nagapattinam were
announced in 2025. In addition, tenders have been issued for a mini-TIDEL Park in the
Nilgiris, which is expected to generate around 1,000 jobs.

8.3 Global Capability Centres
8.3.1

Tamil Nadu is positioning itself as a leading destination for Global Capability Centres,
with growth extending beyond Chennai to tier-II cities. Chennai continues to serve as the
anchor city, hosting major GCCs across technology, life sciences and financial services. At
the same time, tier-II cities are gaining traction, supported by talent availability, policy
support and infrastructure readiness. Coimbatore accounts for about 10.7% of the State’s
GCCs, followed by Madurai at around 1.4 %. Emerging GCC ecosystems are also evident in
Tiruchirappalli and Vellore, reflected in early hiring activity and academic partnerships.

8.3.2 AISHE 2021–22 data place Tamil Nadu among the top five States in India in STEM graduate
output. With over five lakh graduates annually, including around two lakh engineers, the
State’s higher education ecosystem continues to support the expansion of knowledgebased services and industries. To sustain this growth, the State has notified the
Global Capability Centre Policy 2024–27, providing payroll subsidies for high-skill jobs,
infrastructure support for innovation facilities and reimbursement of intellectual property
filing costs, complemented by existing startup, R&D and tier-II city support policies.

8.4 Tamil Nadu’s Potential for Artificial Intelligence (AI)
8.4.1

Tamil Nadu has initiated systematic efforts to deploy Artificial Intelligence (AI) across
government and key sectors. Workshops across 55 government departments identified
55 priority AI use cases, of which 15 show high impact potential, particularly in education,
health, and road safety.

180

Box 8.1 AI in Agriculture
Tamil Nadu has launched AI- and drone-based pilots for paddy cultivation, covering
over 870 acres, reporting yield gains of 15–20 %. These gains are driven by AI-based
soil analysis, pest monitoring, and agronomic advisories, delivered to farmers
through platforms such as WhatsApp, with reported accuracy exceeding 90 %.
The pilots use machine learning models integrated with satellite and drone data to
assess soil health, nutrient status, irrigation needs, and pest risks. Multi-temporal
imagery enables prediction of crop growth stages, supporting timely irrigation
and nutrient application. Drone-based surveillance has improved pest detection
accuracy by about 90 %, contributing to reduced pesticide usage.
These initiatives are implemented under the State’s Digital Agriculture Mission,
with pilots concentrated in the Cauvery Delta and other irrigated regions. Tamil
Nadu Agricultural University (TNAU), Coimbatore, has entered in to a collaboration
with Valyria Visions Private Limited, an artificial intelligence–driven agricultural
analytics start-up, for trait prediction in maize. The partnership focuses on the
development of AI-based modules for predicting crop traits using the university’s
extensive phenotypic, genotypic, and environmental datasets. The initiative aims to
support data-driven decision-making for plant breeders and researchers, enhance
precision agriculture practices, and contribute to sustainable food production.
8.4.2 In the health sector, key AI initiatives include ePathway for cataract detection
and COVA, a mobile-based tool for oral disease screening. These applications
support early diagnosis and improve access to public health services.
8.4.3 The State is developing an AI open data platform (under the Tamil Nadu
AI Mission) to provide sanitized, standardized datasets for start-ups and
developers. Over 100 datasets spanning sectors such as agriculture,
education, and traffic management are being cleaned and standardized for
this purpose at a budget of Rs. 14 crore in the first two years. (Minister
for Information Technology and Digital Services at the Tamil Nadu Global
Startup Summit – October ‘25).

181

Box 8.2 AI in Health
Tamil Nadu is collaborating with IIT Madras to develop AI solutions embedded in
medical equipment across hospitals to assist clinicians by flagging cases for further
review. International evidence shows that AI toovls for tuberculosis (TB) screening
meet or exceed World Health Organization (WHO) performance standards. Building
on this, the State is exploring AI-based TB screening to improve the efficiency and
cost-effectiveness of mass screening, particularly in areas with limited medical
personnel.

8.5 Telecom and Digital Infrastructure
8.5.1

Tamil Nadu has one of the India’s strongest telecom ecosystem, characterized by high
tele density, extensive fibre connectivity, and the presence of major service providers.
Chennai’s role as a key telecommunications landing point strengthens statewide
digital capacity. High internet penetration supports online banking, digital payments,
e-governance and remote services, reinforcing the State’s transition to a mature digital
service economy. NITI Aayog identifies telecom and digital infrastructure as key enablers
of Tamil Nadu’s long-term services growth.

8.6 Banking and Financial Services
8.6.1 Tamil Nadu’s banking sector is undergoing rapid transformation driven
by digital adoption, network expansion, and a renewed focus on financial
inclusion. The State hosts several major banks that evolved from communitybased institutions into national players. These banks have expanded branch
networks, upgraded digital platforms and strengthened MSME credit delivery.
8.6.2 Within the State’s banking and financial services ecosystem, State-owned Non-Banking
Financial Companies (NBFCs) continued to play a critical role in infrastructure financing
during the year. Tamil Nadu Power Finance and Infrastructure Development Corporation
Ltd. (TNPFC) and Tamil Nadu Transport Development Finance Corporation Ltd. (TDFC)
remained the principal NBFCs catering to the power and transport sectors, respectively.
TDFC extended credit exclusively to State Road Transport Corporations. Reflecting its
financial profile, TNPFC’s fixed deposits were assigned an ‘IND A’ rating with a stable
outlook during the year.
8.6.3 The overall ACP performance for the quarter ended September 2025 stood at ₹5 lakh
crore, against the Annual Credit Plan target of ₹9 lakh crore for FY26. The banks in the
State of Tamil Nadu achieved 56.77 % of the Annual Target as of September 2025.
8.6.4 By the end of September 2025, districts with highest CD ratio were Ariyalur (279.76),
Kallakurichi (265.54), Dharmapuri (252.64), Theni (236.49) and Perambalur (233.95).
Districts with the lowest CD ratio were Trichy (110.65), Kanchipuram (102.6), Vellore (89.62),
Chengalpet (84.83) and the Nilgiris (84.06). Chennai stood at 115.15.1
8.6.5 Tamil Nadu leads MSME credit with 43% of loans to manufacturing, per the MSME Pulse2
May 2025 report. It contributes significantly to India’s ₹35.2 lakh crore portfolio, up 13%
YoY to March 2025, with private banks holding a 39% share. District patterns reveal
strong manufacturing focus. Coimbatore sees private banks at 45% of loans, with 48% to
manufacturing; Chennai has 44% from private banks, directing 31% to manufacturing and
26% to long-term capital; Tirupur allocates 75% to manufacturing (36% packing credit)
and counts 46% low-risk borrowers (CMR 1-3); Madurai emphasizes trade at 36%, with
PSBs at 34% and 48% moderate-risk (CMR 4-6).​This long-term, manufacturing-oriented
lending positions Tamil Nadu as a driver of India’s formal MSME growth.

1https://www.slbctn.com/CD_RATIO.aspx
2https://www.sidbi.in/msme-pulse
183

8.6.6 In Tamil Nadu, over 4.71 lakh Self-Help Groups (SHGs) have received ₹34,200 crore in
credit assistance under the SHG-Bank Linkage Programme (SHG-BLP) during the 202425 fiscal year, nearing the ₹35,000 crore target, with an average loan size of ₹7.22 lakh.
For 2025-26, initiatives are underway to credit-link all eligible SHGs. This year, the state
pioneered the issuance of identity cards to Women SHG members across India, bolstering
institutional support for more than five lakh groups and improving their access to financial
and social benefits.

Figure 8.2: Progress Under Service Area Credit Plan – Target & Achievement
up to September 2025

Source: ACP Target Achievement Sheet

8.6.7 Cyber investment scams and digital arrest scams have shown a rising trend among
cybercrime cases reported in Tamil Nadu in recent years. Cyber investment scams involve
fraudulent online schemes that induce individuals to invest in fictitious or deceptive
opportunities by promising abnormally high returns. A comparison of cases reported
between January and August indicates a clear increase in cyber investment scams.
Reports on the National Cyber Crime Reporting Portal rose from 11,024 cases in 2024 to
13,287 cases during the same period in 2025. Digital arrest scams constitute the second
largest category of cyber fraud cases. Reported cases increased from 2,762 between
January and August 2024 to 4,439 during the corresponding period in 2025. Alongside the
increase in reported cases, awareness and reporting of cybercrime has also improved.
The average daily call volume handled by the 1930 cybercrime helpline has increased to
about 1,600–1,800 calls per day, compared to 600–800 calls per day in 2024.
184

8.7 Logistics and Warehousing
8.7.1

Tamil Nadu has emerged as a major logistics and distribution hub owing to its strategic
location, extensive road and rail networks, and strong port infrastructure. The State’s
multimodal ecosystem includes three major ports and an extensive highway network,
supporting both domestic and international trade.

8.7.2

NITI Aayog3 identifies Tamil Nadu as a leading State in logistics efficiency, driven by
large warehousing clusters, integrated industrial corridors, and efficient urban transport
systems. The Chennai region plays a central role in automobile logistics, e-commerce
distribution and export-oriented activities. Continued investment in road connectivity,
urban transport, and port-linked industrial development is expected to further strengthen
logistics performance.

8.7.3

The Government of Tamil Nadu has released the Tamil Nadu Warehousing Policy, 2026
to strengthen the logistics sector by promoting large-scale, balanced, and diversified
warehousing development across the State, with particular emphasis on Delta and
Category C districts and Tier II and Tier III cities. The policy is aligned with the State’s
long-term economic vision and the objectives of the Tamil Nadu Logistics Policy.

8.7.4 Tamil Nadu, with over 40,000 factory units, ranks among the leading States for
warehousing transactions and accounts for approximately 19 % of national logistics
investment. Chennai, Coimbatore, and Hosur have emerged as major warehousing clusters.
The policy seeks to expand storage solutions across commercial, urban, cold storage, and
agro-based segments, while streamlining regulatory and compliance requirements for
developers and operators.
8.7.5 The policy focuses on strengthening existing warehousing clusters and promoting new
developments in under-served regions. It adopts a commodity-based approach aligned
with the State’s industrial and agricultural profile and places emphasis on Ease of Doing
Business through simplified approvals and industry status for warehousing assets.
Interventions are structured under six themes: integrated greenfield development,
brownfield capacity expansion, public–private partnership promotion, sustainable and
smart warehousing, commodity-specific warehousing, and Ease of Doing Business. The
policy promotes warehouse development in government-operated industrial parks and
integration with proposed multi-modal logistics parks and dry ports, alongside clusterbased development along industrial, defence, and connectivity corridors.
3 https://niti.gov.in/sites/default/files/202510/Indias_Services_Sector_Insights_from_GVA_Trends_State_level_Dynamics.pdf
185

8.8 Tourism
8.8.1

Tamil Nadu remains one of India’s most visited destinations, supported by its cultural
heritage, religious centres, natural landscapes and diverse tourism offerings. Tourism is
designated priority sector in Tamil Nadu’s development strategy. Domestic tourist visits
increased from 286.01 million in 2023 to 306.84 million in 2024, reaffirming Tamil Nadu’s
position as the second most visited State, after Uttar Pradesh (with 646.81 million).
Foreign tourist arrivals stood at 1.162 million in 2024. The State accounted for 10.41 % of
domestic and 5.55 % of major foreign tourist visits in 2024.

8.8.2 The Government of Tamil Nadu organised the Tamil Nadu Global Tourism Summit 2026 at
Mamallapuram in Chengalpattu district on February 2 and 3, 2026. The summit served as
a platform to promote tourist destinations across the State and to strengthen public–
private partnerships in the tourism sector. The summit brought together domestic
and international investors, tour operators, tourism entrepreneurs, and stakeholders
from the tourism and trade sectors to explore opportunities for tourism infrastructure
development and innovative tourism experiences. The Tamil Nadu government has signed
MoUs worth nearly ₹22,800 crore with private sector players to strengthen the State’s
tourism ecosystem, with investments expected to generate around 66,000 jobs. A major
focus of the investments is on medical and sports infrastructure, reflecting the State’s
push to diversify tourism.
8.8.3 The Government of Tamil Nadu has identified 40 locations for development as tourist
sites, with a focus on ecotourism and unexplored destinations. Alongside its cultural
and heritage assets, the State’s 1,076 km-long coastline offers substantial potential for
tourism expansion.
8.8.4 The State Industries Promotion Corporation of Tamil Nadu Limited (SIPCOT) has proposed
setting up an eco-tourism park at Top Sengattupatti in the Pachamalai hills of the
Eastern Ghats, marking a shift from its traditional focus on industrial parks to tourism
infrastructure development. Working in coordination with the Tourism Department and
the Tamil Nadu Tourism Development Corporation, SIPCOT has begun identifying sites
with tourism potential across the State and has earmarked a 3.47-acre land parcel at Top
Sengattupatti, where ecotourism activity has recently increased. Once the land is vested
with SIPCOT, the corporation plans to develop the eco-tourism park and lease plots on a
long-term basis to hospitality players for establishing hill resorts or hotels, with the aim
of improving infrastructure and boosting short-stay tourism in the Pachamalai region.

186

8.8.5 A major cultural milestone was achieved with the renaming of the Saint Thiruvalluvar
statue at Kanniyakumari as the Statue of Wisdom, announced during its silver jubilee
celebrations. The renaming honoured Tamil heritage and the universal values represented
by Thiruvalluvar. To marks the occasion, 77 metre-long glass bridge connecting Vivekananda
Rock and the statue was inaugurated at a cost of about Rs.37 crore. To manage rising
visitor numbers, additional passenger boats services from Chinna Muttam Port have been
announced. These initiatives highlight the State’s commitment to heritage conservation
and improve visitor access.

8.8.6 Domestic tourist arrivals in 2025 are large in scale and widely distributed across districts,
indicating a geographically dispersed and resilient tourism system. High and consistent
inflows in districts such as Chennai, Chengalpattu, Coimbatore, Madurai, Tiruchirappalli,
and Tiruvallur reflect the combined role of urban centres, transport hubs, pilgrimage
destinations, and regional economic activity.
8.8.7 Seasonal variation in domestic tourism is limited, with substantial visitor movement
observed throughout the year, including during summer and monsoon months. This
stability suggests that domestic tourism is driven not only by leisure travel but also by
religious visits, education, healthcare, and social travel, making it less sensitive to climatic
conditions.

8.8.9 Foreign tourist arrivals exhibit a high degree of spatial concentration. A limited set of
districts, namely Chennai, Chengalpattu, Madurai, Thanjavur, Kanniyakumari, Tiruchirappalli,
Ramanathapuram, and Tiruvannamalai, account for most international visitors. These
districts correspond to international gateways, heritage locations, and major religious
centres. Several districts record negligible foreign tourist arrivals across multiple months,
indicating that international tourism remains confined to established corridors rather
than being evenly distributed across the State.
8.8.10 Seasonal variation is significantly more pronounced in foreign tourist arrivals than in
domestic tourism. Foreign arrivals peak during the winter months, particularly in
November and December, and decline sharply during the summer and monsoon period,
with August recording the lowest levels. Domestic tourism, by contrast, shows relatively
stable movement across seasons, with substantial visitor volumes even during the
summer months. This difference reflects the greater sensitivity of foreign travel to
climatic conditions and international travel patterns.
8.8.11 The data also indicates that districts with strong religious and heritage significance
consistently attract higher levels of foreign tourists than destinations positioned primarily
for leisure. Connectivity alone does not appear sufficient to attract international visitors.
The presence of well-defined tourism circuits, cultural interpretation, and established
destination identity plays a more decisive role in shaping foreign tourist inflows.
188

8.8.12 Overall, the tourism pattern in 2025 highlights a structurally resilient domestic tourism
base alongside a narrowly concentrated and seasonal foreign tourism segment.
Strengthening foreign tourism will require targeted interventions focused on circuit
development, destination branding, and service quality, rather than uniform expansion
across all districts.
8.8.13 Environmental restoration efforts include Phase I of the Tholkappia Poonga (Adyar Eco
Park) project, which has transformed 58 acres of degraded creek estuary into an urban
wetland supporting biodiversity, research and environmental education. Eco tourism
remains a key focus, with a 52 acre Eco Park under development at Udhagamandalam
Race Course at a cost of Rs. 70 crore, featuring spice gardens, urban forestry, bird
watching zones and nature trails, positioning Ooty as a major eco-tourism destination
8.8.14 The State continues to invest in preserving Tamil literature and cultural heritage through
initiatives such the Thirukkural Global Translation Project (Rs. 1.33 crore), Tamil literary
translation Project (Rs. 10 crore), digitisation of palm-leaf manuscripts, development
of machine translation tools and organisation of Tamil book fairs in India and abroad.
Heritage outreach programmes target younger Tamil diaspora communities.
8.8.15 Archaeological research is being expanded to establish the antiquity of Tamil civilisation,
with recent findings confirming iron smelting 5,300 years ago. Excavations across Tamil
Nadu and in neighbouring States will employ advanced scientific techniques, supported
by an allocation of Rs. 7 crore in 2025-26.
8.8.16 Tamil Nadu remains a leading destination for medical value tourism, accounting for about
25 % of medical tourists visiting India, with around 15 lakh foreign patients annually.
Advanced medical infrastructure, including robotic surgery facilities, and major hospitals in
Chennai, Madurai, Coimbatore and Vellore reinforce the State’s national and international
healthcare reputation. Chennai alone accounts for nearly 80 % of foreign patients.
Transparency in organ donation and high patient satisfaction have strengthened the
State’s global standing. Experts recommend expanding e-medical visas, accreditation,
PPP-based medical clusters and specialised pay wards in government hospitals. In
recognition of the government’s pioneering efforts to strengthen home-based healthcare
delivery, the Makkalai Thedi Maruthuvam scheme was conferred the U.N. Interagency
Task Force Award in 2024.
8.8.17 Food tourism is rapidly growing segment, showcasting Tamil Nadu’s five culinary
traditions-Thanjavur, Mudaliar, Chettinad, Kongu Nadu and Tamil Sahibu, through curated
food trails, cooking experience and culinary circuits, strengthening the State’s position in
culinary tourism.
189

8.8.18 Tamil Nadu Department of Tourism organised the 2025 Tamil Nadu Travel Mart, which
attracted over 5,000 participants. Digital and multimedia campaigns have been
strengthened to increase global visibility.
8.8.19 Tamil Nadu hosts four UNESCO World Heritage Sites, including the Great Living Chola
Temples, Group of Monuments at Mahabalipuram, Nilgiri Mountain Railway, and Western
Ghats sites, representing outstanding achievements in architecture, engineering and
biodiversity.

8.9 Conclusions
8.9.1

Tamil Nadu’s services sector has become a cornerstone of economic transformation,
combining scale, diversity and technological depth. With over half of GSDP originating from
services, the State ranks among India’s most service-intensive economies. Its strength
lies in the breadth of activities spanning IT, finance, healthcare, logistics, education and
tourism, supported by interconnected urban hubs such as Chennai, Coimbatore, Madurai
and Hosur that integrate regional economies into national and global value chains. Digital
infrastructure and human capital underpin this transformation: Chennai’s role as a digital
gateway, backed by submarine connectivity and a skilled workforce, has driven growth in
IT, ITeS, data centres and cloud services, while enabling fintech, e-commerce, telemedicine
and e-governance. Financial services and healthcare add resilience, with regionally rooted
banks expanding digital finance and MSME credit and world-class healthcare systems
supporting medical value tourism alongside strengthened public provisioning. Logistics,
education and tourism broaden the sector’s base: strategic geography and multimodal
connectivity position Tamil Nadu as a logistics hub, while a large education system
sustains skilled labour supply for modern services.

8.9.2 Tourism recovery grounded in heritage, ecology and gastronomy continues to generate
employment and regional spillovers even as reforms target sustainability and quality
improvements. Despite strong performance, challenges persist in tourism infrastructure,
including overcrowding, sanitation gaps, shortages of trained guides and uneven service
quality, particularly in public facilities. Public–private partnership models are widely
viewed as a means to improve operational standards and visitor experience. Chennai’s
urban tourism potential also remains underutilised following the discontinuation of
initiatives such as hop-on hop-off buses and curated city tours. The Tourism Department
has therefore urged districts to propose upgrades in infrastructure, guide services, digital
accessibility and curated outdoor experiences, with industry stakeholders emphasising
that sustained competitiveness depends on improving overall visitor experience.

190

8.9.3 As the State advances toward its goal of a USD 1 trillion economy by 2030–31, services
will remain central. Future growth will depend less on expanding assets alone and
more on embedding resilience, intelligence and adaptability within service systems. By
aligning technological progress with inclusive governance and continuously improving
infrastructure and service quality, Tamil Nadu is well positioned to sustain high-value
growth, deepen global integration and broaden the social reach of its modern services
economy.

191

Chapter 9

Ecology and Environment
9.1 Introduction
9.1.1

Tamil Nadu has diverse geography, ranging from fertile plains and extensive coastline to
several ecological hotspots. With a total of 1,30,058 sq kms, Tamil Nadu has a highly rich
landscape, which includes the Western Ghats, Eastern Ghats, the Central Plateau, and
the Coastal Plains, along with several biodiversity hotspots, forests, high peaks, low-lying
hills, and fertile agricultural lands. The state has a long coastline of about 1,069 kms along
the Bay of Bengal along with key river systems that primarily flow eastwards and forms
alluvial plains that are best suited for agriculture, specifically, the Kaveri River delta. Tamil
Nadu experiences a tropical climate, with the average temperature ranging between
21.6°C and 31.8°C, and the Northeast Monsoon contributing to the annual rainfall.

Box 9.1 Tamil Nadu’s Environmental Governance – At a Glance
Tamil Nadu has emerged as one of India’s leading states in environmental governance and climate
action, combining strong institutions, forward-looking policies, and large-scale implementation
capacity. The State ranks among the top contributors to renewable energy installed capacity
nationally and was an early mover in integrating renewables into its power mix. It has also
demonstrated regulatory efficiency, placing third nationally in the number of environmental
clearances issued in 2024–25, with one of the lowest average processing times.
Tamil Nadu has built one of the country’s most comprehensive environmental monitoring
frameworks, covering air, water, coastal, and industrial pollution, with improving trends observed
across several polluted industrial areas. The State has taken early leadership in sub-national
climate governance through dedicated institutions such as the Tamil Nadu Green Climate
Company and mission-mode initiatives on climate change, wetlands, green cover, and coastal
restoration.
With the highest number of Ramsar sites in India, a large and well-managed protected area
network, pioneering policies on invasive species, and large-scale wetland, mangrove, and coastal
ecosystem restoration, Tamil Nadu has integrated biodiversity conservation with climate
resilience and livelihoods. Together, these efforts position the State as a national frontrunner
transitioning from environmental management to long-term ecological resilience and lowcarbon development.

192

9.1.2

This chapter offers a comprehensive overview of the state’s ecological and environmental
aspects. The subsequent section aims to provide an overview of the changing weather
and climate patterns in the state, impact of climate change and extreme weather events,
and state initiatives. The sub-section on Minerals and Energy Resources highlights
the specific policy interventions in the sector, as well as crucial achievements in the
renewable energy (including green hydrogen) sector. The subsequent section discusses
issues related to pollution and its management in the state. The following section
provides a brief overview of the state of forests and biodiversity in Tamil Nadu. The
chapter concludes with details of the green initiatives and major policy interventions
undertaken by the state government, with possible suggestions for achieving long-term
environmental resilience and sustainability.

9.2 Weather and Climate
9.2.1

Tamil Nadu experiences a predominantly tropical climate characterized by hot summers,
relatively mild winters, and the northeast monsoon, which usually lasts from October to
December. Over the past few years, the state has experienced a rise in temperatures,
with the maximum temperature anomaly exceeding 1°C and the minimum temperature
anomaly exceeding 2°C in 2024, as per the latest India Meteorological Department (IMD)
Annual Report. Tamil Nadu receives highly seasonal and spatially uneven post-monsoon
rainfall, which accounts for approximately 48% of the state’s annual precipitation (Raj
et al., 2025)1. The state also experiences high humidity levels throughout the year,
especially in coastal regions. The high atmospheric moisture experienced even during
the comparatively drier summer months could be a cause of elevated heat-index values,
which also intensifies health risks in urban centres.

A. Weather Patterns
9.2.2 A significant increasing trend of +0.700C/100 years is observed in the state-averaged
annual mean temperature for the period 1901-2024. This trend is more pronounced in
terms of maximum temperature (+0.880C/100 years) compared to minimum temperature
(+0.530C/100 years). The five warmest years on record for Tamil Nadu, in order, are 2019,
2024, 2016, 2017 and 2020. During the post-monsoon season, monsoon season, and the
year 2024, the state received 133 %, 119 % and 127% of its Long Period Average (LPA) rainfall,
respectively. Figures 9.1 and 9.2 below shows the trends in maximum temperature and
minimum temperature, monsoon and annual rainfall departure from LPA, respectively.

1 Raj, Y. A., Balachandran, S., Amudha, B., & Geetha, B. (2025), “Understanding and prediction of Indian Northeast Monsoon over
the years”, Mausam, 76(1), 139-162.

B. Climate Change – Impacts and Vulnerability
9.2.3 A study conducted by the Department of Environment and Climate Change, Government
of Tamil Nadu and Anna University, Chennai provides climate change projections under
the Shared Socioeconomic Pathway 2 - 4.5 and Shared Socioeconomic Pathway 5 – 8.5
scenarios (see Table 9.1 below). The temperature projections indicate that under SPP2–
4.5 scenario, the annual mean maximum temperature in the state is expected to increase
by 0.4°C in the near-century period (2021 – 2050), 1.3°C in the mid-century period (2051
– 2080) and 1.7°C by the end-century period (2081 – 2100). Additionally, heatwave is
projected to increase by 22 days per year in the near - century, 40 days per year in the
mid - century, and 55 days per year in the end – century. The norther districts such
as Chennai, Thiruvallur, Kanjipuram, Vellore, Chengalpattu, and Ranipet are projected to
experience maximum increase by the end of the century.
9.2.4 The annual average total rainfall projections under SPP2–4.5 indicate that rainfall might
increase marginally by 4% in the near-century period, 11% in the mid-century period, and
by 16% in the end-century period. Coastal districts such as Cuddalore, Nagapattinam,
Kanyakumari, and Mayiladuthurai are projected to have maximum change in rainfall by
the end of the century. The table below highlights the projected changes in temperature
and rainfall under SPP2–4.5 as well as SPP2–8.5.
