9.2.5 Tamil Nadu thus faces increasing vulnerability to changing climatic conditions, characterised
by erratic monsoons, frequent droughts, and intensifying heatwaves. A comprehensive
district level vulnerability assessment (DST, 2021)2 suggests that Ariyalur, Perambalur,
Dharmapuri, Ramanathapuram, Nagapattinam, Krishnagiri, Dindigul, Sivagangai, and
Villupuram are relatively highly vulnerable to climate change. From agriculture point
of view, the Southern Agro-Climatic Zone, inclusive of ten districts, namely, Tenkasi,
Tirunelveli, Ramanathapuram, Thoothukudi, Virudhunagar, Sivagangai, Theni, Dindigul,
Madurai, and Pudukottai, is considered particularly susceptible, given the repeated
occurrence of extreme weather events and the dependency on rainfed agriculture
9.2.6 Research findings project a significant decline in the yields of most of the major food
crops in the region, with the losses anticipated due to heat stress and adverse changes in
rainfall distribution. The projected yield changes indicate Sorghum as the most vulnerable
with an expected decline of 13.03%. Groundnut yield is projected to fall by 12.17%, while
Rice and Maize yields are expected to decline by 8.37% and 4.74% respectively. An increase
in temperatures accelerates crop phenological development and reduces critical grainfilling duration, especially for Maize and Sorghum. Rice yield is affected by water stress,
potentially from excess rainfall at harvest. The expected rise in temperature above the
ideal 30°C during flowering and pod filling is projected to be the cause of a fall in groundnut
yields. Black gram yield is an exception, with a projected increase in yields by 8.14%,
attributed mainly to enhanced CO2 fertilisation and extended soil moisture availability
resulting from increased rainy days during its growing season. This underscores the need
for urgent site-specific adaptation strategies, including drought-resilient varieties and
improved water management to enhance crop resilience in the area. Such adaptation
strategies are also required in several other climate sensitive sectors and regions of
Tamil Nadu. Box 1 discusses one such critical public health challenge, viz., heat stress.

2 DST (2021), Climate Vulnerability Assessment for Adaptation Planning in India Using a Common Framework, Department of
Science and Technology, GoI, https://dst.gov.in/sites/default/files/Full%20Report%20%281%29.pdf.

196

Box 9.2 Heat Stress in Tamil Nadu
Tamil Nadu stands on the frontline of climate vulnerability, with heat stress emerging as a
critical public health and infrastructure challenge. A joint study conducted by the Centre for
Climate Change and Disaster Management and Anna University estimated that the annual
number of thermal discomfort days is projected to rise from over 100 days between the
baseline period from 1985 to 2014, to approximately 250 days per year by near the century. The
decadal assessment of Built-up Area and Climate Interactions in Tamil Nadu, published in 2025
by the State Planning Commission, revealed the crucial finding that the average night-time Land
Surface Temperature (LST) in major urban centres increased by nearly 4°C between the 20002005 and 2018-2023 periods. The study also revealed that Chennai’s built-up area increased
from 48% of its total geographic area in 1985 to 74% in 2015, marking a 26% rise over a span of
three decades and indicating a near-saturation in urban land use. The rapid urbanisation, falling
green cover and parallel expansion of built-up area are among the key reasons that lead to a
rise in night-time temperatures in the state, as well as the urban heat island effect.

Source: Decadal Assessment of Built-Up Area and Climate Interactions in Tamil Nadu
The escalating heat stress in the state poses significant health risks, particularly owing to the Urban Heat Island
effect and a lack of nighttime cooling. The reports also identify that cases of health hazards and fatalities are
hugely under-reported or unreported as such adverse impacts are not often attributed or heat stress. Outdoor
workers, children, pregnant women, and people residing in rural and remote areas are particularly susceptible to
heat-related health impacts. While the government has declared heatwaves a state-specific disaster, there is a
pressing need to address heat risk through adequate policy interventions and conscious effort towards addressing
health concerns in the state.

197

9.3 Minerals and Energy
A. Mineral Resources and Environmental Clearance
9.3.1

Tamil Nadu is rich in diverse mineral resources due to its distinctive terrain and natural
landscape, positioning the state as a leading holder of the country’s deposits of vermiculite,
molybdenum, rutile, garnet, and ilmenite. With an estimated valuation of Rs.882 crore,
Tamil Nadu contributes 79% of India’s vermiculite, 66% of its molybdenum, 46% of its
garnet, 34% of its magnesite, and 27% of its titanium minerals. These minerals play a
pivotal role in supporting key sectors of the state’s economy, including construction,
cement, energy, and manufacturing.

9.3.2 The state government has made significant policy interventions, ensuring progress
in sustainable mining practices, waste management, responsible reclamation and
rehabilitation of mines, as well as development of supporting research and technology.
The M-sand policy is a policy that aims at ensuring sustainable mining practices in the
state with several key interventions, including the establishment of a single-window
portal and a centralised monitoring system for M-sand and crushed sand units. The
policy prescribes consistent quality assessments to ensure compliance with prescribed
standards and streamlines the process of obtaining licenses and clearances for quarry
operators. To strengthen the regulation of the quarries and enhance product quality, the
policy also provides a platform that ensures a coordinated oversight by the State Pollution
Control Board, Mines, the Public Works Department, and other related departments.
This joint institutional framework not only contributes to regulatory efficiency but also
ensures transparency with an aim of boosting overall sectoral development.
9.3.3 The state has made significant technological interventions, such as the Mining Tenement
System, Differential Global Positioning System (DGPS) and drone surveillance technology,
and Mineral Management System (MiMaS) to assess the efficiency across the various
stages involved in mining and related activities. The MTS or the Mining Tenement System
for minor minerals automates the lifecycle of mineral concessions and enables applicants
to track their application from its initial stages up to the closure of mines. It develops
images of cities and villages along with survey numbers and details of mining leases,
thus warranting spatial visualisation.

198

9.3.4 The DGPS and Drone Surveys help in creating accurate images of mineral resources, land
boundaries, and mining sites. The government of Tamil Nadu has sanctioned a recurring
expenditure of Rs. 25 crore specifically for the betterment of drone technology with an
intention to measure the volume and extent of analysis of the mines and quarries using
drones, thereby ensuring efficiency and curbing illegal activities in the industry.
9.3.5 The Mineral Management System of the MiMas is an online platform that has been
developed to improve the monitoring and management of mining activities by
streamlining operations, improving transparency, and ensuring regulatory compliance.
The development of the software was entrusted to the Tamil Nadu e-Governance
Agency, which has developed 16 mineral concession modules that facilitate online receipt
of quarry lease applications, tracking of lease applications, and scrutinizing and granting
of lease applications.
9.3.6 The state government has also established a Green Fund to facilitate the reclamation,
restoration, and rehabilitation of abandoned quarries through contributions from the
minor mineral lessees. The contribution rates vary based on whether the minerals will
be utilized within the state or transported to another location, with a 10% seignorage
for within-state operations and 50% otherwise. A total of Rs. 150.48 crore collected
towards the Green Fund has been used for the implementation of projects across the
districts. So far, 377 abandoned quarries have been identified for reclamation/restoration/
rehabilitation.

9.4 Use of Energy across Households and Transport Sector
9.4.1

Tamil Nadu represents a success story in India’s energy consumption landscape,
characterized by a planned and efficient transition towards cleaner cooking fuels.
According to the National Family and Health Survey-5 (NFHS-5, 2019-2021), approximately
83.3% of households in the state utilize clean fuels such as Liquefied Petroleum Gas (LPG),
electricity, or biogas, compared to just 58.6% at the national level. This shift is rooted in
both the Union Government’s Pradhan Mantri Ujjwala Yojana (PMUY) as well as the longstanding state-level welfare initiatives, such as the 2007 free gas stove program, which
laid the infrastructure for LPG adoption. Socio-economically, the educational attainment
of the most educated female member is the strongest predictor of exclusive clean fuel use,
reflecting a shift in intra-household bargaining power and health literacy. Such transition
and use of clean cooking fuels have significant health implications, such as reducing the
odds of Type 2 Diabetes and risks of heart disease among rural women. Presently, urban
centers are witnessing a transition towards electric induction cooktops, driven by Tamil
Nadu’s near-universal electrification and higher reliability in power supply compared to
other states. Future policy interventions must move beyond connection-based targets
199

to focus on affordability and behavioural change. The “last mile” of efficient transition
involves decoupling household energy3 from biomass through sustained nudges and
decentralized renewable energy solutions.
9.4.2 The transport sector in Tamil Nadu is currently undergoing a transformation driven by
rapid urbanization and a strategic shift toward sustainable energy. The surge in vehicle
density is characterized by over 10 lakh new registrations annually, which has historically
intensified the state’s reliance on fossil fuels. To mitigate the environmental impacts
of higher dependency on fossil fuels, including an increase in PM 2.5 concentration and
a rise in Sulphur Dioxide hotspots, especially at high-traffic corridors, the state has
implemented the Tamil Nadu Electric Vehicle (EV) Policy 2023, which has propelled the
region to become the “EV Capital of India.” As of June 2025, the EV penetration stood
at 16.07%, as against a 13.92% in 2024, supported by a rapidly expanding infrastructure
of over 3,000 public charging stations, which was a significant leap from just 151 in
2022. While the current road tax exemption and registration fee waivers for EVs have
successfully driven registrations to over 1.2 lakh units in the 2025-26 fiscal year alone,
sustained policy intervention seems crucial to ensure that the state meets its ambitious
goal of 30% EV penetration by 2030.

9.5 Green Hydrogen
9.5.1

With an aim to develop a 5 million metric tonnes annual production capacity of
green hydrogen by 2023, the National Green Hydrogen Mission fosters the country’s
commitment to energy independence and decarbonisation of major sectors in the
country. It is expected to stimulate over Rs. 8 lakh crore in investments and generate
more than 6 lakh jobs across the country. Tamil Nadu has emerged as a key strategic hub
within this framework, leveraging its vast renewable energy potential to position itself
as a major producer and exporter of green hydrogen and its derivatives, and aims to
contribute at least 40% - 50% to the national targets by 2030. The state government has
collaborated with the Green Hydrogen Organisation to ensure the effective development
and implementation of the Tamil Nadu Green Hydrogen Policy. 4

9.5.2 While the state’s policy aims for parity by 2030, the lack of immediate price competitiveness
creates a set of alternate expectations among domestic users. This uncertainty is
compounded by weak off-take commitments. Although the state has secured massive
3 Malaiarasan, U., K. Thomas Felix, & R. Paramasivam (2019), “Households’ Energy Choice and Demand in Tamil Nadu”, Working
Paper No. 236, Madras Institute of Development Studies, Chennai.

4 Eswaran K., Singh M.K., Leonard A., Hirmer S. (2025), “Adopting Green Ammonia in Tamil Nadu’s Fertiliser Sector: Policy Routes

via Green Hydrogen”, Climate Compatible Growth programme Policy Brief Series https://climatecompatiblegrowth.com/wp-content/uploads/Adopting-Green-Ammonia-in-Tamil-Nadus-Fertiliser-Sector-Policy-Routes-Via-Green-Hydrogen-1-1.pdf

200

MoUs, specifically in Thoothukudi, domestic industries like refining and fertilizers remain
latent in their activities in the absence of adequate incentives without mandated
Hydrogen Purchase Obligations (HPOs), which can justify any green premium. Therefore,
additional management and effective policy interventions that not only promote but also
strengthen such activities are essential for the fulfilment of the goal.
9.5.3 From an environmental standpoint, specific consideration must be extended towards
water demand implications. Producing one kilogram of green hydrogen requires
approximately 9 to 11 litres of demineralized water, but total system requirements can be
higher after accounting for cooling. This necessitates reliance on seawater desalination,
which increases the Levelized Cost of Hydrogen (LCOH) and requires further management.
Additionally, by offering waivers on wheeling charges, electricity duties, and transmission
costs, Tamil Nadu effectively subsidizes private production. If global demand fluctuates
or if technology shifts, rendering current electrolyzer investments obsolete, the state
could face substantial costs through lost revenue and stranded infrastructure assets.
Balancing these fiscal risks against the goal of becoming a global green hydrogen hub
remains the state’s central policy challenge.

9.6 Pollution
9.6.1

Tamil Nadu is a highly industrialised and urbanized state, making pollution a significant
challenge. The main environmental concerns include air and water pollution from
industries, sewage from urban areas, and urban solid waste management. The following
sub-sections examine different types of pollution – including urban solid waste and
plastic waste, and explore the policy measures the state government has implemented
to tackle these issues.

9.6.2 The Tamil Nadu Pollution Control Board (TNPCB) categorises 17 industries into Red (Highly
Polluting), Orange, Green and White (Least Polluting), based on their pollution potentials.
The following table identifies the number of industries falling in each category.

9.6.3 Tamil Nadu’s industrial landscape comprises 6,643 large-scale industrial establishments,

Small industries dominate the industrial landscape with over 84% of total units. Within

number, have a higher pollution impact per unit. Altogether the total number of industries

9.7 Response Strategies for Pollution Control
A. Water Quality and Monitoring Programme
9.7.1

The Tamil Nadu Pollution Control Board (TNPCB), in collaboration with the Central Pollution
Control Board (CPCB), monitors the quality of the state’s water bodies through several
national programmes, including the Global Environmental Monitoring System (GEMS),
Monitoring of Indian National Aquatic Resources (MINARS), Coastal Water Monitoring, and
the National River Conservation Programme (NRCP).

9.7.2

TNPCB operates an extensive water quality surveillance network across 158 locations,
covering 12 major rivers with a total of 71 monitoring stations. These include:
Cauvery River: 27 stations
Bhavani River: 8 stations monitored monthly
Amaravathi River: 3 stations
Tamiraparani River: 13 stations monitored monthly
Palar River: 1 station at Vaniyambadi Municipal Head Works
Other rivers monitored: Adyar, Cooum, Sarabanga, Vasista, Vaigai, Vennar, and
Thirumanimutharu This network enables systematic tracking of water quality trends
across seasons and supports informed environmental management. Moreover, there
are:
Groundwater: 22 monitoring stations (locations) are established to
assess groundwater quality.
Lakes: 8 lakes at Ooty, Kodaikanal, Yercaud, Veeranam, Porur, Poondi,
Pulicat, and Redhills.
Coastal Waters: 34 monitoring stations are deployed to evaluate coastal
water quality.

Sewage Treatment Plants (STPs): 16 STPs are monitored to assess effluent quality.

and canals.

Under NWMP programme, the water quality of Ooty, Kodaikanal, Yercaud, Veeranam,
Porur, Poondi, Pulicat and Redhills lakes are monitored on monthly basis. In addition,
water quality monitoring and management in the state includes:

Chennai City Waterways Monitoring Programme

The TNPCB monitors the water quality of four waterways in Chennai city (i.e.) Adyar
River, Buckingham Canal, Cooum River and Otteri Nallah at 17 locations to assess the
pollution level by collecting monthly samples

Coastal Water Quality Monitoring

The Tamil Nadu Pollution Control Board is monitoring sea water quality at 34 locations,
under NWMP, in 13 coastal districts of the State to assess the sea water quality of coast
lines by collecting monthly samples.

Polluted River Stretches

The Central Pollution Control Board (CPCB) has identified ten numbers of river stretches
in Tamil Nadu as “Polluted River Stretches (PRS)” during 2022.

B. Common Marine Discharge Scheme
9.7.4

The Cuddalore SIPCOT (State Industries Promotion Corporation of Tamil Nadu) Industries
Common Utilities Limited is a common marine discharge scheme, commissioned in January
2000. It collects treated trade effluent from units in SIPCOT Phase I & II, Cuddalore,
including textile processing, pharmaceutical, dye, chemical, pesticide, and miscellaneous
units, and disposes of it into the sea. The facility includes 6 collection sumps, interconnecting pipelines, the pipeline is anchored with concrete blocks and features diffuser
arrangements to release the effluent into the sea at a depth of 8 meters below the
surface at a rate of 500 cubic meters per hour.

C. Sewage Treatment Plants (STPs) by the Local Bodies
9.7.5

Tamil Nadu operates 102 STPs with 2200.75 MLD capacity across urban areas. TNPCB
directs local bodies to expand sewerage systems and install GPS-enabled digital locks
on tanker lorries to prevent illegal sewage discharge into water bodies. Three lorry
registrations were recommended for cancellation in 2023-2024 for violations.

9.8 Solid Waste Management
9.8.1

Local bodies play major roles in the total quantity of solid waste generation. In the
collected solid waste, 53% is treated by the local bodies. The bio-mining of legacy waste
has been initiated by the Local Bodies in 287 locations in the State. TNPCB is monitoring
the environmental (Ground Water and Ambient Air) qualities in and around the old dumpsites periodically.

9.8.2 States, in collaboration with Urban Local Bodies (ULBs) have developed decentralised
pathways to enhance collection mechanisms, segregation of waste at source and disposal
practices. Overall, Tamil Nadu generates 14,600 Tonnes per day (TPD) out of which the
Greater Chennai Corporation alone contributes 5,000 TPD. In comparison, cities like
Indore, Lucknow generates around 2,100 metric tonnes of waste daily. Hyderabad on the
other hand, is the second-highest generator of MSW among all major Indian cities. The
city accounts for 8,000 tonnes per day.

204

Box 9.3 State Industries Promotion Corporation of Tamil Nadu (SIPCOT)
SIPCOT has undertaken a range of environmental and sustainability initiatives across its industrial
complexes, parks, and growth centres. As mandated by environmental clearance norms, SIPCOT
has formed a dedicated Environmental Management Cell to oversee compliance and secure
necessary statutory approvals.
The land-use policy under SIPCOT emphasizes the protection of ecologically sensitive areas such
as forests, wetlands, and coastal zones. Industrial siting is guided by environmental safeguards,
and units are encouraged to adopt Zero Liquid Discharge systems and install continuous emission
monitoring in coordination with TNPCB. It also supports green infrastructure by developing
boundary greenery and recommending 33% green cover within units. SIPCOT promotes rainwater
harvesting, restoration of nearby water bodies, and closure of unauthorized borewells to
conserve groundwater around the industrial areas.
Energy-efficient practices like LED lighting and solar power are being gradually introduced, while
industries are urged to adopt clean technologies and reduce single-use plastics. encourages
allottee units to implement, adopt and use of green and sustainable technologies such as
Solar, Wind, Thermal, Biomass, Electric & Hybrid vehicles, etc. Awareness programs and training
sessions are conducted with regulatory bodies, and plans to issue Green Awards to industries
that recognizes sustainable practices. Overall, SIPCOT aims to foster environmentally responsible
industrial growth through integrated planning, compliance, and stakeholder engagement.

various essential functions such as door-to-door segregated waste collection, sweeping
of bus routes and intertrain roads, waste collection from markets and commercial
areas, segregation and disposal of wet, dry, and hazardous wastes at designated waste
processing facilities. The city also offers decentralized centres that includes, Micro
Compost Centres (MCC) and Resource Recovery Centres (RRC) which are accessible at the

waste, it still needs to bolstered, as the study reveals only 20.03% of the total waste is
segregated.

9.8.5 Though the same approach is followed in Indore under their decentralised waste
management, studying public behaviour and educating them is a distinct approach that
has led to the city emerging as the cleanest in India for the eight consecutive years.
Grassroots innovations such as street plays, wall paintings, FM radio, and cultural events
and ‘Indore 311 app’ etc, were used to spread the messages on cleanliness. In addition to
this fine were also imposed on uncooperative citizens. Partnership with NGOs were also
made to communicate how composting and segregation can be done at home.
9.8.6 Lucknow became a net-zero waste city in 2025 by expanding its scientific waste-processing
capacity and ensuring that all the waste it generates each day is fully treated instead
of being dumped. In addition to its two already existing plants, the Lucknow Municipal
Corporation set up three processing units at the Shivri Solid Waste Management Plant,
each capable of handling 700 MT/day, allowing the city to process its entire daily waste
of around 2,100 MT.
9.8.7 Hyderabad follows a strategic approach in collection, transportation, processing and
disposal of solid waste. In order to inculcate the good practices like waste segregation
and recycling, the city also conducts Information, Education and Communication (IEC
campaigns) with the public and the stakeholders. The city uses about 60–70 % of the
8,000 tonnes of MSW to generate electricity.

B. Plastic Waste Management
9.8.8 According to the Annual report submitted by the Urban Local Bodies (ULBs) for the year
2023-2024, a total quantity of 1,763 tonnes/ day of plastic waste have been generated
from 21 Municipal corporations, 138 Municipalities and 536 Town panchayats in Tamil
Nadu. Out of this 73.5% (1,128 tons/day) of the waste was collected and channelized for
recycling and other purposes. Almost 209 authorized plastic waste recyclers operate in
Tamil Nadu. The total amount of plastic waste channelized for recycling is around 281
tonnes/day. As of 2024 there are an overall 788 PIBOs in Tamil Nadu that have introduced
5.02 lakh tonnes of plastic packaging. Among this, the producers account for 9.09 %
(45,662.94 tonnes), the importers contribute 73.72% (3,70,142.03 tonnes) while 17.18%
(86,285.80 tonnes) coming from the Brand Owners.
9.8.9 The Government issued a ban on single use plastics (SUPs) in 2018. Against this backdrop
Meendum Manjappai initiative of the Government of Tamil Nadu should be looked at
(see Box 4).

Box 9.4 Meendum Manjappai Initiative
The Government of Tamil Nadu has initiated a significant step to address plastic use
and plastic waste generation in the state with the launch of the flagship initiative, Meendum
Manjappai Campaign, as a first step towards minimising plastic use in the state. The campaign,
launched in December 2021, translates to “Back to the Yellow Bag” as an effort to encourage a
public movement to revitalise the use of traditional reusable cloth bags. The campaign focuses
on building public consciousness and inducing a shift in consumer behaviour with the successful
completion of over 2.53 lakh awareness initiatives, inclusive of rallies, street plays, and even
airing educational content in schools. The state introduced Manjappai Vending Machines (MVMs)
in public spaces. As of March 2025, 203 MVMs have been successfully installed across 38 districts,
dispensing approximately 4.2 lakh cloth bags. Additionally, he associated digital application also
ensures ease of access and thereby further strengthens the local supply of cloth bags. Data
from the Tamil Nadu Pollution Control Board (TNPCB) reveals that officials have conducted
over 17 lakh raids on commercial establishments and vendors. These substantial efforts have
resulted in 2684.72 tonnes of SUP seized from the state and a total of Rs 22.85 crore of fine
imposed in this regard. The campaign strategically utilises a combination of awareness drives,
promotion of eco-alternatives and diligent enforcement to efficiently carry out this objective,
making it an effective example to follow.

9.9 Forest and Biodiversity
9.9.1

Tamil Nadu is a land of diverse geographical landscapes with a defined mix of mountains,
plateaus, and plains forming the basis for its rich biodiversity and a repository of unique
flora and fauna. The state can be physiographically divided into 4 major regions, including
of Eastern Ghats, Coastal Plains, Western Ghats, and Central Plateaus.

207

A. Forests
9.9.2 The forest cover in the state is divided into three major groups based on temperature
zones, namely, Tropical Forests, Montana Subtropical Forests, and Montane Temperate
Forests, which are further subdivided into nine forest types according to moisture and
physiognomic variation. Around 26,450.22 sq km of the state is under forest cover (see
Table 9.5 below), and the total forest and tree cover area is 31,820.94 sq km as per the
India State of Forest Report, 2023. The Green Tamil Nadu Mission sets an ambitious
target of further expanding the forest cover to 33% of the total geographical area over
a decade to enhance ecological resilience and sustainability.

9.9.3 The state has designated 9,179.676 sq km, ie, 39.59% of its forest area as Protected Area.
With 5 tiger reserves, 5 elephant reserves, 5 national parks, 3 conservation reserves, and
20 Ramsar sites, the state is a pioneer in forest and wildlife conservation. Additionally,
the state also ensures that diligent measures are taken for the protection of various
endangered birds and animals, as well as wetland habitats, through the establishment of
18 sanctuaries and 17 bird sanctuaries. The state is endowed with a rich faunal diversity
with 165 species of amphibians, 177 species of reptiles, 454 species of birds, and 187
species of mammals. The endemic fauna also includes 36 species of amphibians, 63
species of reptiles, 17 species of birds, and 24 species of mammals. Moreover, Tamil Nadu
ranks 1st in angiosperm diversity across India, featuring over 2000 different species of
floral wealth.
9.9.4 The Government of Tamil Nadu carries out special efforts towards biodiversity and forest
conservation in the state. Carefully employing dedicated forces, infrastructure, as well
as advanced technology, the state addresses threats like illicit fencing, encroachment,
and poaching through Differential Global Positioning Systems (DGPS), Remote Sensing,
and Geographical Information Systems (GIS) for boundary demarcation and surveillance.
Strict surveillance activities include forest stations, forest protection squads, and roving
check posts. The state also employs the Forest Fire Management System to ensure faster
detection and response to forest fires.
208

9.9.5 Emphasising the need for modernising forest management, the state prioritises
sustainable practices to safeguard invaluable forest resources, supporting biodiversity,
providing essential ecosystem services, and sustaining livelihoods. To enhance the
state’s response to such challenges, the Government has sanctioned Rs. 52.83 crore for
the “Modernisation of Tamil Nadu Forest Force” scheme during the 2022-2025 period.
Furthermore, the State Government is implementing around 49 schemes focused on
comprehensive forest and wildlife management. This robust framework is legally
underpinned by rules framed under the Biological Diversity Act, 2002, which ensures
the conservation, sustainable use, and equitable sharing of benefits from biological
resources, notably requiring the establishment of biodiversity management committees
and registers at the local level to ensure compliance and protection.
9.9.6 There has been a significant increase in planned activities and policy interventions in
the state over the years to protect the ecosystems and build resilience against climate
change, which often involves activities that aim at an increase in forest cover in the state.
The Restoration of Degraded Forest Landscape in Tamil Nadu - Phase I project executed
over the years 2021-22 to 2025-26 has conducted restoration works for over 31,060 Ha
of degraded forests across 33 districts in the state with an outlay of Rs 149.30 crore in
the fiscal year 2024-25 towards the cause. The Tamil Nadu Biodiversity Conservation and
Greening Project for Climate Change Response, which aims at climate change mitigation,
biodiversity conservation, resolution of human-wildlife negative interactions, and capacity
development of the forest department, has also actively contributed towards the
restoration of 700 ha of mangroves in Thiruvarur district, with another 350 ha underway.

B. Invasive Species
9.9.7 The presence of Invasive Alien Plant Species in Tamil Nadu poses a critical environmental
challenge to the state, with the potential to severely degrade its rich biodiversity and vital
ecosystems. These non-native exotics, comprising nearly 36.6% of the state’s recorded
flora, disrupt the ecosystem service provision and threaten endangered species. A variety
of invasive plants, including Lantana camara, Prosopis juliflora, Acacia mearnsii (Wattle),
and Senna spectabilis, have established themselves and infested vast forest areas in the
state.
9.9.8 The state’s strategy to tackle is guided by Tamil Nadu Policy on Invasive Plants and
Ecological Restoration (TNPIPER), one of the first state-level policies in India devised
exclusively to target invasive species infestation. The framework prioritises identification,
prevention of spread, and strategic eradication, signalling a shift in focus back to native
species restoration.
209

9.9.9 For the year 2024-2025, a substantial amount of Rs 45.41 crore has been sanctioned
specifically for the removal of Latana camara. As of January 31, 2025, the state has
cleared a total of 16,131.61 infested hectares, demonstrating a vast increase in the scale
of operation compared to previous years. Over the years, the overall approach has moved
towards integrated control measures, which are proving effective in the management of
invasive species in the state.
9.9.10 The intense competition for resources among the native and invasive species initiates an
“invasive meltdown” where one invasive species facilitates the establishment of others.
However, often they also serve as an important source of livelihood for communities
dependent on the species. Therefore, a broader, inclusive management strategy is
necessary to benefit both local biodiversity and the dependent communities.

C. Human-Wildlife Conflict
9.9.11 Tamil Nadu has adopted a multi-dimensional strategy that integrates traditional field
methods with cutting-edge technology to address the escalating challenges of humanwildlife conflict. At the core of its physical intervention, the government has used a
variety of barriers, including elephant-proof trenches, solar-powered hanging fences, and
innovative steel wire rope fences, while simultaneously improving forest habitats through
the creation of fodder resources and water holes to discourage animals from straying. A
significant milestone in this effort is the state’s leadership in technological adoption with
the inauguration of a Rs.7.24 crore AI-based surveillance system at Madukkarai to prevent
elephant-train collisions and the establishment of a centralized Command Control Centre
that monitors sensors across 90 locations. These high-tech solutions are used by the
deployment of Rapid Response Teams and anti-poaching squads for the strategic use
of the safe relocation of straying animals. Beyond field operations, the government has
streamlined social and legal frameworks by notifying the Power Fences Rules of 2023
to regulate electric fencing and ensuring the instant payment of compensation for crop
damage or fatalities. Through the formation of specialized committees involving farmers,
scientists, and forest officials, Tamil Nadu is fostering a collaborative environment to
study crop-raiding patterns, particularly by wild pigs and elephants. By combining these
proactive monitoring systems, such as radio-collaring and early warning alerts, with
standardized rescue protocols and community sensitization, the state aims to harmonize
the safety of human habitations with the vital conservation of its wildlife.

210

Box 9.5 The Gudalur model illustrates how technology can shift human
wildlife conflict mitigation from a reactive approach to a predictive and
preventive system
Located at the tri-junction of Tamil Nadu, Kerala, and Karnataka, the Gudalur Forest Division
forms a critical elephant migratory corridor. It comprises 14,997 ha of highly fragmented
forest spread across nearly 370 patches. Decades of forest conversion into tea and coffee
plantations and human settlements have intensified human–elephant interactions. Additionally,
86 swamps—68 of them within 200 metres of habitations—act as persistent conflict hotspots
by attracting elephants for water and fodder.
Over the past decade, the division has implemented a comprehensive, technology-enabled
conflict mitigation framework integrating data analytics, artificial intelligence, drones, early
warning systems, and a centralized command-and-control mechanism for real-time monitoring
and predictive response. Under the Modernisation of Forest Force Scheme, a state-of-the-art
Command and Control Centre was established at Nadugani at a cost of ₹6 crores, serving as the
operational hub for coordinated conflict response.
Drone technology has been a major innovation, enabling forest personnel to guide elephants
away from human habitations with minimal disturbance and reduced risk to field staff. These
technological interventions are reinforced by strong community participation.
As a result, Gudalur has achieved a 50% reduction in human casualties over two years, zero
unnatural elephant deaths in 2025, elimination of human fatalities in the Cherambadi Range
after 15 years, improved public trust in the Forest Department, and more efficient use of
manpower and financial resources.

D. Common Property Resources
9.9.12 Common Property Resources (CPRs) are natural assets, such as village forests, shared
water bodies, and communal pastures that are accessible for the community with no
definition of exclusive property rights. Across the country, especially in rural areas, CPRs
play a significant multi-functional role, enabling lifestyle, economic sustenance, and often
psychological strength to the members of the community. Management of CPRs becomes
extremely crucial due to several challenges that pose a threat to the community that is
dependent on the same.
9.9.13 CPRs are fundamental to the economic life of rural households, especially for the
economically backward and socially deprived village dwellers. Several studies conducted
in Tamil Nadu over the years consistently point to a worrying trend of decline in the
area under CPRs. Evidence from as early as 1998 in the 54th Round of NSSO has shown
that the extension of state control over natural resources has led to the decay of the
community management system. Apart from economic and developmental reasons,
politics and social factors also seemingly impact the decline of CPRs over the years.

211

9.9.14 The management of common resources involves a complex interplay of formal and
informal systems. In Dindigul district, Indigenous Knowledge Systems have been useful
in the conservation of CPRs. These traditional systems often manage the commons with
a multifunctional perspective, integrating them into the local culture. Empirical evidence
found in the context of the region including Nagapattinam, highlights that core problems
lie in institutional structure. Despite having legal authority, local governing bodies like
the Panchayats are often unable to effectively enforce any regulation on the use and
management of common resources. This institutional weakness, coupled with a lack of
willingness to enforce obligations among users, is a primary cause of over-exploitation
and degradation. Addressing regulatory gaps and institutional effectiveness, proactive
involvement of the local government, and effective management of private encroachment
must be in place to ensure the sustainability of CPRs in the state.

9.10 Wetlands
9.10.1 Tamil Nadu is India’s leader in wetland conservation, boasting the highest number of
Ramsar Convention sites in the country—currently 20 designated wetlands. The state
is home to over 42,000 waterbodies, most of which provide diverse and invaluable
ecosystem services to communities. A recent study by the State Planning Commission
(SPC)6, Government of Tamil Nadu, conducted by the Madras Institute of Development
Studies (MIDS), examined the state’s 141 priority wetlands—those identified by the
government for potential official notification. The study found that these wetlands
currently provide economic benefits worth Rs.8,303.79 crore each year. However, if these
wetlands were restored and rejuvenated, their annual economic value could increase
to Rs.28,214.37 crore. This represents a potential net gain of Rs.19,910.58 crore per year
(State Planning Commission, 2025).
9.10.2 Having recognised the ecological, social, and economic importance of the vital wetland
ecosystems, the state has made consistent efforts towards the protection of the same.
The state’s commitment is showcased through the multi-pronged approach under the
Tamil Nadu Wetlands Mission (TNWM) and its focus on advanced technology, community
involvement, and the protection of the globally significant sites in the state.
9.10.3 The mission has been launched for five years, from 2021-2022 to 2025-2026, with an
initial budget of Rs. 115.15 crore allocated for the ecological restoration of 100 wetlands
with a special focus on local livelihoods. In 2024, the Government accorded an additional
6 State Planning Commission (2025). ‘Economic Valuation of Ecosystem Services: A Study of 61 Prioritised Wetlands/Waterbodies in Tamil Nadu’, State Planning Commission (SPC), Government of Tamil Nadu (a study executed by the Madras Institute of
Development Studies, Chennai).

212

sanction of Rs. 26.60 crore to fund activities to be undertaken during the period 20242025 to 2026-2027. The mission explicitly focuses on mapping 100 wetlands throughout
Tamil Nadu, the preparation of integrated management plans for effective governance,
and includes other effective strategies such as research, awareness campaigns, and
ecosystem-based eco-restoration of wetlands. The state has also committed Rs. 20
crore for the integrated development of selected Ramsar sites and other wetlands, with
funds being utilised for immediate development activities. In addition to this, several
activities and state-funded academic research, as well as community-level strategies,
have been initiated by the state government in ensuring the protection and development
of wetlands in the state.

9.11 Coastal Region
9.11.1

Tamil Nadu, being the southernmost state in the country, has the second-longest
coastline in India, covering 1096 km, which accounts for 15% of the country’s total coastal
region and spreads along the Bay of Bengal, the Indian Ocean, and the Gulf of Mannar.
The coast is shaped by significant geographical features, including the Kaveri River delta,
large beaches and critical brackish water ecosystems like the Pulicat Lake. This dynamic
coastal stretch is of immense ecological value, housing vital coral reefs and seagrass
meadows that support endemic species like the endangered Dugong.

9.11.2 However, it is highly susceptible to severe challenges, including impacts of sea-level rise
and frequent cyclonic activity, and escalating anthropogenic pressures from pollution
and overexploitation as well as coastal erosion. In response to these vulnerabilities,
the state has initiated comprehensive conservation efforts, particularly the Tamil Nadu
Coastal Restoration Mission (TN-SHORE), a large-scale project aimed at enhancing
ecological resilience through nature-based solutions. The core strategy involves intensive
restoration of degraded habitats like mangroves (e.g., Pichavaram) and seagrass beds,
alongside the establishment of dedicated protected areas in an attempt to contribute to
the well-being of the coasts. It is a comprehensive initiative which focuses on improving
coastal biodiversity, coastal protection through enhancement of coastal resilience, and
strengthening coastal management through Integrated Coastal Zonal Management. This
multi-layered initiative is designed to maintain resilience and encourage sustainable
utilisation of the rich endowment of coastal resources.
9.11.3 The strategic vision of TN-SHORE also incorporates the dual objectives of enhancing
community resilience and promoting sustainable economic growth across the coastline.
It is carefully structured to ensure that the ecological and socio-economic benefits
derived from coastal development are sustained over the long term, while simultaneously
213

mitigating risks arising from both natural hazards and human-induced impacts, thereby
complying with key aspects of the Sustainable Development Goals. With an estimated
outlay of Rs 1,675 crore, the mission and associated strategic objectives are organised to
ensure targeted interventions towards the protection of the coastline. Further efforts in
this regard also concentrates on pollution abatement and the promotion of sustainable
livelihoods, with the overarching goal of safeguarding this unique landscape and ensuring
the long-term sustainability of the vital ecosystem.

9.12 Green Initiatives
I. Green Buildings
9.12.1 As of 2024, India has certified over 15,000 green building projects through the Indian
Green Building Council (IGBC), covering more than 13 billion square feet. Tamil Nadu
accounts for approximately 5% of these certified projects, demonstrating the state’s
strong commitment to sustainable development. Tamil Nadu has over 1,087 green building
projects spanning 597 million square feet, ranking 5th nationally in its transition to green
building infrastructure. Among the state’s major cities, Chennai leads with more than
560 buildings covering 332 million square feet—representing 56% of all green buildings in
Tamil Nadu. Coimbatore has 23 green building projects, followed by Salem with 12, Trichy
with 11, and Madurai with 6.
9.12.2 The government has implemented several initiatives to promote green buildings and
energy efficiency: Data Centre Incentives: A 25% subsidy (capped at Rs. 5 crore) for
data centre units that implement green and sustainable initiatives. Green Certification
Programs: Provision for efficient buildings to obtain green ratings through the Indian Green
Building Council (IGBC/LEED Certification) and the Green Rating for Integrated Habitat
Assessment (GRIHA) systems. Renewable Energy Integration: Developers are permitted
to source and distribute green power within integrated townships, and are encouraged
to install rooftop solar, wind, or hybrid renewable energy systems. Lifestyle for Climate
Certification: Launched in 2025 under the Tamil Nadu Climate Change Mission, this
certification assesses sustainable measures in existing buildings and provides a platform
to rate and certify buildings under development. Energy Conservation Recognition:
The state has acknowledged the need for energy-saving measures across government
buildings, private buildings, independent houses, and apartments to mitigate climate
change impacts. Promotion of Energy Audit and Conservation of Energy Scheme

214

This scheme helps businesses improve energy efficiency through financial support. The
Government reimburses: 50% of energy audit costs, up to a maximum of Rs.0.75 lakh
per audit per unit 25% of costs for machinery and equipment replacement, retrofitting,
and technology upgrades aimed at improving energy efficiency Chief Minister’s Solar
Rooftop Capital Incentive Scheme This scheme provides subsidies for grid-connected
domestic solar photovoltaic (PV) systems. Eligible applicants receive: Rs.20,000 per
kilowatt from the state government An additional 30% subsidy from the Ministry of New
and Renewable Energy (MNRE), Government of India

II. Removal of Legacy Waste
i. Construction and Demolition Waste Processing
To manage construction and demolition waste, processing facilities have been sanctioned
in six Corporations: Coimbatore, Madurai, Trichy, Salem, Tuticorin, and Tiruppur.

ii. Air Pollution Control Measures
To prevent dust and air pollution, road sweeping machines have been sanctioned in five
Corporations: Coimbatore, Madurai, Trichy, Salem, and Tuticorin.

iii. Atal Mission for Rejuvenation and Urban Transformation
(AMRUT) 2.0
Under this mission, 320 projects with an estimated cost of Rs.2,746.16 crore are currently
being implemented across Urban Local Bodies (ULBs) under the Directorate of Municipal
Administration.

iv. Urban Forests
Urban greening projects have been established on land reclaimed through bio-mining.
Between 2022 and 2023, 53,589 tree saplings were planted, creating approximately 127
acres of green space.

215

v. Clean Tamil Nadu Company and Integrated Cleanliness Mission
The Government of Tamil Nadu announced the Clean Tamil Nadu Company & Integrated
Cleanliness Mission (2025) to effectively manage solid waste in both rural and urban
areas. This mission aims to integrate waste management across these regions, with an
initial allocation of Rs.10 crore.

9.13 Tamil Nadu Model of Sub-state Climate Action
9.13.1 Tamil Nadu has become a leader in India’s climate governance by creating a statelevel framework that connects climate resilience with biodiversity conservation and
community-based planning. Recognizing the urgency of climate action, the state has
established dedicated climate institutions and developed strategies aligned with
national and international climate objectives7. To coordinate its climate efforts, Tamil
Nadu established the Tamil Nadu Green Climate Company (TNGCC), one of India’s first
specialized agencies for climate action. TNGCC operates through four strategic missions:
the Tamil Nadu Climate Change Mission (TNCCM), the Green Tamil Nadu Mission (GTNM),
the Tamil Nadu Wetlands Mission (TNWM), and the Tamil Nadu Coastal Restoration Mission
(TN-SHORE).
9.13.2 Tamil Nadu has charted a clear path toward achieving Net Zero emissions by 2070.
According to the state’s Greenhouse Gas Inventory, despite being highly industrialized,
Tamil Nadu accounted for only 7% of India’s total emissions in 2019. More impressively,
the state reduced its emission intensity relative to GDP by nearly 60% over a 14-year
period. To build on this progress, Tamil Nadu has designed targeted interventions including
renewable energy expansion, improved energy efficiency, industrial decarbonization, and
a major shift to electric mobility. The goal is for renewable energy to comprise 60% of
the state’s installed power capacity in the coming years.
9.13.3 The state is pioneering grassroots, district-level climate action strategy in four pilot
districts: Nilgiris, Coimbatore, Ramanathapuram, and Virudhunagar. These districts are
developing localized decarbonization plans tracked through a real-time Climate Action
Tracker. Projections indicate these districts could reduce emissions by up to 92% by
2050 through clean energy adoption, transportation transformation, and nature-based
solutions capable of capturing significant amounts of CO₂.
9.13.4 Tamil Nadu’s climate agenda extends beyond emissions reduction to encompass
large-scale ecological restoration, biodiversity protection, and sustainable livelihood
7 https://www.thehindu.com/opinion/op-ed/the-tamil-nadu-model-of-sub-state-climate-action/article70276313.ece
216

development. With over 30% of its land area designated as protected and major wetland
and mangrove restoration projects underway, the state is integrating nature-based
solutions with livelihood security—particularly in climate-vulnerable coastal areas. Local
communities are central to this transformation through participatory approaches that
incorporate traditional and local knowledge.
9.13.5 Tamil Nadu’s climate strategy—combining district-level implementation, evidence-based
planning, robust institutions, and nature-centered development—provides a replicable
model for other Indian states and subnational governments worldwide seeking equitable
and effective climate governance.

9.14 Proposed Measures to Protect the Environmental
Capital in the State
A. Nature-Based Solutions
9.14.1 With the increasing climate vulnerabilities, it has become critical to adopt effective
interventions to embrace climate resilience worldwide. Nature-based solutions are
measures that protect, sustainably manage, or restore natural ecosystems to tackle
societal challenges—such as climate change, human health, food and water security,
and disaster risk—while simultaneously enhancing human well-being and supporting
biodiversity. Nature-based solutions involve the protection, sustainable management, or
restoration of natural ecosystems to address societal issues like climate change, health,
food and water security, and disaster risks, providing benefits for both people and
biodiversity. It is considered to be a cost-effective method to address the risks of climate
while also looking into the problems on biodiversity and declining quality of lands. It is
estimated that nature-based solutions could contribute 37% of the mitigation required
until 2030 to achieve the goals of the Paris Agreement.

B. Payments to Ecosystem Services
9.14.2 The Global Environment Facility (GEF) has made substantial investments in Payment for
Ecosystem Services (PES) initiatives since its inception. Frameworks such as the Millennium
Ecosystem Assessment (MEA), The Economics of Ecosystems and Biodiversity (TEEB),
and the Intergovernmental Platform on Biodiversity and Ecosystem Services (IPBES)
emphasize PES as a key mechanism for sustaining ecosystem services. The UNESCAP
defines PES as a voluntary transaction and a well-defined environmental service (or a
217

land use likely to secure that service), purchased by at least one environmental service
buyer from at least one environmental service provider, if and only if the environmental
service provider meets the conditions of that contract and secures the environmental
service provision. PES scheme is very beneficial that it helps in mobilizing funds for
biodiversity conservation.

Box 9.6: Farming Linked with Global Carbon credit market in Uttar Pradesh
The Uttar Pradesh government has recently launched an agroforestry-based carbon-credit
initiative that functions as a Payment for Ecosystem Services (PES) model by compensating
farmers for the carbon sequestered through tree planting on their farmland. Launched around
2023 and implemented on the ground from 2024–25, the scheme aims to promote largescale agroforestry, enhance green cover, and provide farmers with a new stream of income
through carbon finance. Under this programme, farmers voluntarily register with the Forest
Department, plant approved tree species on their land and become eligible for carbon-credit
payments. To motivate early participation, the state provides advance payments—about
Rs.10,000 per farmer—before carbon sequestration is fully realised. The value of each carbon
credit is calculated at $6. As reported, 25,140 farmers have registered so far, and 237 farmers
have already received advance payments, signalling high initial uptake. The plantations will be
periodically verified by third-party agencies such as TERI, and carbon credits will be issued and
purchased every five years for up to 30 years, offering farmers long-term financial returns.
This model blends environmental benefits with livelihood enhancement by valuing carbon
sequestration as an ecosystem service and monetising it through carbon markets. The project
works on the basis of ‘polluter pays principle’, where polluting industries buy carbon credits.
The scheme was implemented in six forest divisions in 2024 and seven more were added into
the second phase.

C. Environment and Social Governance
9.14.3 SEBI proposed a new regulatory framework to strengthen ESG disclosures, ratings, and
investing in India, aiming to curb greenwashing while keeping compliance practical. The
plan introduces a Business Responsibility and Sustainability Reporting (BRSR) Core—a
focused set of assured ESG indicators like emissions, water use, waste, gender diversity,
and employee well-being—rolled out gradually to the top 1000 listed companies. The India
ESG Outlook Report 2025 by CSRBOX evaluates the environmental, social, and governance
(ESG) performance of 229 leading companies on the Bombay Stock Exchange across 13
sectors. It highlights the dominance of Scope 3 emissions, which can constitute up to
98% of total emissions in some industries.

218

9.15 Climate/Green Finance
9.15.1 As discussed in the previous sections, Tamil Nadu set for itself some ambitious climate
goals, including, to achieve carbon neutrality well ahead of the national target of 2070,
and to enhance the green cover in the state from 23.7% to 33% in ten years. The
Government of Tamil Nadu has taken some important policy initiatives to combat climate
change including, setting up of three key missions (viz., Climate Change Mission, the Green
Tamil Nadu Mission, and the Tamil Nadu Wetlands Mission) with a budget of about $90
million; setting aside Rs. 1000 crore Green Fund to finance projects aimed at mitigating
climate change impacts; launching of the District Climate Mission; and enhancing efforts
to climate-proof existing sectoral plans and building the capacity of government officials.
Further, Chennai City Corporation has issued green municipal bonds to raise Rs. 205.59
crore for sustainable urban infrastructure projects. Srinivasan et al. (2023)8 highlight that
the State Action Plan on Climate Change 2.0 has estimated the investment needs for the
period 2021-2030 in the climate-related sectors as Rs. 3.24 lakh crore, with over Rs. 1.03
lakh crore identified as gap.
9.15.2 In addition to the above-mentioned policy initiatives, the State Government is also
exploring other financing mechanisms and instruments that include, (a) carbon markets
and carbon credits; (b) blended finance and international funds; and (c) insurance
mechanisms.

9.16 Conclusions
9.16.1 Tamil Nadu’s environmental trajectory reflects both the scale of its economic
transformation and the relative strength of its governance architecture. The state has
long been recognised for institutional capacity in environmental regulation, supported
by an extensive network of regulatory bodies, conservation agencies, and legislative
instruments addressing industrial pollution control, coastal zone management,
biodiversity conservation, and climate planning. Yet rapid industrialisation, urban
expansion, and intensifying climate risks are placing mounting pressure on ecosystems
and regulatory systems, exposing gaps in coordination and implementation. This tension
is evident in water pollution management, where Common Effluent Treatment Plants
(CETPs) have played a crucial role in mitigating industrial discharge in major textile,
leather, and chemical clusters. Upgrading treatment technologies, expanding capacity,
and strengthening operational oversight would consolidate these gains. Complementing
conventional regulation with transparency-driven approaches—such as real-time
8 Srinivasan, M., K. Ghoge, S. Haldar, A.B. Bazaz, & A. Revi (2023). Climate Finance in India 2023, Indian Institute for Human Settlements, https://iihs.co.in/knowledge-gateway/wp-content/uploads/2023/11/20231128_Climate-Finance-in-India2023.pdf.

219

public disclosure of water quality data, compliance reporting, and accessible grievance
mechanisms—can deepen accountability and community participation. Similarly, the
expansion of solid waste streams, particularly electronic and plastic waste, underscores
the need to strengthen enforcement of Extended Producer Responsibility frameworks,
formalise recycling systems, and integrate informal waste workers into regulated value
chains to ensure both environmental effectiveness and social equity. In parallel, air
pollution challenges arising from industrial activity, vehicular growth, and construction
suggest a role for market-based instruments, including tradable emission permits, as
supplements to existing regulatory tools.
9.16.2 Institutionally, Tamil Nadu possesses a robust administrative framework that can be
leveraged through improved interdepartmental coordination, integrated planning
platforms, and enhanced vertical alignment between state and local governments.
Developing comprehensive environmental data ecosystems—by strengthening platforms
such as ENVIS—would support evidence-based policymaking and public accountability.
Environmental assessment processes also require recalibration, particularly in ecologically
sensitive regions where project-level evaluations often fail to capture cumulative and
spatially aggregated impacts. More broadly, climate action must be embedded within
mainstream development planning rather than pursued as a parallel policy track.
Integrating mitigation and adaptation strategies with disaster management, land-use
regulation, infrastructure planning, and urban governance is essential for strengthening
resilience to floods, droughts, heatwaves, and coastal erosion. While Tamil Nadu has
laid a strong institutional foundation for environmental governance, the next phase of
reform must prioritise implementation depth, coordination, data integration, and policy
coherence to ensure that ecological sustainability remains compatible with economic
growth, social inclusion, and long-term climate resilience.

220

Chapter 10

Women and Welfare
10.1 Introduction
10.1.1 Tamil Nadu’s approach to women’s development is anchored in a strong and sustained
policy commitment, supported by a comprehensive institutional framework and a long
history of social investment. This commitment is articulated in the State Policy for Women
20211, which sets out a clear vision of equitable access to services and entitlements,
freedom from discrimination and violence, and equal opportunities for women to realise
their aspirations. As the State builds on this foundation, attention is now focused on a
limited set of emerging issues, including variations in labour-force participation, rising
non-communicable disease risks, and infrastructure gaps related to childcare and mobility.

Box 10.1 Tamil Nadu in Women’s Development – A Snapshot
Tamil Nadu is among India’s strongest-performing states on key dimensions of women’s
development. The State has achieved high levels of female literacy and secondary education
completion, alongside an advanced demographic transition characterised by fertility well below
replacement level and a higher age at marriage. These outcomes reflect sustained public
investment and institutional continuity over time.
The State also records some of the strongest maternal and reproductive health outcomes in the
country, supported by near-universal institutional deliveries, high antenatal and postnatal care
coverage, and a dense public health network. Tamil Nadu’s comprehensive welfare architecture,
including universal food security and social protection programmes, has played a central role in
reducing vulnerability and improving women’s human development outcomes.
In the labour market, Tamil Nadu displays a distinctive and relatively advanced employment
structure. Women’s labour force participation is higher than in most Indian states, with a greater
presence in manufacturing and services rather than agriculture, reflecting a more diversified
economic base. Taken together, these outcomes place Tamil Nadu in a strong position as it
addresses second-generation challenges related to job quality, unpaid care work, nutrition, and
preventive health.
10.1.2 This chapter presents an integrated, data-driven assessment of the status of women and
the welfare architecture supporting them in Tamil Nadu. It draws on multiple nationally
representative datasets, including the National Family Health Survey (NFHS-5), Periodic
1Government of Tamil Nadu. (2021). Tamil Nadu State Policy for Women 2021. Department of Social Welfare and Women Empowerment.

221

Labour Force Survey (PLFS 2023-24), alongside administrative statistics from government
departments, and insights from recent studies. Together, the chapter aims to provide a
multidimensional understanding of women’s welfare and empowerment across various
dimensions. For most indicators, we benchmark Tamil Nadu against the all-India average
and against peer states with comparable economic prosperity—Gujarat, Maharashtra,
Karnataka, and Kerala.
10.1.3 The chapter examines socio-cultural and demographic determinants, including marriage
patterns, fertility transitions, education, and women’s agency within households. It also
evaluates women’s health outcomes - highlighting emerging concerns and districtlevel disparities and analyses women’s economic participation, exploring lifecycle work
patterns, sectoral employment, unpaid work burdens, and constraints created by social
norms and mobility. Further, it maps women’s representation in political, administrative,
and judicial leadership and outlines the state’s institutional mechanisms and key schemes,
and concludes with a set of evidence-led policy recommendations.

10.2 Socio-cultural Context
A. Marriage Patterns
10.2.1 Tamil Nadu has consistently performed better than most Indian states in delaying the
age at marriage. The median age at first marriage among women aged 25-29 stands at
22 years - almost two years higher than the national average, based on NFHS-5 data
(Table 1) - reflecting the cumulative effect of rising education, improved mobility, and
longstanding social reform movements in the state. The prevalence of child marriage2
is comparatively low at 12.8 % among women aged 20–24, significantly below levels in
Gujarat, Karnataka, and Maharashtra, although still higher in rural than urban Tamil Nadu.
Continued efforts to reduce rural child marriage to single digits by 2030 remain crucial
for sustaining the state’s demographic gains.
10.2.2 A distinctive feature of the state’s marital landscape is the continued prevalence of
consanguineous unions. At around 28%, Tamil Nadu reports the highest incidence among
its peer states (Table 10.1). These patterns are culturally rooted and carry recognised
genetic risks. Rising urbanisation and targeted public health communication may help
reduce the prevalence of such unions.

B. Fertility and Family Planning
10.2.3 Tamil Nadu is also well advanced in its demographic transition. The total fertility rate (TFR)
of 1.76 reported in NFHS-5 was already below the replacement level of 2.1 (Table 10.2).
More recent administrative estimates place fertility even lower, around 1.4, positioning
Tamil Nadu to become the first Indian state to reach population stabilisation by 20353.
Some fertility indicators require policy attention. Teenage pregnancy, at 6.3%, remains
higher than in Kerala and Karnataka and closer to the national average. Additionally,
caesarean deliveries account for 44.9% of all births, nearly twice the national level, with
sharp inter-district variation (68% in Kanyakumari versus 28% in Tiruvannamalai).

10.2.4 Female sterilisation continues to dominate contraceptive use, accounting for 57.8% of all
methods, despite the state’s fertility decline. With Tamil Nadu’s fertility rate having fallen
below replacement level, there is a strong case for reassessing the current incentive
structure that heavily favours permanent or difficult-to-reverse female sterilisation
procedures over other contraceptive methods or male participation in family planning.

10.3 Women’s Resources and Agency
10.3.1 Women’s access to education resources has expanded substantially in Tamil Nadu. Female
literacy among women aged 15–49 has reached 84%, and 24.3 % have completed 12 or
more years of schooling, levels exceeded only by Kerala (Table 10.3). The higher education
landscape is particularly strong, with the female Gross Enrolment Ratio (GER) standing
at 47.3 % (AISHE 2021–22)4, reflecting a combination of longstanding welfare measures,
reservations, scholarships, and hostel support.
10.3.2 At the same time, low access to early childhood education remains a concern. Only onethird of girls (and boys) aged 2–4 attend pre-school or anganwadi programmes (NFHS-5),
indicating significant room for improvement.

10.3.3 Tamil Nadu performs well in terms of women’s access to economic resources (Table 10.4).
An impressive 92% of women have their own bank or savings account—higher than the
national average, as well as most peer states. Awareness of microcredit programmes is
high (74%), and 18% of women report taking loans from them, underscoring the deep
penetration of financial inclusion programmes. Household decision-making agency, while
not uniform, is relatively strong: 87% of women report participating—alone or jointly—in
decisions regarding the use of their own earnings, and 78% do so for their husbands’
earnings. Nearly three-quarters of participants make decisions on their own healthcare,
household purchases, and family visits.
10.3.4 Digital and media exposure reveal a more mixed picture. Tamil Nadu has the highest
television viewership among peer states, but print media readership is among the lowest
(15%). Internet use among women remains modest (47%). The rural–urban digital divide
is pronounced: only 69% of rural women own a mobile phone, and only 39% report using
the internet.
10.3.5 Taken together, Tamil Nadu exhibits strong achievements in education, financial inclusion,
and household decision-making, yet significant challenges persist in early childhood
education, digital access, and gender gaps in earnings and safety. These structural
constraints continue to shape the extent to which women can translate their educational
gains into agency in both private and public domains.

10.4 Health, Nutrition, and Well-being
A. Reproductive and Maternal Health
10.4.1 Tamil Nadu has historically been one of India’s strongest performers in advancing women’s
health, reflecting sustained public investment in maternal and reproductive health
services. The state leads all major states in antenatal and postnatal care, with 71.3% of
women receiving all recommended components of antenatal care - more than double the
national average - and 92.7% receiving complete postnatal checks as per NFHS-5 data
(Table 10.5). Nearly universal institutional births further highlight the robustness of Tamil
Nadu’s maternal health system.
10.4.2 Access to menstrual hygiene products is also widespread, with 87.4% of women using
sanitary napkins and only 12.7% relying on cloth, compared to 49.6% nationally. However,
sanitary pad disposal presents a growing environmental challenge.

10.4.3 Despite these achievements, some outcome indicators require close attention. Tamil
Nadu reports the highest proportion of pregnancies ending in non-live births among
its peer states (9.9%, compared with 6.5% in Kerala). These patterns point to persistent
gaps in underlying maternal health conditions and quality of care, even in the context of
strong service delivery.

229

10.4.4 Anaemia remains a significant health concern among women. Over half of all women
aged 15–49 (53.4%) are anaemic, with substantial inter-district variation—exceeding 66%
in Perambalur and falling to around 41% in Theni. Dietary diversity needs improvement,
especially towards protein food: 12.6% of women report not consuming pulses or beans
even once a week, 10.6% do not consume milk or curd weekly, and 36.6% do not consume
fruits (Table 10.6)
10.4.5 Tamil Nadu’s universal Public Distribution System (PDS) provides critical food security,
yet evaluations highlight unintended nutritional consequences. The availability of free or
inexpensive rice may be associated with lower dietary diversity and nutrient-rich foods
- pulses, vegetables, dairy, and fruit - particularly in low-income households. The state
may consider piloting nutrition-sensitive reforms within the PDS to better align subsidies
with women’s nutritional needs.

C. Non-Communicable Diseases and Screening
10.4.6 Emerging non-communicable diseases also warrant attention. Only 44.3% of women
have a normal waist-to-hip ratio, lower than in Gujarat, Maharashtra, or Karnataka,
suggesting an elevated risk of central obesity. The prevalence of thyroid disorders (4.1%)
and diabetes (2.9%), while not the highest among southern states, still reflects growing
metabolic health risks. Cancer screening rates remain low: only 9.8% of women have ever
undergone cervical cancer screening, and 5.6% have had a breast examination. Makkalai
Thedi Maruthuvam5, a state government programme that delivers healthcare services at
the doorstep, including screening for non-communicable diseases and the provision of
medicines, has significantly expanded medical screening coverage. According to the State
Planning Commission’s (2023) study about the implementation of the scheme, in Tamil
Nadu, 69.1% of women were screened for diabetes mellitus and 75.1% were screened for
hypertension in the year preceding the survey.

10.5 Labour Market Outcomes
10.5.1 Tamil Nadu has taken several proactive steps to expand women’s employment - permitting
night-shift work, increasing training subsidies, and investing in safe working women’s
hostels to support urban mobility. To understand the extent of women’s economic
participation, this section uses the Employment-to-Population Ratio (EPR), which provides
a more accurate picture of realised employment than the Labour Force Participation
Rate (LFPR). LFPR systematically undercounts women who would like to work but are not
5 State Planning Commission. (2023). Evaluation of Non-Communicable Disease Cascade Care under Makkalai Thedi Maruthuvam in Tamil Nadu – A Cross-Sectional Study. State Planning Commission, Government of Tamil Nadu. https://spc.tn.gov.in/
wp-content/uploads/M_T_M.pdf

230

actively seeking employment due to the unavailability of suitable jobs, lack of information,
care responsibilities, commuting barriers, or social norms that discourage job-seeking.
EPR captures both employed and discouraged workers, making it the more appropriate
metric for women’s labour market analysis.

10.5.2 Tamil Nadu’s EPR for women (15–64 years) stands at 44.4 %, positioning the state
between South Asia and Southeast/East Asia in terms of women’s economic integration
(Figure 10.1). Tamil Nadu performs better than Sri Lanka (34.1%) and Bangladesh (38.8%),
but is behind Malaysia (53.5%), Indonesia (53.5%), Thailand (68.1%), South Korea (62.6%),
and Vietnam (73.8%) (ILO 2024; World Bank 2025). These international comparisons
thus underscore the significance of economic structure—particularly export-linked,
labour-intensive manufacturing and skilled services—in facilitating large-scale women’s
employment.

10.5.3 Comparing Tamil Nadu with its domestic peers offers further insights (Table 10.7).
Tamil Nadu’s EPR (48.9%, principal plus subsidiary status) is the second-highest among
major states, according to the PLFS 2023–24 analysis. Excluding unpaid work in family
enterprises—a category that traps many women in unremunerated roles—Tamil Nadu
ranks at the very top with an EPR of 44.7%. Only 4.2% of women are engaged in unpaid
family work, compared with more than 15% in states such as Gujarat, demonstrating the
state’s relative success in enabling market-based, remunerated work.
10.5.4 Rural women’s employment stands at a striking 62%, boosted significantly by employment
under the national rural employment guarantee scheme under the Mahatma Gandhi
Rural Employment Guarantee Act 2005 (MGNREGA). Women accounted for 86.7% of total
person-days under the scheme in 2025 in Tamil Nadu, compared to 47% in Gujarat and
46% in Maharashtra, reflecting Tamil Nadu’s effective implementation. In contrast, urban
women’s employment is lower at 33.5%, but a significantly higher share of urban working
women is in regular salaried jobs. Over half of employed urban women held regular
salaried jobs in 2023–24, indicating greater access to formal employment in cities.

232

C. Lifecycle Patterns of Women’s Work
10.5.5 Tamil Nadu exhibits a distinctive employment lifecycle. Younger women, particularly those
in their twenties, tend to have relatively low employment rates. Only 23.4% of married
women aged 20–29 is employed—well below the national average (32.8%), based on PLFS
2023–24. These patterns suggest that marriage and household responsibilities remain a
significant barrier to women’s early labour market participation in the state.
10.5.6 The employment rate for women rises steadily in the 40-60 age group (Figure 10.2). Tamil
Nadu’s EPR surpasses national averages for women aged 40–49 and remains strong
among women aged 50–59, as well as those aged 60–64. This suggests delayed entry
or early withdrawal due to education and care responsibilities, followed by re-entry as
household burdens ease.

10.5.7 Education-employment patterns reveal a critical paradox. Tamil Nadu records exceptionally
high employment among women with little or no education, but one of the lowest
employment rates among educated women (Table 10.8). Only about 1 in 3 diploma-holders
and graduates are employed, which is lower than in Kerala (47%), Karnataka (40%), and
the national average (34.5%). This mismatch highlights a structural gap between the
skills developed by Tamil Nadu’s education system and the sectors that currently employ
women workers.
10.5.8 Regardless of the underlying reasons for low employment among highly educated women,
the presence of high-quality, skilled jobs is a prerequisite for translating education into
labour-market participation. Tamil Nadu is well positioned in this regard to capitalise
on the rapid expansion of Global Capability Centres (GCCs), offering a significant and
growing opportunity for educated women to enter formal, well-paid, and skills-intensive
employment (Box 10.2).

Box 10.2 The Promise and Potential of GCCs
The state has the distinctive feature of having a higher proportion of women enrolled in higher
education than men. The central challenge now lies in ensuring adequate absorption into skilled
employment. In this context, Global Capability Centres (GCCs) and Global Service Centres (GSCs)
represent a significant and expanding source of high-quality, skilled jobs that can help bridge
this gap.
GCCs and GSCs in India serve as the in-house strategic units of multinational corporations. Today,
these centres manage core business functions, drive innovation, support global operations,
and anchor digital transformation across various industries, including technology, banking and
financial services (BFSI).
Tamil Nadu is among the leading destinations for GCCs, hosting over 350 such units that
collectively employ around 225,000 people, of whom roughly 32% are women. While most GCCs
remain concentrated in Chennai, an emerging trend of expansion into Tier-2 cities—such as
Madurai, Coimbatore, and Tiruchirappalli—illustrates the state’s increasing attractiveness as a
diversified hub (NASSCOM, 20256). The number of women employed in Tamil Nadu–based GCCs
is expected to rise to approximately 150,000 by 2030.
Companies like DesiCrew a technology solutions provider have sought to employ educated
6 NASSCOM (2024) India GCC Landscape Report – The 5 Year Journey, NASSCOM-ZINNOV, https://zinnov.com/centers-of-excellence/zinnov-nasscom-india-gcc-landscape-report-the-5-year-journey-report/, accessed on November 20, 2025

235

women in rural areas, including first generation women students. Supportive policies and
targeted incentives can encourage the scaling of such enterprises, enabling the replication of
inclusive rural development models across the state. Doing so would broaden access to highquality, skilled jobs, improve women’s economic mobility, and enable Tamil Nadu to fully leverage
the opportunities presented by the expanding GCC ecosystem.
10.5.9 Large, labour-intensive industries—textiles, apparel, footwear, and tobacco—account for
nearly 70% of all female industrial employment. These sectors have historically provided
women with stable employment; however, they remain low in technological intensity
and innovation, resulting in lower wages and slower productivity growth. This requires
the state to simultaneously generate quality employment for the skilled women seeking
work.
