236

10.6.1 Unpaid domestic and care work remains the most significant constraint on women’s
labour force participation. The Time Use Survey 2024 data show that 81.6% of women
participate in unpaid domestic work, compared with only 27.4% of men (Table 10.9).
Women spend 392 minutes daily on domestic and caregiving tasks—more than twice
the 164 minutes spent by men. Men’s participation in domestic work mirrors the Indian
average, suggesting limited evolution in gender norms.
10.6.2 Overall, Tamil Nadu stands out for its high levels of non-farm employment among women
and its unusually low dependence on unpaid family work. However, low employment
among young and educated women, limited expansion into high-skilled sectors, and the
persistence of domestic responsibilities as major barriers suggest that the next phase
of gender-inclusive growth will require sustained investments in childcare, transport,
and sectoral diversification. The lack of district-level employment statistics, reliable
information on intra- and inter-state women migrants, systematic mapping of childcare
facilities, and disaggregated records on welfare-scheme beneficiaries significantly
constrains deeper analysis. It limits the state’s capacity to design targeted, evidencebased interventions.

10.7 Women in Leadership and Decision-Making
10.7.1 Tamil Nadu’s sustained investments in women’s education, health, and social development
have resulted in a leadership landscape that is more gender-inclusive than in many other
Indian states. Yet representation varies significantly across domains of governance,
public administration, policing, and the judiciary. Women hold a strong presence in
local government, district administration, and the subordinate judiciary, but remain
considerably underrepresented in higher political offices, senior bureaucracy, the Indian
Police Service (IPS), and the higher judiciary. These patterns reflect both institutional
design and structural barriers that continue to shape women’s leadership trajectories.

10.7.2 Women’s representation is highest in local political institutions. In 2024, women accounted
for 56% of Panchayat Ward Members and 51.8% of Urban Local Body Councillors, far
exceeding the all-India average of 44% (Table 10.10). These gains reflect the impact of
constitutionally mandated gender reservations, combined with Tamil Nadu’s long history
of decentralised governance. However, representation declines sharply at higher levels of
political leadership. Only 5.1% of Members of the Legislative Assembly (2021) and 12.8% of
Lok Sabha MPs from Tamil Nadu (2024) are women.

B. Leadership in State Bureaucracy
10.7.3 In public administration, women constitute approximately 32% of the Tamil Nadu cadre
of the Indian Administrative Service (IAS). However, Tamil Nadu fares marginally better at
the district-level leadership: 36.8% of District Collectors in 2024 were women, indicating
that once women enter the IAS, they have relatively greater opportunities to occupy
district executive roles.

238

C. Police and Judiciary leadership
10.7.4 The Tamil Nadu police force is comparatively gender-diverse, with 26.7% women personnel
as of 2023—more than double the national average of 12%. This progress is partly due
to sustained recruitment efforts and the state’s pioneering model of All-Women Police
Stations. However, representation narrows at senior levels: only 15.3% of IPS officers in
the Tamil Nadu cadre are women.
10.7.5 Tamil Nadu also performs relatively well in the judicial system. Women constitute close
to 40% of judges in the subordinate judiciary, substantially above the national average of
30%. In the higher judiciary, women’s representation remains lower but still better than
the national average: 21.8% of Madras High Court judges in 2025 are women, compared
with around 14–15% across all Indian High Courts.
10.7.6 Tamil Nadu’s women leadership reveals three broad and consistent patterns. Women are
strongly represented in positions where gender quotas exist, such as local bodies and
the subordinate judiciary; they achieve moderate representation in institutions where
transparent, merit-based recruitment systems are in place, such as district administration
and district courts; and they remain significantly underrepresented where gatekeeping is
most entrenched.
10.7.7 These patterns align with national and international evidence showing that gender
quotas and open recruitment processes expand women’s access to leadership, whereas
male-dominated nomination and appointment systems restrict advancement. For
Tamil Nadu—already a frontrunner in women’s social development—the next stage
of leadership inclusion will require strengthening women’s political pathways through
leadership training and party-level nomination reforms, revisiting service norms that
restrict women’s mobility in the bureaucracy and police and sustaining investments in the
legal pipeline by expanding opportunities in legal education, clerkships, and early-career
judicial roles. Strengthening these institutional pathways is essential for translating the
state’s broad social gains into equitable representation at all levels of leadership.

239

10.8 Institutional Mechanisms and Policy Recommendations
A. Institutional Architecture
10.8.1 Tamil Nadu’s institutional architecture for women’s welfare operates through three
interconnected tiers that collectively shape policy formulation, implementation, and
monitoring. At the apex sits the Department of Social Welfare and Women Empowerment,
the nodal agency responsible for programmes for women, children, senior citizens, and
transgender persons. This is supported by district-level social welfare officers who
manage the delivery of schemes and ensure coordination with local institutions. The
third pillar is the State Commission for Women, established in 1993 as a statutory body
for grievance redressal, oversight, and advocacy.
10.8.2 In addition to these core institutions, Tamil Nadu has created specialised bodies that
address distinct vulnerabilities. The Widows and Destitute Women Welfare Board support
rehabilitation, safety, and economic reintegration for women in distress. The pioneering
Transgender Welfare Board, established in 2008, reflects Tamil Nadu’s longstanding
commitment to inclusion. However, further strengthening of institutional mechanisms
is required to ensure fairness and employment opportunities for transgender individuals
(Box 10.3).
10.8.3 The state hosts 99 Women Industrial Tailoring Cooperative Societies, a Transgender
Tailoring Society, and 25 Weaning Food Industrial Cooperative Societies8. Workplace
safety has been strengthened through the constitution of 43,783 Internal Committees
under the Sexual Harassment of Women at the Workplace Act, 2013.

8 Government of Tamil Nadu, Commissioner of Social Welfare, Women Welfare and Empowerment Department. (2025–2026).
Policy note 2025–2026.

240

Box 10.3 Transgender Rights and Welfare Policy Framework for Tamil Nadu
The Indian Constitution guarantees transgender persons equality, dignity, and the right to selfidentify under Articles 14, 15, 16, 19(1)(a), and 21. International evidence suggests that transgender
persons constitute 0.3–0.5% of the adult population (Zhang et al., 20209); applied to Tamil
Nadu’s projected 2026 population by the Ministry of Statistics and Program Implementation,
this translates to an estimated 171,528–285,880 individuals.
Tamil Nadu has been a national pioneer in transgender welfare, establishing India’s first
Transgender Welfare Board in 2008 and building a comprehensive system of social, economic, and
legal support. The Board facilitates identity documentation (ID, ration card, voter ID), provides
assistance with house patta, ensures access to healthcare and insurance, offers free housing
schemes, and supports education through loans and skill-training programmes. Livelihood
support includes equipment such as sewing machines, formation of SHGs, establishment of
Short Stay Homes, and direct financial aid.
In 2024–25, 1,599 transgender individuals received monthly pensions, and up to Rs. 50,000
in annual assistance was provided to 200 individuals for self-employment purposes. Under
the Vidiyal Payanam scheme, transgender persons are eligible for free bus journeys. Nearly
10,000 individuals have obtained transgender ID cards through the Thirunangaigal mobile app,
introduced in 2021. A transgender tailoring cooperative operates under the Women Industrial
Cooperatives, supporting community-led economic participation.
Education-focused support has expanded through the Higher Education Assistance Scheme
(2024)10, which fully covers tuition and hostel fees for transgender students.

10.9 Key Schemes and Implementation
10.9.1 Tamil Nadu implements one of India’s most extensive portfolios of schemes for the
welfare of women and girls. A complete list of active schemes—organised by category,
department, and purpose—is provided in Appendix 1.

9 Zhang, Q., Goodman, M., Adams, N., Corneil, T., Hashemi, L., Kreukels, B., Motmans, J., Snyder, R., and Coleman, E. (2020).

Epidemiological considerations in transgender health: A systematic review with focus on higher quality data. International
Journal of Transgender Health, 21(2), 125–137. https://doi.org/10.1080/26895269.2020.1753136

10 Government of Tamil Nadu. (2024). Higher Education Assistance Scheme for Transgender Persons. Social Welfare and
Women Empowerment Department.

241

A. Direct Income Support and Household Security
10.9.2 The state government operates two cash transfer schemes: an unconditional cash
transfer scheme, Kalaignar Magalir Urimai Thogai (KMUT), and a conditional cash transfer
for higher education, Pudhumai Penn Scheme11, which illustrates Tamil Nadu’s shift toward
direct empowerment through financial support.
10.9.3 KMUT, launched in September 2023, has recorded a wide reach within a short period. The
impact evaluation by Rukmini (2025)12 finds 64% household coverage in rural areas and
55% in urban areas, with strong inclusion of disadvantaged groups—46% of rural and
16% of urban beneficiaries. Women report improved self-confidence, greater control over
household spending, and reduced reliance on small borrowings.
10.9.4 Pudhumai Penn supports girls from government schools and government-aided Tamilmedium schools pursuing higher education. The State Planning Commission (2024)
evaluation indicates that the scheme has reduced dropout risks, enabled transitions
into higher education, and significantly benefited low-income rural households. 27.6% of
beneficiaries from farm labourer families secured admission into engineering programmes.
Beneficiaries primarily use the assistance to cover college fees, supplementary educational
expenses, and transportation costs.

C. Mobility and Work Participation
10.9.5 Mobility is central to women’s access to both education and employment, and Tamil Nadu’s
interventions directly address constraints such as cost, safety, and dependence on male
relatives. Vidiyal Payanam13 has expanded women’s independent mobility across the state,
reducing transportation expenses and improving access to workplaces and markets. The
Pink Auto Scheme encourages women to enter the urban transport workforce, opening a
new avenue of stable self-employment while improving the availability of safe transport
options for other women. Free bus pass schemes for students ensure uninterrupted
access to school and college, especially for those from low-income households.

11 State Planning Commission. (2024). Evaluation of Moovalur Ramamirtham Ammaiyar Higher Education Assurance

Scheme (Pudhumai Penn). State Planning Commission, Government of Tamil Nadu. https://spc.tn.gov.in/evaluation-of-pudhumai-penn-scheme/

242

D. Economic Empowerment and Livelihoods
10.9.6 Tamil Nadu’s livelihood programmes span both self-employment and urban wage
employment. The Sathyavani Muthu Ammaiyar Free Sewing Machine Scheme, along
with the state’s network of Women Industrial Tailoring Cooperative Societies, has long
supported home-based and small-scale entrepreneurial activities, particularly among
women seeking flexible work arrangements. The Annai Sathiyavani Muthu Ammaiyar
Motorised Sewing Machine Scheme extends this support by providing improved tools to
economically weaker women, widows, and destitute women, enabling them to increase
productivity and earnings.
10.9.7 Urban employment opportunities are supported by a network of hostels for working
women, which provide safe accommodation, helping women migrate for work in towns
and cities (Box 10.4). Together, these schemes support diverse forms of employment
suited to women’s varied household responsibilities, mobility constraints, and skill levels.

Box 10.4 Enabling women’s migration for employment -The Thozhi Hostels
Initiative
One of the biggest barriers to women migrating independently for work is the lack of safe,
secure, and affordable accommodation in urban areas.
Recognising this evolving need, the Government of Tamil Nadu established the Tamil Nadu
Working Women’s Hostels Corporation Limited (TNWWHCL) in 2020 under the Companies Act,
2013. Its mandate is to build and maintain a high-quality, affordable, and financially viable
network of hostels for working women from all socioeconomic backgrounds. The Corporation is
responsible for creating new Thozhi14 hostels— ‘Thozhi’ meaning ‘friend’ in Tamil—and upgrading
existing facilities to contemporary standards of quality and safety.
According to TNWWHCL, Thozhi hostels are designed to function as a ‘home away from home,’
offering secure, comfortable, and hygienic living spaces at affordable rates. Importantly, the
security deposit is set at the equivalent of one month’s rent—significantly lower than what
is required in most private paying-guest accommodations—reducing barriers for women
transitioning into urban employment. It is worth noting that rooms are also available for shorter
stays, subject to availability, thereby catering to women’s need for temporary services.
14 World Bank (2024) Tamil Nadu Working Women’s Hostels – A Home Away from Home, September, 23. https://www.worldbank.org/en/news/feature/2024/09/23/tamil-nadu-working-women-s-hostels-a-home-away-from-home, Accessed last on
November 20, 2025

243

As of November 2025, 19 Thozhi hostels were fully operational across 15 districts, collectively
serving 1,824 working women. Demand remains high, with an overall occupancy rate of 87% in
October 2025 and full occupancy in Chennai, Trichy, and Coimbatore, as per data shared by the
management.

10.10 Policy Recommendations
10.10.1 Tamil Nadu implements a wide array of schemes for girls and women. Yet, there is no unified
system to track, over time, which schemes an individual beneficiary has accessed—or to
identify those who have been entirely excluded. Establishing a longitudinal, beneficiarylevel tracking mechanism is crucial for assessing cumulative benefits, preventing
duplication, reducing the fiscal burden, and ensuring that the most vulnerable girls and
women are effectively reached.
10.10.2 Tamil Nadu’s next phase of women’s development requires a decisive shift from an
input-driven approach to an outcome-driven governance model that evaluates whether
programmes translate into meaningful improvements in empowerment, mobility, safety,
employment, and wellbeing. A unified gender outcomes dashboard with district-level
indicators on education completion, labour-force participation, workplace safety, health,
nutrition, and mobility would allow policymakers to identify disparities early, allocate
resources efficiently, and strengthen accountability.
10.10.3 Stronger institutional capacity is essential for this transition. Persistent inter-district
variation in scheme performance reflects uneven staffing, weak inter-departmental
coordination, and limited gender-specialised expertise. A major constraint is the limited
availability of recent causal evaluations.
10.10.4 Women’s employment requires a stronger policy focus. Despite high educational
attainment, women remain concentrated in low-productivity services and face rising
educated unemployment. Tamil Nadu should prioritise job creation in sunrise sectors,
advanced manufacturing, digital services, and GCCs. Urban support systems for housing
and child/elderly care must expand to meet the needs of single working women.
10.10.5 Further research is needed to understand women’s advancement within industry, including
the impact of childcare breaks on promotion prospects and the role of implicit biases in
limiting women’s progression into supervisory roles15. Findings from such research can
inform labour reforms and workplace policies.
15 Mahambare, V. (2023). Women in workforce: Tamil Nadu’s progress and challenges. Industrial Economist. https://industrialeconomist.com/women-in-workforce-tamil-nadu/3/, Accessed last on November 20, 2025.

244

10.10.6 Shifting social norms remains fundamental. School-based gender-sensitisation modules
that address household labour sharing, financial autonomy, decision-making, and the
prevention of domestic violence16 can shape attitudes early and contribute to long-term
change. Successful pilot interventions should be scaled statewide.
10.10.7 Finally, improving women’s health must be prioritised. This includes strengthening
menstrual hygiene management, ensuring access to sustainable products, expanding
clean public washrooms, reducing anaemia, improving cervical and breast cancer screening,
addressing the rising prevalence of non-communicable diseases and promoting healthier
diets.

10.11 Conclusions
10.11.1 Tamil Nadu’s experience in women’s development is distinguished by its breadth of
coverage, policy continuity, and institutional depth. Over several decades, sustained
public investment and a welfare architecture centred on gender inclusion have generated
substantial gains across core indicators of wellbeing. Women marry later, fertility has
fallen below replacement levels, institutional deliveries are near universal, and educational
attainment—particularly in higher education—is among the highest in India. Financial
inclusion is widespread, decision-making agency within households is comparatively
strong, and women are well represented in local governance and segments of public
administration. These achievements reflect a development trajectory in which social
reform and state intervention have systematically expanded women’s capabilities. Yet the
contemporary phase reveals a transition from access deficits to structural constraints.
Despite high levels of human capital formation, labour market participation among
young and educated women remains low, with marriage and care responsibilities sharply
reducing engagement during prime working years and only a minority of graduate women
in employment. Occupational concentration in low-productivity sectors and persistent
gender wage gaps point to binding constraints rooted less in capability deficits than in
entrenched social norms, care burdens, mobility limitations, and insufficient availability
of secure and gender-responsive employment opportunities.
10.11.2 Parallel tensions are visible in health and leadership outcomes. While Tamil Nadu’s public
health system ensures extensive service delivery, emerging concerns—such as anaemia,
inadequate dietary diversity, rising non-communicable diseases, low cancer screening, and
elevated caesarean rates—signal a shift in priorities from access to quality, prevention,
and long-term wellbeing. Food security mechanisms, including the universal
16 Government of India. (2005). The Protection of Women from Domestic Violence Act, 2005. Ministry of Law and Justice.
245

PDS, may require nutrition-sensitive recalibration to better address women’s health
outcomes. Patterns of representation further demonstrate that where institutional
pathways are deliberately inclusive—through quotas or transparent recruitment—
women’s participation rises significantly, underscoring that structural design rather
than individual capability constitutes the principal constraint. The next phase of policy
must therefore move beyond expansion toward transformation, embedding longitudinal
monitoring of women’s trajectories and placing employment generation at the centre
of gender strategy through growth in high-productivity sectors and investments in
childcare, mobility, and housing. Strengthened attention to nutrition, menstrual health,
and screening for chronic diseases, alongside sustained efforts to reshape norms around
care and economic autonomy, will be essential. Tamil Nadu has established a strong
foundation of gender equity; the challenge ahead lies in converting these social gains into
enduring economic participation, leadership, and wellbeing across the life course.

6. Social Sector
Box A6.1: Technical Note on UDISE+ Ratios
The enrolment ratios computed by UDISE+ are likely to be underestimated, especially at the
higher education levels. While the numerators reflect actual enrolment reported by the State,
the denominators are based on population projections rather than actual counts. In the absence
of a Census for more than a decade, the Sample Registration System (SRS) estimates agespecific populations using assumptions that do not adequately account for Tamil Nadu’s rapid
fertility decline. The State’s Total Fertility Rate (TFR) has been among the lowest in India—
currently around 1.3 birth per women and continuing to fall. Tamil Nadu also maintains complete
civil registration of births and deaths, and the number of newborns has sharply declined over
the past decade. Yet these empirical trends are not factored into SRS projections, inflating the
denominator and thereby depressing GER and related indicators. For policy purposes, it would
therefore be advisable for the Department of Economics and Statistics to generate its own
age-cohort projections based on actual birth registrations post-2011, until new Census data
become available.

Box A6.3: ASER vs. SLAS: Two Lenses on Learning Outcomes
The Annual Status of Education Report (ASER), conducted by the Pratham Foundation as an
NGO-led initiative, focuses on assessing foundational literacy, numeracy, and digital skills among
children and adolescents aged 14–16 in rural India, targeting villages and households where
educational challenges are often most acute. This nationwide survey reached 649,491 children
across 17,997 villages in 605 rural districts, with state-level details available, and employs a
household-based volunteer methodology to gauge basic “ability to do” tasks.
In contrast, the State Level Achievement Survey (SLAS) 2025, led by the State Planning Commission
(SPC) of the Tamil Nadu government, serves as a comprehensive diagnostic tool adapted from
the National Achievement Survey framework to evaluate learning outcomes in government and
government-aided schools, identifying gaps for evidence-based reforms. Covering a broader
scope and larger sample than ASER, it included approximately 980,340 students from 45,924
schools across all 38 districts of Tamil Nadu—encompassing rural, suburban, and urban areas—
and targeted Classes 3, 5, and 8 in subjects like Tamil, English, Mathematics, Science/EVS, and
Social Science (the latter for higher classes). Administered in early February 2025 via OMR-based
multiple-choice questions, it emphasized conceptual understanding, with findings notably
highlighting learning gaps in urban centers such as Chennai and its suburbs for reading and
mathematics.
Key differences between ASER and SLAS lie in their coverage, methodology, and purpose: ASER
offers a NGO-led, rural-only snapshot for advocacy on the learning crisis, while SLAS provides a
SPC-led, school-based technocratic assessment of grade-level competencies statewide to inform
targeted interventions. For instance, ASER 2024 results in Tamil Nadu indicated post-pandemic
recovery in rural foundational skills but levels still lagging 2018 benchmarks, whereas SLAS
2025 revealed declining proficiency in higher grades and technical subjects, with Kanyakumari
district leading at 66.5% overall average; southern districts, girls, and aided schools generally
outperformed others. These insights drive action plans, such as performance-based grouping
in Coimbatore for remedial support and statewide resource allocation.
Regarding accuracy and credibility, ASER’s transparent NGO approach delivers a representative

250

rural overview but has faced criticism from the Tamil Nadu Education Minister for its volunteerdriven household data collection. SLAS, as a semi-official technocratic evaluation, boasts
higher institutional trust for policy use, bolstered by its extensive scale across rural and urban
government schools.

ASER 2024 and SLAS 2025: A Comparison
ASER 2024

SLAS 2025

NGO-led (Pratham Foundation)

numeracy

8)

crisis

children can read/do math.

in rural and urban areas; Or how well schools teach the curriculum
